\chapter*{Abstract}
\addcontentsline{toc}{chapter}{Abstract}

\project{} is a hybrid Python/C++ machine-learning framework designed to support
the complete data-science workflow, from data preparation and model selection to
pipeline execution, code acceleration, deployment, and benchmark comparison. The
framework combines a high-level Python interface with native execution
components to improve usability, organization, and performance in machine-learning
experiments.

\medskip
\noindent The system is organized around six main modules: Auto Preprocessing, Accelera
AutoML, Accelera Pipe, the Code Parallelizer, Deployment, and the Benchmark
Platform. The Auto Preprocessing module automates common preparation steps for
tabular, text, and image data, including missing-value handling, encoding,
scaling, feature selection, text vectorization, and report generation. The
Accelera AutoML module supports classification and regression tasks by searching
over candidate models, preprocessing choices, and hyperparameter configurations
under time and trial constraints.

\medskip
\noindent Accelera Pipe represents machine-learning workflows as directed graph nodes for
preprocessing, modelling, prediction, metric evaluation, branching, and merging.
This graph-based design helps organize complex workflows and supports reuse of
shared execution paths. The Code Parallelizer analyzes C/C++ and supported Python
code, extracts loop features, predicts parallelization opportunities, applies
safety rules, and emits OpenMP-annotated C++ code when parallelization is safe.
The Benchmark Platform provides a foundation for comparing datasets,
submissions, metrics, and leaderboard results, while the Deployment module
supports the future use of trained models as deployable services.

\medskip
\noindent This report presents the motivation, market visibility study, literature survey,
system design, implementation methodology, testing, and experimental results of
Accelera. The results show that the implemented modules can reduce repetitive
machine-learning development work, improve workflow organization, and provide a
basis for future performance optimization and fair benchmark comparison.

\chapter{Introduction}
\label{chap:introduction}

Accelera is a hybrid Python/C++ machine-learning framework designed to support
the complete data-science workflow, starting from data preparation and model
selection, and extending to graph-based execution, native code acceleration,
deployment, and benchmark comparison. The project is not a single isolated
algorithm. Instead, it is a collection of connected modules that cooperate to
make machine-learning experiments easier to build, easier to repeat, and faster
to execute when possible.

\medskip
\noindent The main idea behind Accelera is simple: Python gives developers a productive
environment for experimentation, but real machine-learning workflows often need
more structure and better execution control. A typical workflow may include data
cleaning, preprocessing, feature engineering, model selection, prediction,
metric evaluation, reporting, deployment, and comparison between several
alternatives. When these steps are written as separate scripts, repeated work,
unclear execution order, and performance bottlenecks become common problems.

Accelera addresses this by combining six main units:
auto\_preprocessing, accelera\_automl,
accelera\_pipe, parallelizer, the benchmark platform, and
the Deployment module.

\section{Motivation and Justification}

Machine-learning development is often presented as a simple process of choosing
a model and calling \texttt{fit}. In practice, the model is only one stage in a
larger workflow. Before training, the data must be cleaned, split, transformed,
encoded, scaled, and sometimes reduced. After training, the output must be
evaluated, compared, reported, possibly deployed, or submitted to a benchmark
system. This creates several practical problems.

\begin{itemize}
  \item \textbf{Repeated preprocessing code:} Similar cleaning, encoding,
  scaling, and feature-selection steps are often rewritten for every dataset.

  \item \textbf{Manual model comparison:} Users may need to try many models and
  configurations before finding a good solution.

  \item \textbf{Linear workflow limitations:} Traditional scripts or simple
  pipelines may duplicate work when several branches share early operations.

  \item \textbf{Python loop bottlenecks:} Custom preprocessing functions written
  in Python may become slow when they contain large numeric loops.

  \item \textbf{Weak experiment visibility:} Without reports, graphs, and saved
  artifacts, it becomes harder to understand what preprocessing and evaluation
  steps were applied.

  \item \textbf{Deployment gap:} After training, models may still need extra
  work before they can be used as services or prediction endpoints.

  \item \textbf{Lack of fair comparison:} Pipeline results need a structured
  place where metrics, submissions, and benchmarks can be compared.

  \item \textbf{Deployment gap:} A trained model is not useful to external
  users until it is packaged, served through an API, configured for its runtime
  environment, and exposed on a local or remote host.
\end{itemize}

\noindent These problems justify the need for Accelera. The project provides automation
where the workflow is repetitive, graph execution where the workflow contains
branches, native acceleration where supported custom code is slow, deployment
support where trained models need to be used in practice, and benchmark
components where results must be compared. The motivation is not only speed; it
is also organization, reproducibility, usability, and practical use.

\medskip
\noindent The \texttt{auto\_preprocessing} module is motivated by the fact that data
preparation usually consumes a large part of the development effort. It handles
common preprocessing decisions for tabular datasets, such as dropping duplicate
records, detecting column types, imputing missing values, encoding categorical
features, applying robust scaling, selecting features, saving preprocessors, and
generating reports. It also supports text preprocessing using cleaning,
tokenization, stemming, TF-IDF vectorization, and target encoding. For image
workflows, it provides preparation paths for classification
tasks. This makes the project useful before the model-selection stage even
begins.

\medskip
\noindent The \texttt{accelera\_automl} module is motivated by the difficulty of manual
model selection. A user may not know which estimator, preprocessing choice, or
hyperparameter setting will perform best. AutoML reduces this burden by
evaluating candidate configurations under controlled budgets. This makes the
search process more systematic and provides useful outputs such as the best
model, score, leaderboard, and run history.

\medskip
\noindent The \texttt{accelera\_pipe} module is motivated by the need to represent
machine-learning workflows as graphs rather than only as linear chains. In
branch-heavy experiments, several alternatives may share the same preprocessing
steps. A graph representation makes these dependencies explicit. It also allows
the system to reason about execution order, branch selection, reuse of fitted
paths, and intermediate-data lifetime. This gives the user a high-level Python
interface while the backend manages a more structured execution model.

\medskip
\noindent The \texttt{parallelizer} module is motivated by the performance gap between
Python and native compiled code. Python is easy to write, but loop-heavy Python
functions can be slow. The parallelizer attempts to optimize supported custom
functions by converting them to C++, extracting loops, computing loop features,
classifying safe parallelization opportunities, applying rule-based safety
checks, inserting OpenMP pragmas, and compiling the result as a native
Python-callable extension. If the optimization path fails, the original function
is preserved, which keeps the workflow correct.

The benchmark platform is motivated by the need for organized
comparison. A machine-learning framework becomes more useful when users can
compare pipelines on common datasets and metrics. The benchmark module therefore
represents a future-facing part of the project, where submissions, metrics, and
leaderboard-style results can help users evaluate their solutions fairly.

Finally, the Deployment module is motivated by the final step that many
machine-learning projects postpone: making the trained model available outside
the notebook or local script. Deployment normally requires packaging artifacts,
building an API layer, preparing dependencies, exposing ports, configuring
servers, and checking that the service responds correctly. Accelera includes
this step so that the workflow can continue from model creation to a reachable
prediction service.

\begin{table}[!htbp]
\centering
\acceleraTableStyle
\caption{Problems addressed by Accelera.}
\label{tab:intro-problems-solutions}
\begin{tabular}{p{0.30\textwidth}p{0.29\textwidth}p{0.31\textwidth}}
\toprule
\textbf{Problem} & \textbf{Accelera component} & \textbf{Expected benefit} \\
\midrule

Manual and repetitive data preparation &
auto\_preprocessing &
Automates common preprocessing and produces saved artifacts and reports. \\
\midrule

Time-consuming model selection &
accelera\_automl &
Searches candidate models and configurations under controlled budgets. \\
\midrule

Repeated work in branch-heavy workflows &
accelera\_pipe &
Represents the workflow as a graph and exposes shared execution structure. \\
\midrule

Slow custom Python loops &
parallelizer &
Converts supported functions to native code and inserts safe OpenMP pragmas. \\
\midrule

Model deployment after training &
Deployment support &
Helps prepare trained models or pipelines for service-based inference. \\
\midrule

Difficult result comparison &
Benchmark platform &
Stores benchmark information, metrics, submissions, and leaderboard views. \\
\midrule

Local-only trained models &
Deployment module &
Packages saved models into validated API services that can run in Docker,
Heroku, or AWS EC2 workflows. \\

\bottomrule
\end{tabular}
\end{table}

\section{Project Objectives and Problem Definition}

The main objective of Accelera is to provide an integrated framework for
constructing, preprocessing, executing, evaluating, optimizing, and comparing
machine-learning workflows. The project aims to keep the familiar Python
programming style while adding automation, graph execution, native acceleration,
and benchmark-oriented evaluation.

\medskip
\noindent The main objectives of the project are:

\begin{enumerate}
  \item Build an automated preprocessing module for tabular, text, and image
  datasets.

  \item Implement AutoML classifiers and regressors that search across supported
  model configurations using scoring metrics, cross-validation, time budgets,
  trial budgets, and optional ensemble construction.

  \item Provide a graph-based pipeline module that represents workflow steps as
  executable graph nodes rather than only as a sequential list.

  \item Support pipeline operations such as preprocessing, model fitting,
  prediction, metric evaluation, branching, merging, and executed-graph reuse.

  \item Integrate custom preprocessing functions with the parallelizer so that
  supported loop-heavy functions can be optimized automatically.

  \item Implement a conservative code parallelization path that uses C++,
  OpenMP, loop-feature extraction, classification, rule-based safety checks, and
  native compilation.

  \item Provide reporting tools that make preprocessing outputs, model metrics,
  and pipeline structure more visible to the user.

  \item Build a benchmark platform prototype for datasets, submissions, users,
  metrics, and leaderboard-based comparison.

  \item Provide a Deployment module that packages trained models and generated
  artifacts into usable services, supports local and remote deployment
  workflows, manages deployment configuration, and reduces the infrastructure
  knowledge required to expose a trained model through an API.
\end{enumerate}

\medskip
\noindent The problem addressed by the project can be stated as follows:

\begin{quote}
Machine-learning workflows in Python are easy to write but become difficult to
organize, repeat, compare, and optimize when they include many preprocessing
steps, model alternatives, custom functions, evaluation paths, and deployment
requirements. Accelera aims
to preserve Python usability while adding automated preprocessing, budgeted
model search, graph-based execution, safe loop-level acceleration, and
benchmark-oriented evaluation, while also supporting the final step of turning
trained artifacts into accessible services.
\end{quote}

Workflow complexity increases when preprocessing steps, branches, and candidate
models grow, while execution performance may suffer from repeated work and slow
Python loops. Accelera addresses both issues by combining high-level workflow
automation with lower-level execution optimization.

\section{Project Outcomes}

The project outcomes are the implemented modules and the integration between
them. Each module provides a different part of the final system.

\subsection*{Outcome 1: Automated preprocessing}

The auto\_preprocessing module prepares datasets for machine-learning
models. For tabular data, it handles duplicates, missing values, column-type
detection, categorical encoding, robust scaling, target processing, feature
selection, and report generation. For text data, it applies cleaning,
tokenization, stemming, TF-IDF vectorization, and label encoding. For image
data, it supports classification preparation. The outcome is a
set of processed arrays, saved preprocessors, selected features, and reports.

\subsection*{Outcome 2: AutoML model search}

The accelera\_automl module provides estimator-style AutoML classes for
classification and regression. It searches candidate configurations, evaluates
them with cross-validation, supports warm-starting from dataset metafeatures,
and can build voting or stacked ensembles. The outcome is a fitted final model,
best score, leaderboard, and run history.

\subsection*{Outcome 3: Graph-based pipeline execution}

The accelera\_pipe module provides a graph-based pipeline interface.
It represents preprocessing, model, prediction, metric, branch, and merge
operations as graph nodes. The output of a pipeline run includes predictions and
an ExecutedGraph, which stores the selected fitted path for later use.
This outcome gives the project its workflow-execution layer.

\subsection*{Outcome 4: Source-level parallelization}

The parallelizer module accelerates supported loop-heavy code. It can
process C/C++ code, supported Python code, custom Python functions, and
transformer methods. It extracts loops, computes features, classifies
parallelization opportunities, applies safety rules, inserts OpenMP pragmas, and
compiles native Python-callable extensions when possible. If any step fails, it
falls back to the original Python implementation.

\subsection*{Outcome 5: Pipeline and parallelizer integration}

One of the most important outcomes is the connection between
accelera\_pipe and the parallelizer. When a preprocessing node
contains a supported custom Python function, the pipeline attempts to optimize
it before adding it to the graph. This allows the system to combine graph-level
workflow execution with code-level acceleration.

\subsection*{Outcome 6: Reporting and benchmark support}

The project includes reports for preprocessing, model metrics, and graph
visualization. It also includes a benchmark platform prototype with backend and
frontend components for datasets, metrics, users, submissions, and leaderboards.
These outcomes improve visibility and comparison.

\subsection*{Outcome 7: Deployment support}

The Deployment module extends Accelera beyond experimentation by converting
saved machine-learning artifacts into runnable services. It organizes
config.json files, model pickle files, serving code, schema
validation, telemetry logging, Docker packaging, and target-specific commands
for local containers, Heroku, and AWS EC2. This outcome is important because it
allows Accelera to support the complete journey from building a model to making
the model reachable through an endpoint.

\begin{table}[!htbp]
\centering
\acceleraTableStyle
\caption{Summary of project outcomes.}
\label{tab:intro-outcomes}
\begin{tabular}{p{0.30\textwidth}p{0.60\textwidth}}
\toprule
\textbf{Outcome} & \textbf{Description} \\
\midrule

Automated preprocessing &
The project provides reusable preprocessing support for tabular, text, and image
data, with generated reports and saved preprocessing artifacts. \\
\midrule

Automated model selection &
The AutoML module helps search and compare candidate classification and
regression models under time and trial constraints. \\
\midrule

Graph-based pipeline execution &
Accelera Pipe represents machine-learning workflows as executable graphs,
supporting preprocessing, modelling, prediction, metrics, branching, and merging. \\
\midrule

Code-level acceleration &
The Parallelizer attempts to optimize supported loop-heavy Python/C++ code using
static analysis, safety checks, OpenMP pragmas, compilation, and caching. \\
\midrule

Benchmarking support &
The Benchmark Platform provides a foundation for dataset-based evaluation,
prediction submission, metric calculation, and leaderboard-style comparison. \\
\midrule

Deployment support &
The Deployment module packages trained models and supporting artifacts into
validated API services that can run locally in Docker or be deployed to
supported cloud/server targets. \\

\bottomrule
\end{tabular}
\end{table}

\newpage
\section{Document Organization}

The rest of this report is organized into seven main parts: Chapter 2 presents the market visibility study, 
Chapter 3 discusses the literature survey and related work, Chapter 4 explains the methodology and system 
design of the main Accelera modules, Chapter 5 presents testing, verification, and experimental results, 
and Chapter 6 concludes the report by summarizing the contributions, limitations, and possible future improvements. 
The appendices include supporting material such as documentation, usage instructions, feasibility details, 
and additional implementation notes.


\chapter{Market Visibility Study}
\label{chap:market-visibility}

This chapter studies the market need for Accelera and compares it with existing
tools that solve related problems. The goal is to show who can benefit from the
project, what alternatives already exist, and why Accelera provides a different
value. The chapter also presents a simplified business case and financial
analysis to clarify how the project could be developed into a product or service.

\medskip
\noindent Accelera targets the data-science workflow as a complete process, not as one
isolated task. Some existing tools focus on preprocessing, others focus on model
selection, others focus on competition platforms or code acceleration, and
others focus only on hosting or cloud deployment.
Accelera also can be used as a compination of five connected parts (end-2-end) E2E module:
auto\_preprocessing, accelera\_automl,
accelera\_pipe, parallelizer, the benchmark platform, and
the Deployment module.

\section{Targeted Customers}

\medskip
\noindent The targeted customers for Accelera are users and organizations that build,
test, compare, and optimize machine-learning workflows. These customers can be
divided into several groups, as shown in Table~\ref{tab:target-customers}.

\begin{table}[!htbp]
\centering
\acceleraTableStyle
\caption{Targeted customers for Accelera.}
\label{tab:target-customers}
\begin{tabular}{p{0.25\textwidth}p{0.33\textwidth}p{0.32\textwidth}}
\toprule
\textbf{Customer group} & \textbf{Main need} & \textbf{How Accelera helps} \\
\midrule

Students and beginners &
Need an easier way to build complete machine-learning workflows without writing
every step manually. &
Provides automated preprocessing, model search, reports, and a pipeline
interface that organizes the workflow. \\
\midrule

Data scientists &
Need to prepare data, compare models, and repeat experiments quickly. &
Reduces repetitive preprocessing, supports AutoML search, and gives reports and
leaderboards for comparison. \\
\midrule

Machine-learning engineers &
Need structured, reusable, and optimized workflows that can be inspected and
executed reliably. &
Provides graph-based pipeline execution, executed-graph reuse, and integration
with custom preprocessing acceleration. \\
\midrule

Researchers &
Need to compare approaches, measure results, and experiment with workflow and
code optimization. &
Provides modular components for benchmarking, graph execution, OpenMP
parallelization, and performance evaluation. \\
\midrule

Small teams and startups &
Need a practical toolkit that reduces development time without building an
internal ML platform from scratch. &
Combines preprocessing, AutoML, pipeline execution, reports, and benchmark
support in one project, with deployment support for turning selected models
into accessible services. \\

\bottomrule
\end{tabular}
\end{table}

\medskip
\noindent The most important customer segment is the data-science and machine-learning
user who wants to move from a raw dataset to an evaluated model with less manual
work. For beginners, Accelera provides guidance and automation. For experienced
users, it provides a more structured execution model and opportunities for
acceleration.

\medskip
\noindent Accelera can also be useful in educational environments. In many machine
learning courses, students repeat the same steps: clean the data, encode
features, scale numerical values, split the dataset, train models, calculate
metrics, and compare results. Accelera can make these steps easier to understand
because it provides reports and separates the workflow into clear modules.

\medskip
\noindent For professional users, the value is different. They care more about
reproducibility, execution time, reusable pipeline structure, and fair
comparison. The \texttt{accelera\_pipe} and benchmark modules are especially
useful here because they organize workflow execution and comparison. The
\texttt{parallelizer} adds value when the workflow includes supported custom
preprocessing functions that are slow in Python.

\subsection*{Customer Value Proposition}

The main value proposition of Accelera is that it reduces the gap between
experimentation and structured execution. Instead of forcing the user to choose
between simple scripts and large industrial platforms, Accelera provides a
research-friendly framework with practical automation and optimization features.

\begin{itemize}
  \item \textbf{Less manual work:} preprocessing and model search are partially
  automated.
  \item \textbf{Better organization:} workflows can be represented as graph
  pipelines instead of scattered scripts.
  \item \textbf{Better visibility:} reports and benchmark views help users
  inspect results.
  \item \textbf{Possible speedup:} supported loop-heavy preprocessing code can be
  accelerated through C++ and OpenMP.
  \item \textbf{Fairer comparison:} benchmark components help compare pipelines
  using common datasets and metrics.
  \item \textbf{Practical delivery:} deployment commands help move saved models
  from local artifacts to runnable API services.
\end{itemize}

\section{Market Survey}

Many existing tools provide features related to Accelera, but most of them focus
on only one part of the workflow. Some tools focus on preprocessing, some on
AutoML, some on competitions, and some on code acceleration. Accelera is
different because it tries to connect these ideas into one toolkit.


\subsection{AutoCleanML}

AutoCleanML is a Python tool focused on automating data cleaning and
preprocessing tasks for machine-learning datasets. It is useful because data
preparation is one of the most repetitive and time-consuming parts of the
machine-learning workflow. The tool helps automate common data-cleaning and
preprocessing decisions, which can make dataset preparation easier, especially
for beginners. It also provides reporting features that explain some of the
preprocessing decisions applied during the workflow.

\medskip
\noindent However, AutoCleanML mainly focuses on preprocessing and data cleaning rather
than the complete machine-learning workflow. It does not provide a graph-based
pipeline execution engine, does not target custom code acceleration or OpenMP
parallelization, and does not provide a benchmark platform for comparing full
pipelines.

\medskip
\noindent Compared with AutoCleanML, Accelera includes preprocessing but does not stop
there. It also includes AutoML search, graph pipeline execution, source-level
parallelization, reports, and benchmark support. Therefore, Accelera aims to be
closer to a complete workflow framework.


\subsection{scikit-learn}

scikit-learn is one of the strongest tools in the machine-learning
market. It provides a wide range of models, preprocessing tools, metrics, and a
simple Pipeline abstraction. For this reason, it is the most important
competitor for accelera\_pipe.

\textbf{Pros:}
\begin{itemize}
  \item Mature and widely used.
  \item Provides many models, preprocessing utilities, and metrics.
  \item Has a simple and reliable pipeline interface.
  \item Integrates well with the Python data-science ecosystem.
\end{itemize}

\textbf{Cons:}
\begin{itemize}
  \item The standard pipeline is mainly linear.
  \item Branch-heavy workflows may require repeated pipelines or external search
  utilities.
  \item It does not focus on native graph scheduling for pipeline branches.
  \item It does not automatically convert supported custom preprocessing
  functions to C++ and OpenMP.
\end{itemize}

Accelera uses \texttt{scikit-learn} as a strong baseline rather than ignoring
it. The difference is that \texttt{accelera\_pipe} represents the workflow as a
graph and integrates with the Parallelizer for supported custom preprocessing
acceleration. For this reason, \texttt{scikit-learn} is the most important
competitor for \texttt{accelera\_pipe}.


\subsection{AutoML Tools}

AutoML tools such as Auto-sklearn, TPOT, and AutoGluon focus on automating model
selection and hyperparameter search. They are useful because they reduce the
manual effort needed to test many models and configurations. These tools can
often provide strong baseline performance, and some of them also support
ensemble construction.

\medskip
\noindent However, most AutoML tools focus mainly on model search rather than complete
workflow execution. They do not mainly target graph-based pipeline execution,
and they do not automatically accelerate custom preprocessing loops using
OpenMP. Some AutoML systems can also be heavy or complex for beginners.

\medskip
\noindent Accelera includes \texttt{accelera\_automl}, but also connects AutoML with
preprocessing, graph execution, reports, and parallelization. This makes the
AutoML module one part of a larger framework rather than the only feature.


\subsection{Code Acceleration Tools}

Tools such as Numba, Cython, and manual C++ extensions can speed up Python code.
They are relevant because Accelera also tries to accelerate supported custom
preprocessing functions. These tools can significantly improve performance for
numeric and loop-heavy workloads, and they provide more control for experienced
developers who understand optimization and compilation.

\medskip
\noindent However, these tools often require users to understand extra syntax,
compilation steps, or type constraints. They also do not automatically decide
where acceleration fits inside a machine-learning pipeline. In addition, they do
not provide pipeline graphs, AutoML, reports, or benchmark features.

\medskip
\noindent The Accelera Parallelizer is different because it is designed to work with the
pipeline module. It attempts to optimize supported custom preprocessing
functions and attach the optimized callable back to the pipeline node. This makes
code acceleration part of the workflow instead of a separate manual task.
```


\subsection{Deployment and Hosting Platforms}

Deployment platforms such as Heroku, AWS EC2, Render, Railway, and Google Cloud
Run are relevant to Accelera because they help developers publish software
services. Their main value is infrastructure: they provide servers, routing,
containers, or managed execution environments. This is important, but it does
not by itself solve the machine-learning-specific work of collecting model
artifacts, preserving deployment configuration, validating input schemas, and
connecting a trained model to an inference API.

\textbf{Pros:}
\begin{itemize}
  \item Provide public or cloud-hosted environments for running applications.
  \item Reduce the need to own physical servers.
  \item Support common deployment units such as containers, web services, or
  virtual machines.
\end{itemize}

\textbf{Cons:}
\begin{itemize}
  \item Usually require the user to prepare the ML serving code and runtime
  dependencies separately.
  \item Do not automatically understand Accelera model artifacts,
  \texttt{config.json}, schema validation, or prediction telemetry.
  \item General-purpose platforms may be difficult for beginners because they
  involve CLI tools, SSH, ports, environment variables, and deployment logs.
\end{itemize}

Accelera's Deployment module is different because it is not only a hosting
target. It is part of the ML workflow itself. It receives the outputs produced
by training or pipeline modules, copies model binaries, writes deployment
requirements, builds a Docker service, exposes prediction endpoints, validates
requests, records telemetry, and provides commands for local Docker, Heroku,
and AWS EC2. This integration increases Accelera's market value because it
makes the system closer to an end-to-end ML platform rather than only a model
training toolkit.


\begin{table}[!htbp]
\centering

\scriptsize
\renewcommand{\arraystretch}{1.08}
\caption{Market comparison summary.}
\label{tab:market-comparison}

\toprule

and health checks for Accelera model services. \\

\newpage
\section{Business Case and Financial Analysis}

Accelera can be developed as an open-source framework with optional hosted
services around benchmarking, reporting, team collaboration, managed execution,
and deployment assistance. The open-source part helps adoption because students,
researchers, and developers can use the framework freely. The business value
can come from hosted benchmark features, team dashboards, managed experiment
tracking, deployment templates, and support services.

\subsection{Business Case}

The main business case is based on reducing time spent in repetitive
data-science work. Users and teams spend time preparing data, comparing models,
rewriting scripts, debugging pipeline steps, and measuring results. Accelera can
reduce this time by providing one framework that handles multiple stages of the
workflow. The Deployment module strengthens this business case because
organizations do not only need trained models; they need trained models to
become usable services. Automating deployment-related steps can reduce manual
DevOps effort, reduce time-to-market, and lower the technical barrier for users
who understand ML but are less experienced with Docker, SSH, cloud machines, or
service exposure.

\begin{table}[!htbp]
\centering
\acceleraTableStyle
\caption{Possible business value of Accelera.}
\label{tab:business-value}
\begin{tabular}{p{0.30\textwidth}p{0.30\textwidth}p{0.30\textwidth}}
\toprule
\textbf{Business value} & \textbf{Reason} & \textbf{Possible customer} \\
\midrule

Faster experimentation &
Automated preprocessing and AutoML reduce repeated manual trials. &
Students, data scientists, small teams. \\
\midrule

Better organization &
Graph pipelines and executed graphs make workflows easier to inspect and reuse. &
ML engineers and research teams. \\
\midrule

Performance improvement &
Parallelizer can speed up supported loop-heavy preprocessing code. &
Teams with custom preprocessing bottlenecks. \\
\midrule

Better comparison &
Benchmark platform supports submissions, metrics, and leaderboards. &
Universities, communities, and organizations. \\
\midrule

Shorter deployment path &
Deployment support helps package model artifacts, build a serving API, and run
the service on supported targets. &
Small teams, prototypes, and applied ML projects. \\
\midrule

Lower entry barrier &
Reports and automation make ML workflows easier for beginners. &
Education and training programs. \\

\bottomrule
\end{tabular}
\end{table}

The first possible product direction is a hosted benchmark platform. Users can
submit pipelines, compare results on public datasets, and view leaderboards. A
free tier can support public datasets and basic submissions, while paid tiers can
support private benchmarks, team spaces, larger storage, and advanced reports.

The second possible product direction is a managed workflow dashboard. This
dashboard can visualize preprocessing results, model leaderboards, pipeline
graphs, and runtime comparisons. Teams can use it to organize experiments and
share results without relying only on notebooks.

The third possible product direction is professional support and integration.
Organizations that want to use Accelera internally may need help adapting the
framework to their datasets, infrastructure, or benchmark requirements. This
creates a possible support and consulting model.

The Deployment module also creates a practical commercial path for a managed
deployment tier. A hosted product could provide prepared deployment templates,
private model-serving environments, monitored endpoints, and simplified rollback
support. Even when the current implementation remains lightweight and
developer-controlled, its presence makes Accelera easier to present as a
complete platform because it connects experimentation with operational use.

\subsection{Financial Analysis}

The financial analysis can be divided into expected costs and possible revenue
streams. Since Accelera is mainly a software project, the capital expenditure is
limited compared with hardware-based products. Most costs come from development,
hosting, cloud resources, storage, and maintenance.

Possible revenue streams include:

\begin{itemize}

  workspaces, and larger storage limits.

  monitoring for teams.

  support for organizations.
  \item \textbf{Education packages:} university or training-center packages for
  teaching machine-learning workflows.
\end{itemize}

A simple financial plan can start with low-cost hosting and manual support. In
the first stage, the focus should be adoption and validation. The open-source
framework can attract users, while the benchmark platform can collect early
feedback. In the second stage, private benchmark spaces and team dashboards can
be introduced. In the third stage, enterprise support and managed compute can be
added if the user base grows.

The expected break-even point depends on hosting cost, development effort, and
the number of paying users. Since the software can begin as open source, the
initial financial risk is lower than products that require manufacturing or
special hardware. The main challenge is user adoption. If Accelera can attract
students, researchers, and small teams through the free framework, the benchmark
and reporting services can become the commercial layer that supports future
development.


\chapter{Literature Survey}
In this chapter, we give an overview of the topics, algorithms, and techniques needed to understand the project as a whole. We discuss the technical subjects and background knowledge, and then summarize the research papers related to our project.

\section{Background and Previous Work}
\noindent This section introduces the main background needed to understand the Accelera project as a whole. It explains the important concepts, techniques, and tools that were used in different parts of the system. These background topics help clarify how the project supports data preparation, pipeline execution, report generation, model evaluation, and benchmarking. The goal of this section is to give the reader enough context before moving to the methodology and implementation chapters.

\subsection{Data Science and Machine Learning}
Data science is the field that uses data, statistics, programming, and domain
knowledge to understand a problem and extract useful insights from it. It is
usually used when we have raw data and we want to turn it into useful
information or predictions.

\noindent Machine learning is one of the main parts of data science. Instead of writing
fixed rules for every case, we train a model on examples, and the model learns
patterns from these examples. After training, the model can use what it learned
to make predictions on new data.

\noindent In supervised machine learning, the training data contains input features and a
target label. The model learns the relationship between the features and the
target. If the target is a class, such as spam or not spam, the problem is called
classification. If the target is a continuous number, such as price or
temperature, the problem is called regression.

\subsubsection{Data Exploration and Preprocessing ~\cite{komorowski2016exploratory}}
Exploratory Data Analysis, or EDA, is the step where we inspect the dataset
before building the model. We summarize columns, draw visualizations, check
missing values, and try to understand relationships between variables. This step
helps us notice problems early instead of discovering them after training the
model.

\noindent Data preprocessing is the stage of transforming raw data into a clean and usable
format for machine learning models. In real datasets, the data may contain
missing values, duplicated rows, different feature scales, and categorical
columns that cannot be used directly by many models. Because of this,
preprocessing is usually considered an essential part of a machine learning
workflow.

\noindent Missing value imputation is used when some values in a dataset are empty.
Instead of removing all rows that contain missing values, the missing values can
be replaced using a suitable value, such as the mean, median, mode, or another
estimated value.

\noindent Robust scaling is a scaling technique that uses the median and the interquartile
range instead of the mean and standard deviation. It is useful when the data
contains outliers, because the median and interquartile range are less affected
by extreme values:
\[
x' = \frac{x - \mathop{\mathrm{median}}(X)}{\mathop{\mathrm{IQR}}(X)}
\]
where $x'$ is the scaled value, $x$ is the original value,
$\mathop{\mathrm{median}}(X)$ is the median of the feature values, and
$\mathop{\mathrm{IQR}}(X)$ is the interquartile range.

\noindent Categorical encoding is the process of converting text categories into numeric
values. Many traditional machine learning models and preprocessing pipelines
expect numerical input. Two examples are binary encoding and frequency encoding.
Binary encoding first gives each category a number, then converts this number
into binary form and stores the binary digits in separate columns. Frequency
encoding replaces each category with how often it appears in the dataset:
\[
f(c) = \frac{\mathop{\mathrm{count}}(c)}{N}
\]
where $f(c)$ is the frequency of category $c$,
$\mathop{\mathrm{count}}(c)$ is the number of rows containing this category,
and $N$ is the total number of rows.

\subsubsection{Model Evaluation Metrics ~\cite{hossin2015review}}
After training a model, we need metrics to check whether the model is performing
well or not. The selected metric depends on the type of problem. Classification
models are evaluated using metrics that compare predicted classes with actual
classes, while regression models are evaluated using metrics that measure the
difference between predicted numerical values and real values.\\

\noindent\textbf{Classification metrics:}
Classification metrics are used when the output is a class or category, such as
spam or not spam, positive or negative, or one image class from many classes. The
confusion matrix is the base idea behind many classification metrics. It compares
the actual classes with the predicted classes. For binary classification, it
contains four values: true positives, true negatives, false positives, and false
negatives.

\begin{itemize}
  \item \textbf{True Positive (TP):} The model predicts positive, and the actual
  class is positive.
  \item \textbf{True Negative (TN):} The model predicts negative, and the actual
  class is negative.
  \item \textbf{False Positive (FP):} The model predicts positive, but the actual
  class is negative.
  \item \textbf{False Negative (FN):} The model predicts negative, but the actual
  class is positive.
\end{itemize}

\noindent Accuracy measures how many predictions are correct out of all predictions:
\[
Accuracy = \frac{TP + TN}{TP + TN + FP + FN}
\]
\noindent where $TP$ is true positives, $TN$ is true negatives, $FP$ is false positives,
and $FN$ is false negatives. Accuracy is simple and useful when the classes are
balanced, but it may be misleading when one class is much larger than the other.\\
\noindent Precision measures how many predicted positive samples are actually positive:
\[
Precision = \frac{TP}{TP + FP}
\]
\noindent Precision is useful when false positives should be reduced.\\

\noindent Recall measures how many actual positive samples were correctly detected:
\[
Recall = \frac{TP}{TP + FN}
\]
\noindent Recall is useful when false negatives should be reduced.\\

\noindent The F1-score combines precision and recall:
\[
F1 = 2 \times \frac{Precision \times Recall}{Precision + Recall}
\]
\noindent It is useful when both precision and recall are important, especially with
imbalanced datasets.

\noindent\textbf{Regression metrics:}
Regression metrics are used when the output is a numerical value, such as price,
score, or temperature.

\noindent Mean Absolute Error measures the average absolute difference between the real
values and predicted values:
\[
MAE = \frac{1}{n}\sum_{i=1}^{n}|y_i - \hat{y}_i|
\]
\noindent where $y_i$ is the actual value, $\hat{y}_i$ is the predicted value, and $n$ is
the number of samples. MAE is easy to understand because it gives the average
error in the same unit as the target value.\\

\noindent Mean Squared Error gives more penalty to large errors:
\[
MSE = \frac{1}{n}\sum_{i=1}^{n}(y_i - \hat{y}_i)^2
\]
\noindent This means large mistakes affect the score more than small mistakes.\\

\noindent Root Mean Squared Error is the square root of MSE:
\[
RMSE = \sqrt{\frac{1}{n}\sum_{i=1}^{n}(y_i - \hat{y}_i)^2}
\]
\noindent RMSE is easier to read than MSE because it returns the error to the same unit as
the target value.\\

\noindent R-squared score measures how well the regression model explains the variation in
the actual values:
\[
R^2 = 1 - \frac{\sum_{i=1}^{n}(y_i - \hat{y}_i)^2}{\sum_{i=1}^{n}(y_i - \bar{y})^2}
\]
\noindent where $y_i$ is the actual value, $\hat{y}_i$ is the predicted value, and
$\bar{y}$ is the mean of the actual values. A value close to 1 means the model
explains the data well, while a value close to 0 means the model does not explain
the data well.

\subsection{Natural Language Processing (NLP) ~\cite{sabharwal2021introduction}}
\noindent Natural Language Processing is the field that helps computers
understand and work with human language. It is used in many applications such as
text classification, sentiment analysis, spam detection, and chatbots. Text data
cannot be used directly by most machine learning models, so it needs to be
cleaned and converted into numbers first.

\noindent In text preprocessing, the text may be converted to lowercase, cleaned from
unnecessary symbols, split into words, and simplified using stemming. After that,
methods such as TF-IDF are used to transform the text into numerical features.

\subsubsection{Term Frequency-Inverse Document Frequency (TF-IDF)}
Term Frequency-Inverse Document Frequency is one common technique used
to convert text into numerical features. It gives each word a weight based on
how important it is in one document and across the full dataset. Term Frequency
measures how often a word appears in a document. Inverse Document Frequency gives
lower weight to words that appear in many documents, because these words are
usually less useful for classification:
\[
TFIDF(t,d) = TF(t,d) \times IDF(t)
\]
where $TFIDF(t,d)$ is the weight of term $t$ in document $d$, $TF(t,d)$ is the
frequency of the term in the document, and $IDF(t)$ measures how rare the term
is across all documents.

\subsection{Data Augmentation}
Data augmentation is a technique used to increase the diversity of a dataset
without actually collecting new data. It works by applying transformations to
existing samples to create new modified versions. For example, in image
classification, augmentation may include flipping, rotating, cropping, or
changing brightness. This can help the model generalize better because it sees
more variations during training.

\subsection{Machine Learning Pipelines}
A machine learning pipeline is a sequence of steps used to prepare data, train a
model, make predictions, and evaluate results. Pipelines are useful because they
organize the full workflow in one structure instead of writing each step
separately.

A typical pipeline may include preprocessing steps such as scaling, encoding, or
feature selection, followed by a machine learning model. This makes experiments
easier to repeat and reduces mistakes, because the same preprocessing steps are
applied during training and testing.

One of the most common tools for building machine learning pipelines is
\texttt{scikit-learn}. It provides a \texttt{Pipeline} object that connects
transformers and estimators together. In this project, \texttt{scikit-learn} is
considered the main competitor for the \texttt{accelera\_pipe} module because
both systems aim to help users build machine learning workflows in Python.

\subsection{Graph-Based Pipeline Execution}
Traditional machine learning pipelines usually execute steps in a linear order.
This is suitable for simple workflows, but it can become inefficient when several
branches share the same preprocessing steps or when multiple models are compared.

Graph-based execution represents the workflow as nodes and edges. Each node
represents an operation, such as preprocessing, model training, prediction, or
metric evaluation. Each edge represents the dependency between operations. This
representation can help avoid repeated work, because shared steps can be
executed once and reused by different branches.

The \texttt{accelera\_pipe} module follows this idea by representing the
pipeline internally as an execution graph. This allows the system to support
branching, merging, parallel execution of independent paths, and better
management of intermediate data.

\subsection{Code Parallelization}
Code parallelization is the process of running parts of a program at the same
time to reduce execution time. In many programs, the most expensive part is a
loop that repeats the same operation over a large amount of data. If the loop
iterations are independent, they may be executed in parallel.

Manual parallelization is difficult because the developer must check that there
are no unsafe dependencies between iterations. If a loop writes to shared
variables or depends on results from previous iterations, parallel execution may
produce wrong results. Because of this, automatic parallelization tools try to
analyze code and decide whether a loop can be safely parallelized.

\subsection{OpenMP}
OpenMP is a programming interface used to add parallel execution to C and C++
programs. It allows developers to use compiler directives, called pragmas, to
tell the compiler that some parts of the code can run in parallel.

For example, the directive \texttt{\#pragma omp parallel for} can be used before
a loop to distribute loop iterations across multiple threads. OpenMP is useful
because it does not require rewriting the whole program. However, the pragma must
be inserted only when the loop is safe to parallelize.

\subsection{Deployment}
Deployment is the process of moving software from a development environment to
an environment where users or other systems can access it. In machine-learning
projects, deployment is important because the trained model must be connected
to software that receives input, runs inference, and returns predictions.

\subsection{Model Deployment}
Model deployment means packaging a trained model together with the code and
dependencies needed to use it. A deployed model is usually exposed through a
service endpoint so that other applications can send data and receive
predictions. This is different from only saving a model file, because a saved
file still requires a runtime service before it becomes usable by external
users.

\subsection{Machine Learning Deployment}
Machine-learning deployment adds extra concerns beyond ordinary software
deployment. The system must preserve preprocessing steps, load model binaries,
validate input schemas, handle prediction errors, and sometimes track requests
for monitoring. These concerns explain why Accelera treats deployment as a
module connected to the ML workflow rather than only as a cloud-hosting task.

\subsection{Cloud and Server Deployment}
Cloud deployment runs a service on infrastructure provided by a cloud provider.
Server deployment runs the service on a machine that may be local, virtual, or
cloud-hosted. In both cases, the deployed service must receive network traffic
on a configured port and must be started with the correct dependencies and
environment variables.

\subsection{Web Services, APIs, and REST APIs}
A web service is a program that exposes functionality over the network. An API
defines how other programs interact with that service. A REST API commonly uses
HTTP methods and URLs to exchange structured data. In the Deployment module,
prediction requests are sent to API endpoints such as \texttt{/predict}, while
status information is exposed through endpoints such as \texttt{/health}.

\subsection{HTTP, HTTPS, JSON, URLs, and Ports}
HTTP is the common protocol used by web clients and servers. HTTPS is the secure
version of HTTP. JSON is a text format frequently used to send structured data
between clients and APIs. A URL identifies where a service can be reached, and
a port identifies the network entry point used by the service on a machine.
These concepts are needed to understand deployed prediction endpoints.

\subsection{Docker and Containerization}
Docker is a containerization tool that packages an application with its runtime
environment. A container helps the same service run consistently across
machines because dependencies, files, and startup commands are described
explicitly. The Deployment module uses Docker to package the FastAPI serving
application, model files, configuration, and required Python libraries into one
runnable service image.

\subsection{Virtual Machines, AWS, and AWS EC2}
A virtual machine is a software-defined computer that runs on physical cloud
hardware. AWS is a major cloud provider, and EC2 is its virtual-machine service.
Deploying to EC2 gives the user flexible control over the server, but it also
requires practical infrastructure steps such as SSH access, file
synchronization, Docker installation, port exposure, and health checking.

\subsection{Heroku and Container Hosting}
Heroku is a platform for deploying web applications with less direct server
management. It can run containerized applications through its container
registry. In Accelera, Heroku is treated as a deployment target where the
prepared Docker service can be pushed and released through Heroku CLI commands.

\subsection{SSH and CLI Tools}
SSH is a secure protocol for connecting to remote machines and running commands.
A CLI is a text-based interface used to execute commands from a terminal. The
Deployment module uses CLI workflows for local Docker, Heroku, and EC2. For
EC2, SSH is used to run remote commands, build Docker images, start containers,
and read logs.

\subsection{Environment Variables and Configuration Files}
Environment variables are runtime values provided outside the source code, such
as the port on which a server should listen. Configuration files store structured
settings used by the program. Accelera uses \texttt{config.json} to locate model
artifacts, define input schema rules, and configure tracking behavior.

\subsection{Pickle Files and Model Artifacts}
Pickle files with the \texttt{.pkl} extension are commonly used in Python to
serialize trained objects such as Scikit-learn models or preprocessors. In the
Deployment module, these files are treated as model artifacts. They are copied
into the deployment workspace, loaded by the model service, and used during
inference.

\subsection{Version Control, Git, GitHub, and Repositories}
Version control records changes over time and helps users return to previous
states. Git is a widely used version-control system, GitHub hosts Git
repositories, and a repository stores a project's files and history. Accelera
also implements a lightweight model VCS for deployment artifacts. This VCS is
not a replacement for Git; it snapshots model files and deployment
configuration so that earlier model versions can be restored.

\subsection{Backend Services, FastAPI, and Uvicorn}
A backend service runs server-side logic and responds to client requests.
FastAPI is a Python framework for building APIs, and Uvicorn is an ASGI server
used to run FastAPI applications. The Deployment module uses FastAPI to define
prediction and health endpoints, while Uvicorn starts the application inside
the Docker container.

\subsection{Deployment Scripts}
Deployment scripts automate repeated infrastructure commands. Instead of asking
the user to copy files, build containers, start services, and check endpoints
manually, a script can execute these steps in sequence. Accelera's
\modulepath{deployment.py} script provides commands for preparing files,
building Docker images, running locally, deploying to Heroku, and deploying to
EC2.

\newpage

\section{Comparative Study of Previous Work}
\mbox{}

\subsection{Data Preprocessing and Feature Engineering for Data Mining:
Techniques, Tools, and Best Practices ~\cite{paraskevas2025data}}
Koukaras and Tjortjis discuss the role of data preprocessing and feature
engineering in machine learning and data mining projects. They explain that
preprocessing is not only a preparation step before model training, but also an
important part of the complete machine learning pipeline. The paper also
presents a general preprocessing framework that includes data cleaning, data
transformation, feature construction, feature selection, and dimensionality
reduction. For each stage, it explains different techniques and discusses the
advantages and disadvantages of each one.


\subsection{\texttt{scikit-learn} Pipeline}

\texttt{scikit-learn} is one of the most widely used machine learning libraries
in Python~\cite{pedregosa2011scikit}. It provides many preprocessing tools,
machine learning models, evaluation metrics, and utilities for building complete
machine learning workflows.

The \texttt{Pipeline} object in \texttt{scikit-learn} allows users to connect
several processing steps together. Each step is usually a transformer, and the
final step is usually an estimator. This makes the workflow cleaner and easier
to reuse.

In this project, \texttt{scikit-learn} is the main competitor for
\texttt{accelera\_pipe}. Both systems provide a way to build machine learning
workflows. However, \texttt{scikit-learn} pipelines are mainly linear, while
\texttt{accelera\_pipe} uses a graph-based backend. This allows
\texttt{accelera\_pipe} to support branching, shared intermediate results, and
parallel execution of independent parts of the workflow.

\subsection{OMPar: Automatic Parallelization with AI-Driven Source-to-Source Compilation}

OMPar is a related work that focuses on automatic parallelization using
AI-driven source-to-source compilation~\cite{ompar2024}. The main idea of
OMPar is to analyze C and C++ code, detect loops that can be parallelized, and
generate OpenMP pragmas automatically.

OMPar is important for this project because it inspired the general direction of
the Parallelizer module. Both approaches use a staged process that includes loop
analysis, loop classification, and pragma insertion. This makes the problem
easier to handle because the system does not treat parallelization as one single
step.

However, there is an important difference between OMPar and our Parallelizer.
OMPar uses an AI-based pragma generation approach. In our project, the pragma
generator was replaced with a rule-based generator. This decision was made
because the available training data was limited, and some of the available
examples were synthetic data. Depending completely on generated or biased data
could lead to unsafe pragma generation.

Therefore, our Parallelizer uses AI mainly to support loop classification, while
the final pragma insertion is controlled by deterministic safety rules. This
makes the system more conservative, but also safer and easier to explain.

\subsection{Combined Algorithm Selection and Hyperparameter Optimization}
Automated machine learning treats model-family selection, preprocessing choices,
and hyperparameter tuning as one structured optimization problem. Instead of
manually testing a small set of estimators, an AutoML system defines a search
space and evaluates configurations under a consistent validation protocol.

Given a set of algorithms $\mathcal{A}$ and a hyperparameter domain
$\Lambda_a$ for every algorithm $a$, combined algorithm selection and
hyperparameter optimization seeks

\begin{equation}
  (a^*, \lambda^*) =
  \arg\min_{a \in \mathcal{A},\,\lambda \in \Lambda_a}
  \widehat{L}(a, \lambda; D),
\end{equation}

\noindent where $\widehat{L}$ is an estimated validation loss on dataset $D$.
Auto-WEKA formalized this combined view by searching over both learning
algorithms and their hyperparameters~\cite{autoweka}. The domain is
conditional: parameters belonging to a random forest are inactive when the
selected model is logistic regression. This conditional structure motivates the
use of the ConfigSpace representation in Accelera AutoML.

\subsection{Search Strategies for AutoML}
Grid search evaluates a fixed Cartesian product and scales poorly as the number
of dimensions increases. Random search allocates trials more effectively when
only a subset of hyperparameters strongly affects performance~\cite{randomsearch}.
Sequential model-based optimization improves on uninformed sampling by fitting a
surrogate to observed configuration costs. SMAC uses a random-forest model for
algorithm configuration problems and selects candidates through an acquisition
function~\cite{smac}.

\medskip
\noindent Accelera AutoML follows this general model-based pattern. It first
evaluates initial and warm-start configurations, represents each configuration
as a numeric vector, augments that vector with fidelity variables, and fits a
random-forest regressor. Predictions from individual trees provide an empirical
mean and standard deviation. Expected improvement then ranks a randomly sampled
candidate pool. The implementation is inspired by SMAC principles but is a
project-specific optimizer rather than a dependency on the external SMAC
package.

\subsection{Multi-Fidelity Evaluation}
Full cross-validation on an entire dataset is expensive, particularly for large
ensembles or boosting models. Multi-fidelity optimization first evaluates
candidates using cheaper approximations. Fidelity may be varied by the number of
training instances, the number of validation folds, training iterations, or
model capacity. Poor candidates can be discarded early, while promising ones
receive more resources.

\noindent In this work, fidelity is represented by a tuple

\begin{equation}
  f = (s, q, k, b),
\end{equation}

\noindent where $s$ is the stage index, $q$ is the sample fraction, $k$ is the
number of cross-validation folds, and $b$ is a multiplicative model budget. The
fidelity vector is included in the surrogate input so that observations from
different stages remain distinguishable.

\subsection{Meta-Learning for Warm Starts}
Meta-learning attempts to transfer knowledge across machine-learning tasks. An
AutoML system can characterize a new dataset using metafeatures, compare it
with previously evaluated datasets, and schedule successful historical
configurations early. Auto-sklearn combines Bayesian optimization,
meta-learning, and ensemble construction~\cite{autosklearn}. The intended
benefit is not to guarantee that a historical configuration remains optimal, but
to reduce the cold-start period before useful observations are available.

\medskip
\noindent Accelera AutoML computes task-dependent statistical descriptors.
Shared descriptors include the number of instances and features,
numeric/categorical ratios, missing-value ratios, skewness, kurtosis, and
categorical symbol statistics. Classification additionally records class
entropy, class-count statistics, and class probabilities. Regression records
target mean, standard deviation, skewness, and kurtosis. Stored datasets are
ranked by distance in this metafeature space, and candidate configurations from
the closest datasets are converted back into active ConfigSpace objects.

\subsection{Ensemble Learning}
Ensembles combine several fitted models to reduce the risk of selecting one
unstable estimator. Voting uses the predictions or probabilities of multiple
base estimators. Stacked generalization instead trains a meta-model on
out-of-fold predictions from base models~\cite{stacking}. Out-of-fold
generation is essential: fitting the meta-model on predictions made on each
base model's own training observations would produce leakage and an overly
optimistic result.

\medskip
\noindent The Accelera implementation provides both strategies. Stacking uses
bagged base estimators, creates out-of-fold prediction blocks, performs forward
selection, and fits a logistic-regression meta-model for classification or a
ridge-regression meta-model for regression. The original feature matrix may
optionally be appended to the prediction blocks.

\subsection{Related AutoML Systems}

\begin{table}[H]
\centering
\acceleraTableStyle
\caption{Selected AutoML systems and their primary design emphasis}
\label{tab:automl_related_systems}
\begin{tabularx}{\textwidth}{>{\raggedright\arraybackslash}p{0.22\textwidth}X}
\toprule

\subsection{Model Deployment Platforms}
Deployment platforms such as Heroku, AWS EC2, AWS Elastic Beanstalk, Render,
Railway, Google Cloud Run, and Hugging Face Spaces provide ways to host
applications or model demos. Their role is important because they give
developers infrastructure for running web services. However, these platforms
mainly provide the hosting layer. The user usually remains responsible for
building the ML serving application, preparing dependency files, loading model
artifacts, validating inputs, logging predictions, and maintaining model
versions.

Accelera's Deployment module is different in scope. It is not a general cloud
provider; it is an ML-oriented deployment layer inside the Accelera workflow.
It takes the generated configuration and model pickle files, prepares a FastAPI
serving container, exposes prediction endpoints, runs schema validation,
records telemetry, and provides commands for local Docker, Heroku, and AWS EC2
deployment. Therefore, Accelera complements infrastructure platforms by
preparing the ML service itself and connecting it to the artifacts created by
the rest of the framework.

\begin{table}[H]
\centering

\label{tab:deployment_related_platforms}

\midrule

Application hosting with container deployment support. &
Accelera prepares the ML serving container and provides Heroku-specific
commands for pushing and releasing it. \\
\midrule
AWS EC2 &
General-purpose virtual machines with full server control. &
Accelera automates file synchronization, remote Docker build/run steps, and
health checks for model services. \\
\midrule
Render, Railway, or Cloud Run &
Managed hosting for web services or containers. &
Accelera focuses on creating the ML API, validation, telemetry, and deployment
package before such hosting is considered. \\
\midrule
Hugging Face Spaces &
Hosted ML demos and interactive applications. &
Accelera emphasizes integration with local model artifacts and repeatable
deployment workflows inside the project. \\
\bottomrule
\end{tabularx}
\end{table}

\section{Implemented Approach}

\subsection{Auto Preprocessing}
In this module, we decided to implement an automated preprocessing approach that
helps users prepare datasets before model training without repeating the same
manual cleaning steps each time. The approach was first designed for tabular
datasets, then extended to support text and image data. Its goal is to inspect
the input, choose suitable preprocessing steps, apply them, save reusable
artifacts, and generate reports that help the user understand what happened to
the data.

\noindent For tabular datasets, the chosen approach follows a classical automated
preprocessing pipeline. The main preprocessing decisions are:

\begin{itemize}
  \item \textbf{Missing values:} Median imputation is used for numerical
  features because it is simple and more robust to outliers than mean
  imputation. For categorical features, most-frequent imputation is used because
  it replaces missing values with valid existing categories.

  \item \textbf{Numerical scaling:} \texttt{RobustScaler} is used for numerical
  features because tabular datasets may contain outliers or skewed values. It
  depends on the median and interquartile range, which makes it more stable than
  standard scaling when extreme values exist.

  \item \textbf{Categorical encoding:} The encoding method is selected according
  to the feature type and cardinality. Binary columns are encoded using ordinal
  encoding, low-cardinality categorical columns are encoded using binary
  encoding, and high-cardinality categorical columns are encoded using frequency
  encoding. This helps reduce the number of generated features and avoids high
  dimensionality.

  \item \textbf{Feature selection:} Mutual information is used because it is a
  filter-based method that can work with both classification and regression
  tasks. It helps keep the most informative features and remove weak features
  before model training.
\end{itemize}

\noindent This approach was chosen because it gives a good balance between automation,
simplicity, and speed. More advanced techniques, such as K-nearest neighbour
imputation, autoencoders, and wrapper feature selection, can be useful in
specific cases, but they need more computation and may make the system harder to
use for beginners.

\noindent One important addition in this project is that the module is not only used for
data preparation. It also generates exploratory data analysis reports so the
user can learn about missing values, feature distributions, correlations, and
the preprocessing actions applied to the dataset. The same idea was extended to
text and image inputs, where the module applies suitable preparation steps for
each data type instead of forcing all datasets into one tabular workflow.

\subsection{Benchmark Platform}
In this module, we decided to implement a benchmark platform that supports
learning through repeated experimentation. The goal is not only to rank models,
but also to let users try a task, submit predictions, see their score, inspect
repository links from other submissions, learn from stronger techniques, improve
their own solution, and submit again.

\noindent The implemented approach separates benchmark creation from submission
evaluation. A benchmark creator defines the problem, dataset links, test set,
hidden target file, target column, problem type, and evaluation metric. After
that, participants submit prediction files and repository links. The system
validates the files, calculates the score automatically using the selected
metric, stores the submission, and displays the result on a leaderboard.

\noindent This approach was chosen because it keeps the learning process fair and
repeatable. All participants are evaluated using the same hidden ground truth
file and the same metric configuration, while the leaderboard and repository
links give users a practical way to compare approaches and improve their work.

\subsection{\texttt{accelera\_pipe}}
In this module, we decided to implement a graph-based machine learning pipeline
approach. The goal of this module is to make machine learning workflows easier
to build while also improving execution when the workflow contains branches or
repeated shared steps.

The general framework of the approach is as follows:
\begin{enumerate}
  \item The user builds a pipeline using high-level Python methods.
  \item The pipeline stores each operation as a node in an internal graph.
  \item Preprocessing, model training, prediction, metric evaluation, branching,
  and merging are represented as graph operations.
  \item The graph executor determines the correct order of execution.
  \item Independent branches can be executed in parallel when possible.
  \item Shared intermediate results can be reused instead of recomputed.
  \item Intermediate data can be released when it is no longer needed.
  \item The executed graph can be stored and reused for inspection or later execution.
\end{enumerate}

\noindent This approach was chosen because it keeps the pipeline idea familiar to
users who already know \texttt{scikit-learn}, but changes the internal execution
model. Instead of treating the workflow as only a sequence of steps,
\texttt{accelera\_pipe} treats it as a graph. This helps the system handle more
complex workflows that include multiple models, shared preprocessing, and branch
comparisons.

\subsection{Parallelizer}
In this module, we decided to implement a conservative automatic parallelization
approach. The goal is to speed up supported loop-heavy code by converting it to
C++, analyzing its loops, inserting safe OpenMP pragmas, and compiling it into a
native Python-callable extension.

The general framework of the approach is as follows:
\begin{enumerate}
  \item Take the input source code, file, function, or transformer method.
  \item Convert supported Python code into C++ when needed.
  \item Extract loops using a compiler-based analysis stage.
  \item Compute loop features such as memory access, dependency indicators,
  reduction patterns, branches, and function calls.
  \item Classify whether the loop may be parallelized.
  \item Apply rule-based safety checks before inserting any pragma.
  \item Insert \texttt{\#pragma omp parallel for} or a reduction pragma only
  when the loop is considered safe.
  \item Compile the transformed C++ code into a native extension.
  \item Return the optimized callable to be used from Python.
\end{enumerate}

\noindent The chosen approach follows the same general idea as AI-assisted
parallelization systems, but with a safer final pragma generation stage. The
pragma generator is rule-based instead of fully AI-generated because the
available data was limited and partly synthetic. This reduces the risk of
generating unsafe OpenMP directives.

\noindent The Parallelizer is also connected to \texttt{accelera\_pipe}. When a
preprocessing node contains a supported custom Python function, the pipeline can
attempt to optimize this function using the Parallelizer. This allows the
project to combine graph-level workflow execution with code-level acceleration.

\subsection{Deployment Module Technologies}
The Deployment module uses a set of practical software-engineering technologies
to move trained artifacts toward a runnable service. This section introduces the
tools used by the module; the detailed module architecture is described later
in Section~\ref{sec:deployment-module}.

Git-style versioning inspired the internal model VCS. The module does not
replace Git for source code, but it applies the same idea of named snapshots to
deployment artifacts. The VCS stores model files and \texttt{config.json}
inside commit directories, records metadata in an index file, and allows a
previous model snapshot to be restored before deployment.

FastAPI and Uvicorn form the serving layer. FastAPI defines HTTP endpoints for
single prediction, CSV prediction, health checks, tracking summaries, and the
interactive GUI. Uvicorn runs the application inside the container and listens
on the configured service port.

Docker is used as the portability layer. The deployment script writes a
\texttt{Dockerfile}, copies serving files, installs runtime dependencies, adds
the saved model artifacts, exposes the service port, and starts the Uvicorn
server. This keeps the runtime environment more repeatable than launching the
service directly from an arbitrary local Python environment.

Great Expectations supports the runtime validation layer. The configuration
file defines expected feature names, data types, required fields, allowed
values, and numeric bounds. Incoming requests are checked and coerced before
they reach the model service, which reduces the risk of invalid payloads
causing hidden prediction errors.

SSH and \texttt{rsync} are used for AWS EC2 deployment. The script connects to
the remote machine, creates the target directory, synchronizes the Dockerfile,
configuration, serving source, and model files, builds the container on the
remote host, starts it, and checks the health endpoint. Heroku deployment uses
Heroku CLI commands to authenticate, push the container image, release it, and
open the deployed application.

Configuration files and generated artifacts are the connection point between
training and deployment. The \texttt{config.json} file tells the service where
to find model pickle files, how to validate input data, and whether telemetry
should be enabled. The deployment scripts then convert these local artifacts
into a concrete service package.


\chapter{System Design and Architecture}
\section{Overview and Assumptions}
This section introduces the assumptions made for each module in Accelera and
explains why these assumptions were needed during the implementation.
The system includes modules for preprocessing, AutoML, graph-based pipeline
execution, code parallelization, deployment, and benchmarking. In this
architecture, the AutoML module provides automated supervised model selection
for tabular classification and regression under configurable search limits.
\subsection{Accelera Pipeline Assumptions}
The system combines Python-facing APIs with native execution components. The
design assumes that machine-learning workflows can be represented as a graph
of operations and that only a restricted, analyzable subset of custom source
code should be translated to parallel C++.
\subsection{Auto Preprocessing Assumptions}

The Auto Preprocessing module was designed based on the following assumptions:

\begin{itemize}
  \item \textbf{Data assumption:} The input data should be valid, meaningful,
  and one of the supported types, so the module can select the correct
  preprocessing workflow.

  \item \textbf{Configuration assumption:} The user should provide the needed
  configuration values, so the module can apply the correct preprocessing steps.
\end{itemize}
\subsection{AutoML Assumptions}

The Accelera AutoML module was designed based on the following
assumptions:

\begin{itemize}
  \item \textbf{Task assumption:} The input task is assumed to be supervised
  tabular classification or regression, so the module can validate the feature
  matrix and target vector using Scikit-learn-compatible utilities.

  \item \textbf{User configuration assumption:} The user is assumed to provide
  the required search settings, such as metric, cross-validation folds,
  time budget, trial limit, and whether meta-learning or ensembles should be
  enabled.
  
\end{itemize}
\subsection{Benchmark Assumptions}

The Benchmark Platform was designed based on the following assumptions:

\begin{itemize}
  \item \textbf{Benchmark data assumption:} The benchmark creator is assumed to
  provide the benchmark files correctly, including the training data, test data,
  and separate hidden target file. The creator is also responsible for ensuring
  that the file separation does not cause data leakage, so the platform can
  evaluate submissions fairly.

  \item \textbf{Learning and submission assumption:} Users are assumed to submit
  prediction files in the required format and, when possible, provide a
  repository link for their solution. This allows other users to compare
  techniques, learn from stronger submissions, improve their own models, and
  submit again.

  \item \textbf{Metric assumption:} The admin or benchmark creator is assumed to
  define the correct metric information for the benchmark, so the scoring script
  can calculate results consistently for all submissions.
\end{itemize}

\subsection{Deployment Assumptions}
The Deployment module assumes that the selected model or pipeline has already
produced serializable model artifacts and a deployment configuration file. The
configuration must describe the model files relative to the deployment
workspace, the expected input schema when validation is enabled, and the
tracking settings used by the serving application.

For local execution and cloud/server execution, the module assumes that Docker
is available on the target machine or can be installed during the EC2 workflow
when the corresponding option is selected. For remote EC2 deployment, the user
must provide a reachable host, a valid SSH user, and any required private key.
The public port used by the service must also be reachable from the client
machine for the final health check and GUI endpoint to work.

\newpage
\section{System Architecture}
\subsection{Block Diagram}
\label{subsec:block-diagram}

The block diagram in Figure~\ref{fig:block-diagram} gives the big picture of
Accelera. It shows the system as a group of connected modules rather than one
single fixed pipeline. This is intentional: a user can use each module alone, or
can connect several modules together to build an end-to-end machine-learning
workflow.

\begin{figure}[!htbp]
\centering
\makebox[\textwidth][c]{%
\includegraphics[width=1.08\textwidth,height=0.55\textheight,keepaspectratio]{imgs/block_diagram.png}
}
\caption{Overall block diagram of the Accelera project architecture.}
\label{fig:block-diagram}
\end{figure}

At the highest level, the user can interact directly with the six main parts of
the project: Auto Preprocessing, Accelera AutoML, Accelera Pipe, Parallelizer,
Benchmark Platform, and Deployment. The blue arrows in the diagram represent
standalone or module-level flows, while the red arrows represent the end-to-end
workflow path.

The architecture follows three broad stages: data preparation, model or graph
construction, and artifact consumption. Auto Preprocessing prepares reusable
features and preprocessing artifacts; AutoML and Accelera Pipe provide
alternative model-building paths; and Deployment or the Benchmark Platform
consumes the resulting model, executed graph, endpoint, or prediction file. The
Parallelizer is a supporting optimization module that can operate independently
on source code or accelerate supported custom preprocessing inside Accelera
Pipe.
```
