The red route represents the complete data-to-service lifecycle, while the blue
routes show standalone use. Modules exchange explicit artifacts rather than
internal state, allowing users to stop after any stage.

The Deployment module turns a saved model or pipeline into a validated API
service by packaging model artifacts, configuration, serving code, and deployment
commands. Its outputs may include a model URL, service endpoint, health endpoint,
telemetry summary, and optional inference UI.


\subsubsection*{Auto Preprocessing flow}

The Auto Preprocessing block starts from a dataset and tries to prepare it for
machine learning usage. The module first profiles the data and detects its type.
Then it applies preprocessing steps depending on the type of input.

\begin{itemize}
  \item \textbf{Tabular data:} missing value imputation, categorical encoding,
  robust scaling, and feature selection.
  \item \textbf{Text data:} cleaning, tokenization, stemming, and TF-IDF feature
  extraction.
  \item \textbf{Image data:} image preparation for classification tasks.
\end{itemize}

The output can be used in two ways. In the standalone path, the user can directly
use the processed data, reports, and saved preprocessors. In the end-to-end path,
the processed data can be passed to the AutoML block for model search.

\subsubsection*{AutoML and selection flow}

The AutoML block receives a dataset, extracts statistics and metafeatures, builds
candidate models and preprocessing choices, and runs a search process. Its goal
is to return the best model according to the selected metric.

The important point is that AutoML is not forced to be the only way to build a
model. The user may choose an AutoML result, or may choose a graph-pipeline
result from Accelera Pipe. This is why the diagram contains a selection switch.

\begin{table}[!htbp]
\centering
\acceleraTableStyle
\caption{Possible paths to the deployment selection switch.}
\label{tab:selection-switch-paths}
\begin{tabularx}{\textwidth}{p{0.26\textwidth}X}
\toprule
\textbf{Path} & \textbf{Meaning} \\
\midrule

AutoML path &
The user lets \texttt{accelera\_automl} search for the best model, then chooses
the selected model for deployment. \\
\midrule

Graph pipeline path &
The user builds a workflow using \texttt{accelera\_pipe}, executes the graph,
and selects the executed graph or best pipeline path for deployment. \\
\midrule

Standalone path &
The user may stop after any module and use its output directly without
continuing to deployment. \\

\bottomrule
\end{tabularx}
\end{table}

\subsubsection*{Accelera Pipe module}

Accelera Pipe is the graph-execution module of the system. Its role is to let the
user define a machine-learning workflow as a graph instead of a fixed linear
sequence. Each graph node represents one operation in the workflow, such as data
input, preprocessing, model training, prediction, metric evaluation, merging, or
branching. The edges between nodes describe data dependencies, meaning that a
node can execute only after its required source nodes have produced their
outputs.

This design allows Accelera Pipe to support workflows that are more flexible
than a traditional sequential pipeline. For example, the graph may contain
multiple model branches, shared preprocessing nodes, or metric nodes used to
select the best path. The module is also responsible for managing execution
order, selected-path extraction, persistence, and intermediate-memory lifetime.
After training, the selected fitted path can be stored in an executed graph so it
can be reused later for inference without keeping unnecessary temporary training
data.

Accelera Pipe therefore works at the workflow level. It does not only run a list
of steps; it manages how the steps are connected, when they are executed, which
branches are selected, and which intermediate results can be released safely.

Formally, a pipeline is represented as a directed acyclic graph

\begin{equation}
G=(V,E), \qquad E\subseteq V\times V,
\end{equation}

where each vertex $v\in V$ is an executable node and each edge $(u,v)\in E$
indicates that $v$ consumes the output of $u$. A node becomes ready only after
all its predecessors have completed:

\begin{equation}
\operatorname{ready}(v)
\Longleftrightarrow
\forall u\in\operatorname{Pred}(v),\;
\operatorname{state}(u)=\text{finished}.
\label{eq:graph-node-ready}
\end{equation}

Ready nodes without mutual dependencies may be scheduled concurrently by the
C++ backend. This is the basis of parallel branch execution; it is different
from OpenMP loop parallelization, which operates inside one custom function.

\begin{table}[!htbp]
\centering
\acceleraTableStyle
\caption{Principal node families in an Accelera Pipe graph.}
\label{tab:accelera-pipe-node-families}
\begin{tabularx}{\textwidth}{p{0.20\textwidth}X}
\toprule
\textbf{Node family} & \textbf{Architectural responsibility} \\
\midrule
Preprocess & Fits or applies a transformer and forwards transformed features. \\
Model & Fits a supervised or unsupervised estimator using upstream data. \\
Predict & Applies a fitted model method such as \texttt{predict} or
\texttt{predict\_proba}. \\
Metric & Evaluates predictions and supplies values used for path selection. \\
Branch/Merge & Expands alternatives or combines compatible branch outputs. \\
\bottomrule
\end{tabularx}
\end{table}

The graph also applies consumer-count-based lifetime management. If $c_t(u)$ is
the number of unfinished downstream consumers of node $u$ after execution step
$t$, then completing consumer $v$ updates the count by

\begin{equation}
c_{t+1}(u)=c_t(u)-1, \qquad u\in\operatorname{Pred}(v).
\end{equation}

Temporary output from $u$ becomes releasable when $c_{t+1}(u)=0$. Consequently,
a transformed array shared by several model branches remains alive until the
last model consumes it, then can be cleared without affecting correctness.
Duplicate first preprocessing nodes may also be shared, allowing equivalent
branches to reference one transformed result rather than repeat the same work.

After metric evaluation, the configured selection strategy determines the path
retained in the \texttt{ExecutedGraph}. This inference graph keeps fitted
preprocessors and models but does not need transient training arrays. It can be
serialized as one pickle artifact and later called with new input data.


\subsubsection*{Code Parallelizer module}

The Code Parallelizer is the code-level optimization module of the system. Its
main role is to analyze supported loop-heavy code and attempt to accelerate it
using C++ and OpenMP. The module can be used as a standalone tool or as an
internal optimization step when Accelera Pipe receives a custom preprocessing
function.

The Parallelizer accepts several kinds of input:

\begin{itemize}
  \item C/C++ file or source code.
  \item Python file or Python source code.
  \item Python function.
  \item Custom transformer method.
\end{itemize}

\begin{table}[!htbp]
\centering
\acceleraTableStyle
\caption{Parallelizer input dispatch and output behavior.}
\label{tab:parallelizer-input-dispatch}
\begin{tabularx}{\textwidth}{p{0.24\textwidth}p{0.30\textwidth}X}
\toprule
\textbf{Input} & \textbf{Normalization} & \textbf{Result} \\
\midrule
C/C++ file & Read native source directly & Parallelized source file. \\
Python file & Convert supported Python AST to C++ & Parallelized source file. \\
C/C++ or Python string & Detect syntax and convert Python when required &
Parallelized C++ source string. \\
Python function & Capture source and helpers, flatten nested functions, then
convert & Cached native Python-callable or original function on failure. \\
Custom transformer & Extract its custom \texttt{transform()} method & Optimized
method attached to the same instance when compilation succeeds. \\
\bottomrule
\end{tabularx}
\end{table}

After receiving the input, the module first normalizes it into a form that can
be analyzed. If the input is supported Python code, it is converted into C++ so
that both Python-originated and native C/C++ code can follow the same analysis
path. The module then extracts loops using a Clang AST based analysis stage.
After loop extraction, it computes features such as memory access patterns,
dependencies, reductions, branches, nested loops, function calls, and
vectorization potential.

These features are passed through a classifier and combined with deterministic
rule-based safety checks. This is important because the classifier alone is not
trusted to change program semantics. If the loop is considered safe, the module
inserts an OpenMP pragma such as \texttt{\#pragma omp parallel for}. If the loop
matches a reduction pattern, the module can generate an OpenMP reduction clause.
If the loop is unsafe or unsupported, it is left unchanged.

For each extracted loop $L$, feature extraction produces a fixed-dimensional
vector $\mathbf{x}(L)\in\mathbb{R}^{40}$. The classifier proposes a class from

\begin{equation}
\hat{y}(L)\in
\{\texttt{parallel\_for},\texttt{reduction},\texttt{none}\}.
\end{equation}

The proposal is then resolved with deterministic evidence. In simplified form,
the implemented decision can be written as

\begin{equation}
D(L)=
\begin{cases}
\hat{y}(L), & \hat{y}(L)\neq\texttt{none},\\
\texttt{reduction}, & L\text{ contains a supported scalar reduction},\\
\texttt{parallel\_for}, & L\text{ has independent array writes},\\
\texttt{parallel\_for}, & L\text{ is a supported consecutive nest},\\
\texttt{none}, & \text{otherwise}.
\end{cases}
\label{eq:parallelizer-decision}
\end{equation}

The safety analysis rejects evidence such as loop-carried dependencies,
indirect writes, and early exits before an independent-write rule is accepted.
For supported reductions, the generated directive has the form

\begin{equation}
\texttt{\#pragma omp parallel for reduction(}\odot\texttt{:r)},
\end{equation}

where $r$ is the reduction variable and $\odot$ is the recognized operator.

\begin{table}[!htbp]
\centering
\normalsize
\renewcommand{\arraystretch}{1.08}
\setlength{\tabcolsep}{4pt}
\caption{Parallelizer stages.}
\label{tab:parallelizer-stages}
\begin{tabularx}{\textwidth}{p{0.23\textwidth}X}
\toprule
\textbf{Stage} & \textbf{Description} \\
\midrule

Input normalization &
Accepts source code, files, functions, or transformer methods and prepares them
for analysis. \\
\midrule

Python-to-C++ conversion &
Converts supported Python code into C++ so it can enter the same loop-analysis
path as native C/C++ code. \\
\midrule

Loop extraction &
Uses compiler-style analysis to locate loops and record loop ranges. \\
\midrule

Feature extraction &
Computes indicators such as reductions, dependencies, array access, branches,
function calls, and vectorization potential. \\
\midrule

Safety decision &
Combines classifier output with deterministic rule-based checks to avoid unsafe
parallelization. \\
\midrule

Pragma insertion &
Adds OpenMP pragmas only when the loop is considered safe. \\
\midrule

Compilation and caching &
Builds optimized native code when needed and reuses cached results when
possible. \\

\bottomrule
\end{tabularx}
\end{table}

The final pragma generation is intentionally rule-based. This makes the behavior
safer and easier to inspect, especially when the available data for fully
AI-generated pragma generation is limited or synthetic.


\subsubsection*{Interaction between Accelera Pipe and the Parallelizer}

The two modules interact at custom preprocessing nodes. If an Accelera Pipe graph
contains a preprocessing node with a supported custom method, the graph can send
that method to the Parallelizer before storing or executing it. The Parallelizer
then attempts to analyze, rewrite, compile, and cache an optimized version of the
method. If successful, the optimized callable is attached back to the same graph
node. If optimization fails, the original method is kept.

\begin{enumerate}
  \item The user defines a graph workflow in \texttt{accelera\_pipe}.
  \item The graph detects a supported custom preprocessing method.
  \item The method is sent to the Parallelizer.
  \item The Parallelizer analyzes, rewrites, compiles, and caches the optimized
  implementation when possible.
  \item The optimized callable is returned and reattached to the graph node.
  \item The graph continues execution using either the optimized method or the
  original method if optimization fails.
\end{enumerate}

This interaction connects workflow-level optimization with code-level
optimization. Accelera Pipe controls the structure and execution of the machine
learning graph, while the Parallelizer attempts to speed up supported custom
code inside that graph. As a result, the system can optimize both the way the
workflow is scheduled and the way expensive preprocessing code is executed.

The replacement contract preserves behavior at the pipeline boundary. Let $f$
be the original Python preprocessing callable and let $\mathcal{C}(f)$ denote
source capture, conversion, OpenMP rewriting, compilation, and module loading.
The callable attached to the preprocessing node is

\begin{equation}
f_{\mathrm{node}}=
\begin{cases}
\mathcal{C}(f), & \text{if every optimization stage succeeds},\\
f, & \text{if source conversion, analysis, or compilation fails}.
\end{cases}
\label{eq:parallelizer-fallback-contract}
\end{equation}

This fallback contract gives the integration three useful properties:

\begin{itemize}
  \item \textbf{Transparent use:} the graph calls the node through the same
  Python interface regardless of which implementation is attached.
  \item \textbf{Failure isolation:} unsupported syntax or a compiler failure
  disables the optimization for that callable without invalidating the graph.
  \item \textbf{Persistent reuse:} source-backed functions retain serializable
  Python source, while successful native modules can be recovered from cache.
\end{itemize}


\subsubsection*{Benchmark Platform flow}

The Benchmark Platform is used for learning, evaluation, and ranking. It does
not take a full user pipeline as input. Instead, it works with benchmark data
links, user prediction submissions, and optional repository links that show the
technique used by the participant.

A benchmark owner creates a benchmark by providing the required CSV links:

\begin{itemize}
  \item training CSV file link,
  \item testing CSV file link,
  \item ground-truth target CSV file link.
\end{itemize}

A participant submits:

\begin{itemize}
  \item a predictions CSV file containing the prediction column,
  \item and optionally a GitHub repository URL for the project.
\end{itemize}

The platform compares the submitted prediction column with the true labels,
computes the score, and updates the ranking. Users can then inspect the
leaderboard, open repository links from other submissions, learn which
techniques performed better, improve their own model, and submit another
prediction file. This makes the benchmark module focused on fair evaluation and
continuous learning, not on directly executing the user's pipeline.

\subsubsection*{Deployment flow}

The Deployment block receives saved model artifacts and the active deployment
configuration. It prepares the deployment service by collecting the model files,
copying the serving source code, writing runtime requirements, generating a
Docker image definition, and exposing a FastAPI application. Inside the serving
container, requests pass through schema validation before reaching the model
service. After inference, telemetry is written so the user can inspect request
counts, statuses, latencies, and prediction activity.

The block also separates the service package from the target environment. The
same prepared service can be built locally in Docker, pushed to Heroku through
container commands, or synchronized to an AWS EC2 virtual machine over SSH and
run as a remote Docker container. This design makes Deployment the final
architectural bridge between the outputs of AutoML or graph pipelines and
real-world usage through a reachable HTTP endpoint.

The final deployment block produces a deployed model URL or service. This output
has two possible meanings:

\begin{itemize}
  \item \textbf{Standalone use:} the user deploys a saved model or pipeline
  directly and uses the endpoint.
  \item \textbf{End-to-end use:} the user selects a model or pipeline through the
  selection switch, deploys it, and receives the final E2E service output.
\end{itemize}

\subsubsection*{Overall architecture meaning}

The main idea of the block diagram is that Accelera is modular but connected.
Each module gives value by itself, but the full system becomes stronger when the
modules are combined.
\begin{table}[!htbp]
\centering
\acceleraTableStyle
\caption{Standalone use versus end-to-end use.}
\label{tab:standalone-vs-e2e}
\begin{tabularx}{\textwidth}{p{0.24\textwidth}X X}
\toprule
\textbf{Mode} & \textbf{Description} & \textbf{Example} \\
\midrule

Standalone use &
The user uses one module and stops at its output. &
Use Auto Preprocessing only to generate processed data and reports. \\
\midrule

End-to-end use &
The user connects several modules together to move from data to deployed result. &
Preprocess data, run AutoML or graph pipeline, select output, deploy service. \\
\midrule

Integrated acceleration &
A module internally uses another module to improve execution. &
Accelera Pipe sends supported custom preprocessing methods to the Parallelizer. \\

\bottomrule
\end{tabularx}
\end{table}

Therefore, the block diagram communicates three important ideas. First, the
system supports direct access to each module. Second, the modules can be
connected into a complete machine-learning workflow. Third, the interaction
between Accelera Pipe and the Parallelizer allows the project to combine
graph-based execution with source-level acceleration.


\input{sections/methdoloy-mohamed.tex}

\clearpage
\section{Auto Preprocessing Module}
\noindent The Auto Preprocessing module automates the data understanding, exploratory data analysis, and preprocessing stages in the data science workflow. It helps reduce the repeated manual work needed to prepare datasets before modeling.

\noindent The module also helps the user understand the dataset before and after preprocessing by generating a report. This report shows important information about the input data and the preprocessing steps that were applied. For tabular data, the report shows information such as the number of rows and columns, data types, missing values, duplicate records, numerical and categorical features, basic statistics and EDA . For text data, it shows information such as the number of rows, target classes, class distribution, and number of missing. For image data, it shows information such as the number of images, image classes, class distribution, image sizes, and corrupted images. After preprocessing, the report shows the steps applied to the data, such as imputation, encoding, scaling, feature selection, text cleaning, TF-IDF feature extraction, image resizing, normalization, and image preparation for classification.

\noindent Another important design point is separating the training phase from the inference phase. In the training phase, the preprocessing objects are fitted on the training data and saved as artifacts. In the inference phase, these saved artifacts are loaded and applied to new data. This ensures that new data is processed in the same way as the training data before it is passed to the trained model for prediction.

\begin{figure}[H]
    \centering
    \includegraphics[width=1\textwidth,clip]{imgs/auto_preprocessed.pdf}
    \caption{Auto Preprocessing Workflow}
    \label{fig:Auto_preprocessing_workflow}
\end{figure}
\subsection{Functional Description}
\noindent This module receives a dataset with its configuration, applies the suitable preprocessing workflow based on the data type and task, and returns processed data ready for training or inference. The module supports tabular, text, and image datasets.
\noindent The module also generates a report when this option is enabled. The report
summarizes information about the dataset and the preprocessing steps applied.

\subsubsection{Tabular datasets}
\noindent For tabular datasets it supports both classification and regression
tasks. It analyzes the dataset, removes duplicate records, splits the data into
training and validation sets, detects column types, handles missing values,
encodes categorical features, scales numerical features, applies target
preprocessing, performs feature selection, and saves the preprocessing objects
needed later for test data.
\subsubsection{Text datasets}
\noindent It supports classification tasks. It cleans the text,
tokenizes it, applies text preprocessing steps, and converts the text into a
numerical representation suitable for machine learning models.
\subsubsection{Image datasets}
\noindent It supports image classification by reading images from class
folders, validating image files, resizing images, applying augmentation, normalizing pixel values, and
preparing the data loaders needed for training.


\subsection{Modular Decomposition}
\noindent The Auto Preprocessing module is divided based on the type of input
data. Each data type has its own preprocessing workflow because every type has a
different structure and needs different techniques:

\begin{itemize}
    \item \textbf{Tabular data preprocessing:} handles structured datasets made
    of rows and columns.
    \item \textbf{Text data preprocessing:} handles text columns and converts
    cleaned text into numerical features.
    \item \textbf{Image data preprocessing:} handles image classification
    folders and prepares image loaders for training.
\end{itemize}

\subsubsection{Tabular Data Preprocessing Functions}


\begin{itemize}
    \item \textbf{\_\_init\_\_():}
    This is the constructor that receives the dataset and the user settings. 
    It validates these settings before starting the preprocessing process. 
    \item \textbf{handel\_bool\_types():}
    This function handles columns that have bool data type. It finds these columns and converts them into integer values.

    \item \textbf{data\_overview():}
    This function gives a general overview of the dataset before preprocessing.
    \item \textbf{drop\_target\_nulls():}
    This function drops rows have null value in the target.

    \item \textbf{drop\_duplicates():}
    This function removes repeated rows from the dataset.

    \item \textbf{split\_data():}
    This function splits the dataset into training and validation sets. The split is
    based on the validation size and random state selected by the user. The output of
    this function is \texttt{X\_train}, \texttt{X\_val}, \texttt{y\_train}, and
    \texttt{y\_val}.

    \item \textbf{get\_data\_info():}
    This function studies each column in the training data. It collects important
    information such as data type, number of unique values,
    missing values, and missing percentage. For numerical columns, it also calculates the mean,and median.

    \item \textbf{is\_drop\_column():}
    This function decides whether a column should be removed or kept. A column is
    removed if it is included in the user selected drop list, if it has only one constant
    value, or if its missing value percentage is higher than the selected threshold. 
    \item \textbf{drop\_col():}
    This function removes the selected columns from both the training and validation
    datasets. It also saves the dropped columns and their reasons in a file. This helps
    the system apply the same dropping step later on testing or new data.

    \item \textbf{detect\_column\_types():}
    This function detects the type of each feature. This step is important because each
    feature type needs a different preprocessing method. The features are divided into
    binary columns, ordinal columns, numerical columns, continuous columns,
    low cardinality categorical columns, high cardinality categorical columns, and
    unsupported columns. The function also stores the preprocessing steps for each
    column to be shown later in the report.
    \begin{table}[!htbp]
    \centering
\acceleraTableStyle
    \caption{Detected Column Types in Tabular Data}
    \label{tab:feature_types}
    \renewcommand{\arraystretch}{1.15}
    \begin{tabularx}{\textwidth}{p{0.23\textwidth}X p{0.25\textwidth}}
    \hline

    \textbf{Feature Type} & \textbf{Description} & \textbf{Examples} \\
    \midrule

    Binary &
    Columns containing exactly two unique values. &
    yes/no, 1/0 \\
    \midrule

    Ordinal &
    Integer columns with a small number of unique values less than or equal to
    \texttt{max\_ordinal\_unique}. &
    Ratings or levels such as 1, 2, 3, 4 \\
    \midrule

    Numerical &
    Integer columns with a number of unique values greater than
    \texttt{max\_ordinal\_unique}. &
    Age, number of transactions \\
    \midrule

    Continuous &
    Floating point columns. &
    Height = 175.5, price = 19.99, temperature = 36.6 \\
    \midrule

    Low Cardinality Categorical &
    Object or string columns with a number of unique values less than or equal to
    \texttt{cardinality\_threshold}. &
    Product categories, country group \\
    \midrule

    High Cardinality Categorical &
    Object or string columns with a number of unique values greater than
    \texttt{cardinality\_threshold}. &
    Product names, cities \\
    \midrule

    Other &
    Columns with unsupported or non standard data types. These columns are excluded from
    the preprocessing pipeline. &
    Dates, complex objects, unsupported formats \\
    \bottomrule

    \end{tabularx}
    \renewcommand{\arraystretch}{1.0}
    \end{table}



    \item \textbf{make\_graphs():}
    This function creates graphs when the report option is enabled. The type of graph
    depends on the feature type and the problem type.
    \Needspace{0.58\textheight}
    \begin{table}[!htbp]
      \centering
      \small
      \caption{EDA graphs generated for tabular preprocessing}
      \label{tab:tabular-eda-graphs}
      \renewcommand{\arraystretch}{1.08}
      \begin{tabularx}{\textwidth}{p{0.26\textwidth}p{0.19\textwidth}X}
      \hline
      \textbf{Column type} & \textbf{Problem type} & \textbf{Generated graphs} \\
      \hline
      Binary and categorical & Classification & Null vs non null pie chart,
      category count plot, and category count split by target class. \\
      \hline
      Binary and categorical & Regression & Null vs non null pie chart, category
      count plot, and target box plot for each category. \\
      \hline
      Ordinal & Classification & Null vs non null pie chart, ordered count plot, and
      ordered count plot split by target class. \\
      \hline
      Ordinal & Regression & Null vs non null pie chart, ordered count plot, and
      target box plot for each ordinal value. \\
      \hline
      Numerical and continuous & Classification & Null vs non null pie chart,
      histogram with KDE, and feature box plot grouped by class. \\
      \hline
      Numerical and continuous & Regression & Null vs non null pie chart, histogram
      with KDE, and scatter plot between feature and target. \\
      \hline
      Target column & Classification & Null vs non null pie chart and target class
      count plot. \\
      \hline
      Target column & Regression & Null vs non null pie chart, target histogram with
      KDE, and target box plot. \\
      \hline
      Numeric columns & Both & Correlation heatmap. \\
      \hline
      \end{tabularx}
      \normalsize
      \renewcommand{\arraystretch}{1.0}
      \end{table}

    \item \textbf{features\_preprocessing():}
    This function applies the preprocessing steps to the input features. It creates
    different pipelines based on the detected feature types:
    \begin{itemize}
        \item Numerical and continuous features are filled using the median and scaled
        using robust scaling.
        \item Binary features are filled using the most frequent value and encoded using
        ordinal encoding.
        \item Low cardinality categorical features are filled using the most frequent
        value and encoded using binary encoding.
        \item High cardinality categorical features are filled using the most frequent
        value and encoded using frequency encoding.
        \item Ordinal features are filled using the most frequent value and scaled using
        robust scaling.
    \end{itemize}
    These pipelines are combined using \texttt{ColumnTransformer}. The transformer is
    fitted on the training data and then applied to the validation data. The fitted
    preprocessor and the generated feature names are saved for later use.

    \item \textbf{target\_preprocessing():}
    This function prepares the target column based on the problem type. For
    classification label encoding is used to convert class labels into numbers. For regression robust scaling is applied. The target
    preprocessor is saved so the same transformation can be used later.

    \item \textbf{features\_importance():}
    This function selects the most useful features using mutual information with the target. Only features with scores
    greater than or equal to the selected threshold are kept. The selected features are
    saved in a file to use it in the inference step. 
    \item \textbf{common\_preprocessing():}
    This is the main function that controls the full tabular preprocessing workflow. It
    calls the previous functions in order. It starts by handling boolean columns,
    generating the data overview,drop rows have null target, removing duplicates, and splitting the data. Then, it
    extracts column information, drops unsuitable columns, detects column types,
    generates graphs, preprocesses the features, preprocesses the target, applies feature
    selection, and generates the report if enabled.It returns the processed
    training and validation data.
\end{itemize}

\Needspace{18\baselineskip}
\begin{lstlisting}[style=algostyle,caption={General tabular preprocessing flow}]
Input: DataFrame D, target column y, problem type, other configurations
Output: X_train, X_val, y_train, y_val

1:  Validate configuration
2:  Store original dataset columns
3:  Build data overview and report information
4:  Remove rows that have null target values
5:  Remove duplicate rows
6:  Split into training and validation sets
7:  Drop user-selected, high-missing, constant, and unsupported columns
8:  Detect column types from training data
9:  Generate feature and target graphs when report is enabled
10: Fit feature preprocessing objects on X_train
11: Transform X_train and X_val
12: Fit target preprocessing object on y_train
13: Transform y_train and y_val
14: Select useful features using mutual information
15: Save fitted objects and metadata
16: Generate report if enabled
\end{lstlisting}



\noindent Figure~\ref{fig:tabular_preprocessing_workflow} shows the full
tabular preprocessing workflow. It starts from the input dataset, applies data
cleaning and feature preprocessing steps, then saves the processed training and
validation data with the preprocessing objects needed later for testing.

\begin{figure}[H]
    \centering
    \includegraphics[width=1\textwidth,clip]{imgs/tabular.pdf}
    \caption{Tabular Preprocessing Workflow}
    \label{fig:tabular_preprocessing_workflow}
\end{figure}

\subsubsection{Text Data Preprocessing Functions}


\begin{itemize}
    \item \textbf{\_\_init\_\_():}
     This is the constructor that receives the dataset and the user settings. 
     It validates these settings before starting the preprocessing process.Also removes any columns that are not the text column or the target
     column. This keeps only the columns needed for text classification. It also
    downloads the required NLTK resources, such as \texttt{punkt} and
    \texttt{stopwords}.
    \item \textbf{make\_graphs():}
    This function creates graphs if the report option is enabled. It creates a graph for
    the text column . It also creates a graph for the target column. These graphs help the user understand the text data and the target class distribution.

    \item \textbf{custom\_text\_tokenizer():}
    This function is used to clean the text and split it into words. It converts the text to
    lowercase, removes extra spaces and unnecessary symbols, and handles some contractions
    such as \texttt{can't} and \texttt{won't}. It also separates numbers from letters.
    Then, the text is tokenized into words. Stopwords are removed, but words like
    \texttt{not} and \texttt{no} are kept because they affect the meaning. After that,
    stemming is used to reduce words to their basic form.

\item \textbf{features\_preprocessing():}
This function applies the preprocessing steps to the text column. It fills
missing text values in the training and validation data with empty strings. 
Then it uses the custom tokenizer . It also stores examples of the text
before and after cleaning in the report. Then \texttt{TfidfVectorizer} is used to convert the cleaned text into numerical
features. The vectorizer uses the user settings, such as the maximum number of
features, n-gram range, maximum document frequency, and minimum document frequency.
The vectorizer is fitted on the training data and applied to the validation
data. The fitted vectorizer is saved so the same
text transformation can be reused later on testing or new data.

\item \textbf{target\_preprocessing():}
This function prepares the target column for classification this function  applies label
encoding to the target values. The fitted label encoder is saved. Also, the text
column and target column information are saved . This helps the
system apply the same target transformation later on testing or new data.

\item \textbf{common\_preprocessing():}
This is the main function that controls the full text preprocessing workflow. It calls
the other functions in the correct order.
It creates the data overview. Then, it removes rows that have missing target
values and removes duplicate rows. Then, it splits the dataset into training and
validation sets. If the report option is enabled, graphs are created. Then, feature preprocessing is applied to clean and
vectorize the text. Target preprocessing is applied to encode the class
labels. Generates the report if enabled.It returns the processed
training and validation data.
\end{itemize}

\Needspace{14\baselineskip}
\begin{lstlisting}[style=algostyle,caption={Text tokenizer flow}]
Input: raw text sentence
Output: cleaned token list

1:  Convert text to lowercase and remove extra spaces
2:  Normalize apostrophe characters
3:  Expand common negative contractions
4:  Add spaces between letters and digits
5:  Remove characters except letters, digits, and apostrophes
6:  Split text into words
7:  Remove stopwords except "not" and "no"
8:  Remove very short tokens
9:  Apply Porter stemming
10: Return cleaned tokens
\end{lstlisting}

\noindent Figure~\ref{fig:text_preprocessing_workflow} summarizes the text
preprocessing workflow. It shows how the text data is cleaned, tokenized, and
converted into TF-IDF features before the final processed data is returned.

\begin{figure}[H]
    \centering
    \includegraphics[width=1\textwidth,clip]{imgs/text.pdf}
    \caption{Text Preprocessing Workflow}
    \label{fig:text_preprocessing_workflow}
\end{figure}
\subsubsection{Image Data Preprocessing}

\noindent It is used for image classification datasets. In this
module, the images are organized inside folders, where each folder represents one class.
It is divided into a set of functions, and each function has a specific role in
the image preprocessing workflow. The main functions are described as follows:

\begin{itemize}
    \item \textbf{\_\_init\_\_():}
    This is the constructor that receives the dataset and the user settings. 
    It validates these settings before starting the preprocessing process.It also prepares empty lists to store invalid images and their labels.
    It saves the image size information in a file so it can be reused later.

    \item \textbf{get\_classes\_mapping():}
    This function creates the class mappings. It gives each class name a numerical label.It creates two mappings:
    one from class name to label, and another from label to class name.These mappings are saved in files. This helps the system use the same labels during
    training, validation, testing, and prediction.

    \item \textbf{data\_preparing():}
    This function reads the images from the class folders. It goes through each class
    folder and checks each image path.If the image is valid and can be opened, its path is added to the list of valid image
    paths, and its class label is added to the labels list. If the image is invalid or
    corrupted, it is added to the invalid images list with its label.
    If there are no valid images, the function raises an error because the preprocessing
    process cannot continue without valid image data.

    \item \textbf{data\_overview():}
    This function collects general information about the image dataset. For the training
    folder, it stores the class names, number of valid images, random sample images,
    number of invalid images, invalid image paths, and the class mapping.
    If a validation folder is provided, it also stores the same type of information for
    the validation folder. This information is used later in the final preprocessing
    report.

    \item \textbf{make\_graphs\_label\_summary():}
    This function creates graphs that show the label distribution. It creates a label
    summary graph for the training folder. If a validation folder is provided, it also
    creates a label summary graph for the validation folder.

    If the training data is split into training and validation sets, the function also
    creates graphs for the labels after splitting. These graphs help the user understand
    if the classes are balanced or not.

    \item \textbf{make\_garphs\_sample():}
    This function creates sample image graphs. It shows random samples from the training
    folder. If a validation folder is provided, it also shows random samples from the
    validation folder.

    If the training data is split into training and validation sets, it also shows sample
    images after splitting. This helps the user visually check the image dataset.

    \item \textbf{make\_graphs\_loader():}
    This function creates sample images after using the DataLoader. It takes a small
    number of images from the training DataLoader and displays them.

    If a validation DataLoader exists, it also displays sample images from it. This step
    helps confirm that the images are loaded correctly after resizing, normalization, and
    augmentation.

    \item \textbf{make\_graphs():}
    This function controls all graph generation steps. It prepares the graph section in
    the report, then calls the label summary graphs, sample image graphs, and DataLoader
    sample graphs.

    \item \textbf{get\_loaders():}
    This function creates the training and validation datasets and DataLoaders. First, it
    creates a \texttt{ClassificationImageDataset} for the training images using the image
    paths, labels, image size, and augmentation settings.

    Then, it creates the training DataLoader with the selected batch size and uses
    shuffling. Shuffling is used for training because it helps the model see the data in
    a different order.

    If validation data exists, the function creates a validation dataset and validation
    DataLoader. Augmentation is not applied to the validation data, and shuffling is not
    used because validation should be more stable.

    \item \textbf{common\_preprocessing():}
    This is the main function that controls the full image preprocessing workflow. It
    starts by creating the class mappings. Then, it prepares the training images by
    collecting valid image paths and labels and saving invalid images.

    If a validation folder is provided, it also prepares the validation images. After
    that, it creates the data overview, splits the data if needed, creates the
    DataLoaders, generates graphs, and creates the final image preprocessing report.

    At the end, the function returns the training DataLoader and validation DataLoader.
    These outputs are ready to be used for training an image classification model.
\end{itemize}

\Needspace{16\baselineskip}
\begin{lstlisting}[style=algostyle,caption={Image training preprocessing flow}]
Input: training image folder, optional validation folder
Output: training and validation DataLoaders

1:  Read class folders from the training directory
2:  Create class-to-label and label-to-class mappings
3:  Save mappings and image size information
4:  Validate image files and skip invalid images
5:  Build the data overview for the report
6:  Split training images if no validation folder is provided
7:  Create training dataset with resize and optional augmentation
8:  Create validation dataset without augmentation
9:  Build DataLoaders
10: Generate label, sample, and loader graphs
11: Generate the image preprocessing report
\end{lstlisting}

\noindent Figure~\ref{fig:image_preprocessing_workflow} explains the image
preprocessing workflow. It shows how image folders are read, invalid images are
detected, class labels are created, images are resized and normalized, and final
DataLoaders are prepared.

\begin{figure}[H]
    \centering
    \includegraphics[width=1\textwidth,clip]{imgs/image_workflow.pdf}
    \caption{Image Preprocessing Workflow}
    \label{fig:image_preprocessing_workflow}
\end{figure}



\subsubsection{Inference and Reusability:}

For all supported data types, the saved preprocessing objects are reused during
testing. This is important because the testing data must be processed in the same way
as the training data.

\begin{itemize}
    \item For tabular data, the system loads the saved preprocessors, selected features,
    dropped columns, and target preprocessor.
    \item For text data, the system loads the saved TF-IDF vectorizer and label encoder.
    \item For image data, the system loads the saved class mappings and image size.
\end{itemize}

This makes the preprocessing module reusable and consistent for training,
validation, testing, and future prediction.
\subsection{Design Constraints}

The Auto Preprocessing module was designed under the following constraints:

\begin{itemize}
  \item \textbf{Supported data types constraint:} The module is limited to the
  supported data types only, which are tabular, text, and image data. Other data
  types are not handled by this module.

  \item \textbf{Input structure constraint:} Each supported data type must follow
  the expected structure. Tabular and text data should be provided as structured
  datasets with a target column, while image data should be organized in folders
  based on class names.

  \item \textbf{Automatic decision constraint:} The module depends on rule-based
  preprocessing decisions. Therefore, the preprocessing choices are limited to
  the implemented rules and supported configuration options.
\end{itemize}

\subsection{Module Summary}
\noindent The Auto Preprocessing module provides one reusable preprocessing
interface for tabular, text, and image data. It prepares data for training,
generates reports when enabled, saves fitted preprocessing artifacts, and reuses
the same artifacts during testing and prediction.


\section{AutoML Module}
\subsection{Functional Description}
\noindent The AutoML module automates supervised tabular model selection for
classification and regression. It receives the feature matrix, target vector,
and user configuration, then searches over valid preprocessing choices, model
families, and hyperparameters under explicit time and trial limits. The module
exposes two Scikit-learn-style estimators, \texttt{AutoMLClassifier} and
\texttt{AutoMLRegressor}, so the user can call familiar methods such as
\texttt{fit}, \texttt{predict}, \texttt{score}, and
\texttt{return\_leaderboard}.

\noindent The workflow starts by validating the input data and resolving the task type.
The engine builds a conditional configuration space where only the
hyperparameters belonging to the selected model are active. Each candidate is
evaluated using cross-validation and the selected metric. The evaluator converts
metrics into a cost value so the optimizer can compare all candidates using one
minimization objective. Failed trials and timeout cases are recorded in the run
history instead of stopping the full search.

\noindent To reduce unnecessary computation, the optimizer uses a staged fidelity
schedule. Early trials can run on smaller sample fractions, fewer folds, and
reduced model budgets. Promising candidates are promoted to higher-fidelity
stages, while weak candidates are discarded earlier. After enough observations
are collected, a surrogate-guided search step ranks new candidate configurations
using expected improvement. Meta-learning warm starts can also suggest initial
configurations from similar historical datasets.

\noindent At the end of the search, the strongest full-fidelity configuration is rebuilt
and fitted on the available training data. If ensemble construction is enabled,
the module can also build voting or stacked ensembles from successful candidate
models. The final model is selected by comparing the best individual model with
the ensemble result, and the module returns the fitted model, best score,
leaderboard, and complete run history.

\subsection{Block Diagram}
At a high level, Accelera AutoML follows a pipeline architecture. The user
interacts with a public estimator, the estimator validates the input data, the
AutoML engine builds a task-specific search space, and the optimizer repeatedly
selects candidate configurations for evaluation. The evaluator scores
candidates using cross-validation and returns structured results to the
optimizer. Meta-learning can provide warm-start configurations, and the ensemble
stage can combine strong candidates before final model selection.



Figure~\ref{fig:gp-automl-block_diagram_automl} shows the main blocks of the
Accelera AutoML module and the flow between user input, search, evaluation, and
final output.

\begin{figure}[H]
\centering
\includegraphics[width=0.92\textwidth]{imgs/block_diagram_automl.jpeg}
\caption{Block diagram of the Accelera AutoML module.}
\label{fig:gp-automl-block_diagram_automl}
\end{figure}

\subsection{Accelera AutoML Module Design}

\subsubsection{Method Overview}

The complete method is organized as the following sequence:

\begin{enumerate}[leftmargin=*]
  \item validate the feature matrix and target;
  \item resolve compatible or user-allowed model families;
  \item construct the conditional configuration space;
  \item compute dataset metafeatures and retrieve warm-start configurations;
  \item evaluate initial configurations at low fidelity;
  \item fit a surrogate after sufficient finite observations exist;
  \item select new candidates by expected improvement;
  \item promote strong candidates through higher fidelity stages;
  \item fit the best full-fidelity individual configuration;
  \item optionally construct and evaluate an ensemble.
  \item return whichever fitted candidate has the stronger validation score.
\end{enumerate}

\subsubsection{Configuration-Space Construction}

The configuration space defines the set of machine-learning pipelines that the
AutoML engine is allowed to explore. In this work, classification and regression
use independent component registries because the available estimators and their
valid hyperparameters differ between task types. Each component in a registry
specifies four main elements: a model name, a hyperparameter factory, conditional
rules, and an estimator builder.
\\
The global configuration contains a categorical variable called
\texttt{model\_name}. This variable represents the learning algorithm selected
for a trial. After a model is selected, only the hyperparameters belonging to
that model are activated. Hyperparameters belonging to other models remain
inactive and are ignored during evaluation. This conditional structure prevents
the optimizer from treating irrelevant parameters as active decisions. For
example, the number of neighbors is meaningful for a k-nearest-neighbors model,
but it is not meaningful for a random forest or a support-vector machine.
\\
Formally, let the set of candidate models be

\[
\mathcal{M} = \{m_1, m_2, \ldots, m_k\}.
\]
\\
Each model \(m_i\) has its own hyperparameter space \(\Lambda_i\). The full
conditional configuration space can be written as

\[
\Lambda =
\bigcup_{i=1}^{k}
\left(
\{\texttt{model\_name} = m_i\} \times \Lambda_i
\right).
\]
\\
This formulation means that each valid configuration consists of two parts: the
selected model and the hyperparameters that are valid for that selected model.
Therefore, the optimizer searches only within the subspace associated with the
chosen estimator, instead of searching over all possible hyperparameters at the
same time.

\begin{table}[H]
\centering
\caption{Example of conditional hyperparameter activation}
\label{tab:gp-automl-conditional-hyperparameters}
\begin{tabular}{p{0.26\textwidth}p{0.34\textwidth}p{0.30\textwidth}}
\toprule
Selected model & Active hyperparameters & Inactive hyperparameters \\
\midrule
Random forest
& number of trees, maximum depth, split criterion
& number of neighbors, kernel type \\
\midrule
K-nearest neighbors
& number of neighbors, distance metric, weighting strategy
& number of trees, maximum depth \\
\midrule
Support-vector machine
& kernel type, regularization parameter, gamma
& number of neighbors, tree depth \\
\bottomrule
\end{tabular}
\end{table}

\noindent Before constructing the final search space, the engine resolves the allowed model
set. The user may provide this set explicitly when only specific algorithms
should be considered. If no explicit set is provided, the engine starts from the
default registry for the task. The engine can then remove incompatible models
based on dataset properties. For example, some algorithms assume non-negative
feature values and can be excluded when the training data contains negative
values.

\noindent Restricting the search space has two main benefits. First, it reduces invalid
trials because the optimizer is less likely to sample configurations that cannot
be evaluated successfully. Second, it allows domain knowledge to be included
without changing the optimizer itself. The optimizer only receives the final
valid configuration space, while model compatibility and filtering rules are
handled by the AutoML engine. This separation makes the system easier to extend,
because new models can be added by registering their hyperparameters,
conditions, and estimator builder without rewriting the search procedure.

\subsubsection{Evaluation and Cost Conversion}

After a configuration is sampled, the evaluator is responsible for estimating
its predictive performance. For a configuration \(\lambda\), the engine first
builds the corresponding estimator and determines the preprocessing strategies
that are allowed for the selected model. The evaluator then tests each
model-preprocessor pair using cross-validation. Among all successful
preprocessing choices, the pair with the best validation performance is retained
as the evaluation result for \(\lambda\).

\noindent Let \(m(\lambda)\) denote the mean validation metric obtained by
cross-validation. Since different metrics have different optimization
directions, the evaluator converts the metric value into a cost value
\(c(\lambda)\). The optimizer always minimizes this cost, so smaller values
indicate better configurations.

\noindent For metrics that are naturally minimized, such as mean squared error or mean
absolute error, the cost can be used directly:

\[
c(\lambda) = m(\lambda).
\]

\noindent For metrics that are naturally maximized, such as accuracy, \(F_1\)-score, or
\(R^2\), the evaluator converts the score into a minimization objective. A common
conversion is

\[
c(\lambda) = 1 - m(\lambda),
\]

\noindent when the metric is bounded between 0 and 1. For metrics that are not strictly
bounded in this range, the evaluator may instead use a negated form:

\[
c(\lambda) = -m(\lambda).
\]

\noindent This conversion gives the optimizer a consistent objective regardless of the
original metric. In all cases, minimizing \(c(\lambda)\) corresponds to improving
the validation performance of the configuration.

\begin{table}[H]
\centering
\caption{Examples of metric-to-cost conversion}
\label{tab:gp-automl-metric-cost-conversion}
\begin{tabular}{p{0.28\textwidth}p{0.26\textwidth}p{0.32\textwidth}}
\toprule
Metric & Original direction & Example cost conversion \\
\midrule
Accuracy & Maximize & \(c(\lambda) = 1 - m(\lambda)\) \\
\(F_1\)-score & Maximize & \(c(\lambda) = 1 - m(\lambda)\) \\
\(R^2\) score & Maximize & \(c(\lambda) = -m(\lambda)\) \\
Mean squared error & Minimize & \(c(\lambda) = m(\lambda)\) \\
Mean absolute error & Minimize & \(c(\lambda) = m(\lambda)\) \\
\bottomrule
\end{tabular}
\end{table}

\noindent If an evaluation fails, the evaluator does not allow the error to terminate the
complete AutoML search. Instead, the failed configuration receives a predefined
failure cost and the error message is stored in the run history. This allows the
optimizer to continue exploring other configurations while still recording which
trials were invalid or unsuccessful.

\noindent The validation procedure depends on the learning task. For classification,
stratified cross-validation is used so that each fold preserves the class
distribution as much as possible. Stratified subsampling is also used when only a
fraction of the training data is evaluated at a lower fidelity. This is important
because random subsampling may accidentally remove minority classes from small
subsets.

\noindent For regression, ordinary K-fold cross-validation is used because there are no
discrete class labels to preserve. Random subsampling is also sufficient for
regression tasks. In classification, the requested number of folds may be
adjusted when the smallest class does not contain enough samples to support the
requested fold count. For example, if the smallest class contains only three
samples, then using five stratified folds is not valid. In this case, the
classifier reduces the fold count to a feasible value. This prevents invalid
cross-validation splits and improves the robustness of the evaluation process.

\subsubsection{Three-Stage Fidelity Schedule}

To reduce the cost of the AutoML search, the engine evaluates configurations
using a three-stage fidelity schedule. Instead of evaluating every configuration
using the full dataset, the full number of cross-validation folds, and the full
model capacity, the system begins with a cheaper approximation. Only
configurations that perform well at cheaper stages are promoted to more
expensive stages.

\noindent The default fidelity schedule is shown in Table~\ref{tab:gp-automl-fidelity-schedule}.

\begin{table}[H]
\centering
\caption{Default evaluation fidelity stages}
\label{tab:gp-automl-fidelity-schedule}
\renewcommand{\arraystretch}{1.25}
\begin{tabular}{c c c c p{0.45\textwidth}}
\toprule
\textbf{Stage} &
\textbf{Data} &
\textbf{Folds} &
\textbf{Budget} &
\textbf{Purpose} \\
\midrule
0 & 20\% & 2 & 25\% &
Inexpensive screening of many configurations. \\

1 & 50\% & 3 & 60\% &
Intermediate confirmation of promising configurations. \\

2 & 100\% & Configured value & 100\% &
Full-fidelity ranking and final comparison. \\
\bottomrule
\end{tabular}
\end{table}

\noindent Let \(s \in \{0,1,2\}\) denote the fidelity stage. Each stage is described by
three values: the sample fraction \(\rho_s\), the number of cross-validation
folds \(k_s\), and the model budget \(b_s\). Therefore, the trial specification
at stage \(s\) can be written as

\[
\tau_s = (\rho_s, k_s, b_s).
\]

\noindent The sample fraction controls how much of the training data is used during
evaluation. The number of folds controls the reliability of the validation
estimate. The model budget controls supported model-capacity parameters, such as
the number of estimators in ensemble models or the number of training iterations
in iterative models. Early stages are therefore cheaper because they use less
data, fewer folds, and smaller model budgets.

\noindent The evaluator scales supported capacity parameters using the model budget. If
\(\theta\) is a capacity parameter and \(\theta_{\max}\) is the value requested
by the sampled configuration, the stage-specific value can be written as

\[
\theta_s =
\max
\left(
\theta_{\min},
\left\lfloor b_s \theta_{\max} \right\rfloor
\right),
\]

\noindent where \(\theta_{\min}\) is the minimum valid value for that parameter. This keeps
the reduced model valid while still preserving the relative capacity requested
by the configuration.

\noindent A newly sampled configuration is first evaluated at stage zero. After a
successful evaluation, the optimizer stores the observed cost in a
per-configuration state. Once enough observations are available at a given
stage, the optimizer ranks configurations according to their costs. Since the
optimizer minimizes cost, smaller values are better. Only configurations inside
the configured top promotion quantile are advanced to the next stage.

\noindent The promotion rule can be written as

\[
\lambda \text{ is promoted from stage } s
\quad \Longleftrightarrow \quad
c_s(\lambda) \leq Q_q
\left(
\{c_s(\lambda') : \lambda' \in \mathcal{C}_s\}
\right),
\]

\noindent where \(c_s(\lambda)\) is the cost of configuration \(\lambda\) at stage \(s\),
\(\mathcal{C}_s\) is the set of configurations evaluated at stage \(s\), and
\(Q_q\) is the cost threshold for the configured promotion quantile.

\begin{figure}[H]
\centering
\renewcommand{\arraystretch}{1.25}
\begin{tabular}{c c c c c}
\textbf{Stage 0} &
\(\longrightarrow\) &
\textbf{Stage 1} &
\(\longrightarrow\) &
\textbf{Stage 2} \\
Cheap screening &
Top quantile &
Confirmation &
Top quantile &
Full ranking \\
\end{tabular}
\caption{Simplified promotion flow in the three-stage fidelity schedule}
\label{fig:gp-automl-fidelity-promotion-flow}
\end{figure}

\noindent The schedule spends the largest computational cost on configurations that were
already competitive under cheaper approximations. This avoids applying
full-fidelity evaluation to every sampled configuration and makes the search
more efficient.

\subsubsection{Surrogate-Guided Candidate Selection}

The search space used by the AutoML engine contains categorical,
continuous, integer, and conditional hyperparameters. Since the surrogate model
requires numerical input, every sampled ConfigSpace configuration is first
converted into a fixed-length numerical vector. Active hyperparameters are
encoded using their numerical or encoded categorical values. Missing or inactive
dimensions are replaced by finite sentinel values so that all configurations
have the same vector length.

\noindent Let \(x_{\lambda}\) denote the vector representation of configuration
\(\lambda\). Since the same configuration can be evaluated at different fidelity
levels, the vector is extended with the fidelity information. The augmented
input vector is defined as

\[
x' = [x_{\lambda}, s, q, k, b],
\]

\noindent where \(s\) is the fidelity stage, \(q\) is the sample fraction, \(k\) is the
number of cross-validation folds, and \(b\) is the model budget. Including these
values allows the surrogate model to distinguish between cheap low-fidelity
evaluations and expensive full-fidelity evaluations of the same configuration.

\noindent During the initial exploration phase, configurations are sampled without relying
on the surrogate model. This provides the optimizer with a set of observed
configuration--cost pairs. After enough successful evaluations have been
collected, the optimizer fits a random-forest regressor using only finite
observed costs. Failed evaluations or non-finite results are excluded from the
surrogate training data because they do not provide a reliable numerical target.

\noindent The random forest acts as an approximation of the unknown cost function. If
tree \(t\) predicts the cost \(\hat{c}_t(x)\) for candidate vector \(x\), then
the surrogate mean prediction is estimated as

\[
\mu(x) =
\frac{1}{T}
\sum_{t=1}^{T}
\hat{c}_t(x),
\]

\noindent where \(T\) is the number of trees in the forest. The uncertainty estimate is
computed from the disagreement between the trees:

\[
\sigma(x) =
\sqrt{
\frac{1}{T}
\sum_{t=1}^{T}
\left(
\hat{c}_t(x) - \mu(x)
\right)^2
}.
\]

\noindent A small value of \(\sigma(x)\) means that the trees give similar predictions,
while a larger value means that the trees disagree more strongly. Therefore,
\(\mu(x)\) estimates the expected cost of a candidate, and \(\sigma(x)\)
estimates the uncertainty of that prediction.

\noindent The optimizer then uses expected improvement to rank candidate configurations.
Because the objective is to minimize cost, improvement is measured relative to
the best observed cost \(c^+\). For a candidate \(x\), the predicted improvement
is

\[
I(x) = c^+ - \mu(x) - \xi,
\]

\noindent where \(\xi\) is a small exploration parameter. A larger value of \(\xi\)
encourages the optimizer to prefer candidates that are more likely to produce a
clear improvement rather than only a small predicted gain.

\noindent The standardized improvement is

\[
z(x) =
\frac{I(x)}{\sigma(x)}.
\]

\noindent The expected improvement is then computed as

\[
\operatorname{EI}(x) =
I(x)\Phi(z)
+
\sigma(x)\phi(z),
\]

\noindent where \(\Phi\) is the standard normal cumulative distribution function and
\(\phi\) is the standard normal probability density function. The first term
rewards candidates with a low predicted cost, while the second term rewards
candidates with higher predictive uncertainty. Therefore, expected improvement
balances exploitation and exploration.

\begin{table}[H]
\centering
\caption{Main quantities used in surrogate-guided candidate selection}
\label{tab:gp-automl-surrogate-quantities}
\renewcommand{\arraystretch}{1.2}
\begin{tabular}{p{0.22\textwidth}p{0.68\textwidth}}
\toprule
\textbf{Symbol} & \textbf{Meaning} \\
\midrule
\(x_{\lambda}\) &
Numerical vector representation of configuration \(\lambda\). \\

\(x'\) &
Augmented vector containing configuration and fidelity information. \\

\(\mu(x)\) &
Mean cost predicted by the random-forest surrogate. \\

\(\sigma(x)\) &
Prediction uncertainty estimated from disagreement between trees. \\

\(c^+\) &
Best observed cost so far. \\

\(\xi\) &
Exploration parameter used in expected improvement. \\

\(\operatorname{EI}(x)\) &
Expected improvement score used to rank candidate configurations. \\
\bottomrule
\end{tabular}
\end{table}

\noindent In each surrogate-guided step, the optimizer samples a pool of candidate
configurations from the conditional search space. The candidates are converted
to numerical vectors, duplicate vectors are removed, and the remaining unseen
candidates are scored using expected improvement. The candidate with the highest
expected improvement is scheduled for evaluation.

\begin{figure}[H]
\centering
\renewcommand{\arraystretch}{1.25}
\begin{tabular}{c}
Sample candidate pool \\
\(\Downarrow\) \\
Convert configurations to numerical vectors \\
\(\Downarrow\) \\
Append fidelity information \\
\(\Downarrow\) \\
Remove duplicate and already-evaluated candidates \\
\(\Downarrow\) \\
Predict \(\mu(x)\) and \(\sigma(x)\) using the surrogate \\
\(\Downarrow\) \\
Compute expected improvement \\
\(\Downarrow\) \\
Select the highest-ranked unseen candidate
\end{tabular}
\caption{Surrogate-guided candidate-selection workflow}
\label{fig:gp-automl-surrogate-candidate-selection}
\end{figure}

\noindent This procedure avoids exhaustive enumeration of the mixed conditional search
space. Instead of evaluating every possible configuration, the optimizer uses the
surrogate model to focus the search on configurations that are either predicted
to perform well or have enough uncertainty to be worth exploring.

\subsubsection{Warm-Start Selection}

Warm-start selection gives the optimizer an informed starting point by reusing
configurations that performed well on related historical datasets. For a new
dataset \(D\), the engine first computes a task-specific metafeature vector
\(m_D\). Historical datasets are represented by stored metafeature vectors
\(m_i\), together with their previously successful configurations.

\noindent The similarity between the new dataset and each historical dataset is measured
using a normalized distance. Let \(r\) be a scaling vector for the metafeature
dimensions. The distance between the current dataset \(D\) and a historical
dataset \(i\) is

\[
d(D,i) =
\left\|
\frac{m_D - m_i}{r}
\right\|_2 .
\]

\noindent The normalization prevents large-scale metafeatures, such as the number of
samples or features, from dominating smaller-scale metafeatures. Historical
datasets are then ranked by increasing distance, so datasets with smaller
distances are considered more similar to the current task.

\noindent After ranking the historical datasets, the engine gathers candidate
configurations from the nearest datasets. These configurations are not inserted
blindly into the search. First, configurations whose model names are not present
in the active model set are removed. This ensures that warm starts respect the
current user constraints and dataset compatibility checks. Next, configurations
are deduplicated using their model name and parameter signature, so repeated
historical suggestions do not occupy multiple positions in the initial queue.

\noindent Each remaining historical configuration is then converted into a valid
ConfigSpace configuration for the current search space. During this conversion,
invalid or unsupported parameters are skipped safely. This can happen, for
example, when the current version of a component does not contain a parameter
that existed in a stored historical configuration, or when the current active
space has stricter bounds. A failed conversion does not stop the search; it only
removes that warm-start candidate.

\begin{figure}[H]
\centering
\renewcommand{\arraystretch}{1.25}
\begin{tabular}{rcl}
Current dataset \(D\)
& \(\xrightarrow{\text{extract}}\) &
Metafeatures \(m_D\) \\

Historical metafeatures \(m_i\)
& \(\xrightarrow{\text{compare with } m_D}\) &
Distances \(d(D,i)\) \\

Distances \(d(D,i)\)
& \(\xrightarrow{\text{sort}}\) &
Nearest historical datasets \\

Nearest datasets
& \(\xrightarrow{\text{collect}}\) &
Historical configurations \\

Historical configurations
& \(\xrightarrow{\text{filter + deduplicate}}\) &
Valid warm-start candidates \\

Valid warm-start candidates
& \(\xrightarrow{\text{convert}}\) &
Initial configuration queue \\
\end{tabular}
\caption{Warm-start transfer from similar historical datasets}
\label{fig:gp-automl-warm-start-transfer}
\end{figure}

\noindent The number of historical datasets used, the number of configurations taken from
each dataset, and the total number of warm-start configurations are bounded by
user parameters. These bounds keep the warm-start stage controlled and prevent
historical suggestions from dominating the search.

\noindent Warm starts are inserted into the initial configuration queue before uninformed
random samples. However, they are still evaluated on the current dataset using
the same validation and cost-conversion procedure as all other configurations.
Therefore, warm starts only guide the early search; they do not bypass
evaluation and cannot directly force the final selected model.

\subsubsection{Parallel Execution and Timeout Control}

The optimizer may suggest a batch of trials in one scheduling step. A batch can
contain promoted configurations from earlier fidelity stages, warm-start
configurations from historical datasets, and newly proposed configurations
selected either by the surrogate model or by random sampling. This batching
allows the search to use multiple CPU cores while keeping the optimizer logic
centralized in the parent process.

\noindent For parallel execution, each trial is executed in a separate worker process. The
worker receives the configuration, builds the corresponding estimator, evaluates
it, and returns an \texttt{EvaluationResult} through a process-safe queue. The
parent process does not wait passively for all workers to finish. Instead, it
periodically checks the worker queues, collects completed results, and monitors
the elapsed time of each running process.

\noindent If a worker process exceeds the configured
\texttt{per\_run\_time\_limit}, the parent process terminates it and records the
trial with a timeout status. The timeout result is stored in the run history in
the same way as other failed evaluations, together with a failure cost. This
ensures that one slow or non-terminating estimator does not block the complete
AutoML search.

\noindent The timeout mechanism can be summarized as follows:

\begin{figure}[H]
\centering
\renewcommand{\arraystretch}{1.25}
\begin{tabular}{rcl}
Optimizer batch
& \(\longrightarrow\) &
Worker processes \\

Worker process
& \(\longrightarrow\) &
Evaluate one trial \\

Successful worker
& \(\longrightarrow\) &
Return \texttt{EvaluationResult} through queue \\

Slow worker
& \(\longrightarrow\) &
Terminate after \texttt{per\_run\_time\_limit} \\

Parent process
& \(\longrightarrow\) &
Collect results and update run history \\
\end{tabular}
\caption{Process-level parallel execution and timeout handling}
\label{fig:gp-automl-parallel-timeout-control}
\end{figure}

\noindent Process-level execution is used instead of thread-level execution because some
estimators cannot always be interrupted reliably once they enter a long-running
fit procedure. Terminating a separate process is more robust than attempting to
stop a thread inside the same Python process. This is especially useful when an
estimator hangs, becomes extremely slow, or calls compiled code that does not
regularly return control to Python.

\noindent Overall, this design improves robustness and resource control. The optimizer can
continue receiving valid results even when some trials fail or time out, and the
caller can control the amount of parallel work performed by the search.


\subsubsection{Final Model and Ensemble Selection}

After the search phase finishes, the engine selects the strongest configuration
evaluated at full fidelity. This configuration is rebuilt from its stored model
name, preprocessing choice, and hyperparameters, and then fitted on all
available training data. This final refitting step is necessary because the
search phase estimates performance using validation folds, while the final model
should use the complete training set before being returned to the user.

\noindent If ensemble construction is disabled, the refitted best individual model is
returned directly. If ensembles are enabled, the engine considers successful
candidates from the run history as possible ensemble members. Failed trials,
timed-out trials, and configurations without valid fitted estimators are ignored.
This ensures that the ensemble is built only from models that were evaluated
successfully during the search.

\noindent The ensemble construction stage supports two strategies: voting and stacking.
For voting, the engine selects a bounded set of competitive estimators from the
run history. These estimators are combined using a task-specific voting model.
For classification, the voting ensemble combines class predictions or class
probabilities. For regression, it combines numeric predictions. Voting is simple
and robust because it does not train an additional meta-model.

\noindent Stacking uses a more expressive structure. Instead of directly averaging the
base-model outputs, it trains a meta-model that learns how to combine them. The
engine first considers candidate diversity before selecting base estimators, so
the stack is not formed only from many near-identical models. Each selected base
estimator is wrapped in bagging to improve stability. The base estimators then
produce out-of-fold predictions, which are used as training features for the
meta-model.

\noindent Forward selection is used to choose the final set of base estimators for the
stacking ensemble. The procedure starts with the strongest single candidate.
Additional candidates are tested one at a time. A candidate is kept if adding it
improves the validation score of the meta-model. The process stops when no
remaining candidate improves the score, or when the configured ensemble size is
reached. However, the minimum base-model requirement is also respected, so the
engine can include enough base estimators to form a valid stack even if early
improvements are small.

\noindent For classification, each base estimator may output a probability vector over the
class labels. Before these outputs are passed to the meta-model, the probability
columns are aligned according to the global class order. This avoids a common
problem where different classifiers store class labels in different orders. If
the columns were not aligned, the meta-model could interpret the probability of
one class as if it belonged to another class. After alignment, the probability
blocks from all base estimators are concatenated and passed to logistic
regression as the classification meta-model.

\noindent For regression, the stacking representation is simpler. Each base estimator
contributes one numeric prediction column. These columns are concatenated to
form the meta-feature matrix, and ridge regression is used as the regression
meta-model. Ridge regression is suitable here because it can combine correlated
base predictions while controlling the size of the learned coefficients.

\noindent The best individual model is always available as a safe fallback. The ensemble
is only returned when it performs at least as well as this individual model under
the same validation metric. The final decision can be written as

\[
\text{Final model} =
\begin{cases}
\text{ensemble}, & \text{if } S_{\text{ens}} \geq S_{\text{best}}, \\
\text{best individual model}, & \text{otherwise},
\end{cases}
\]

\noindent where \(S_{\text{ens}}\) is the validation score of the ensemble and
\(S_{\text{best}}\) is the validation score of the strongest individual model.
This rule prevents the system from returning a more complex ensemble unless it
provides at least the same predictive quality as the best single model. As a
result, ensemble construction can improve performance when the base models
complement each other, while still preserving the simpler individual model when
the ensemble does not provide a measurable benefit.

\subsection{Modular Decomposition}
\noindent The AutoML module is divided into cooperating components so that search,
evaluation, model definitions, warm starts, and final model construction can be
extended separately.

\begin{table}[H]
\centering
\acceleraTableStyle
\caption{AutoML implementation units}
\label{tab:automl_implementation_units}
\small
\begin{tabularx}{\textwidth}{>{\raggedright\arraybackslash}p{0.43\textwidth}X}
\toprule
\textbf{File or directory} & \textbf{Responsibility} \\
\midrule
\modulepath{base.py} & Shared estimator lifecycle, validation, fitted state,
leaderboard, and warning handling. \\
\midrule
\modulepath{classification.py} & Classification defaults and task-specific engine
construction. \\
\midrule
\modulepath{regression.py} & Regression defaults and task-specific engine

\midrule

\midrule
\modulepath{configspace\_search\_space.py} & Task-specific conditional

\midrule
