```
\bottomrule
\end{tabularx}
\end{table}

\subsection{Design Constraints}

\begin{itemize}
  \item The current module targets supervised tabular classification and
  regression.
  \item Inputs must be compatible with Scikit-learn validation utilities.
  \item The evaluator assumes independent and identically distributed samples.
  Classification uses stratified splitting where possible, while regression uses
  ordinary K-fold or shuffle splitting.
\end{itemize}

\subsection{Module Summary}
\noindent The AutoML module provides an Accelera-integrated model-search layer for
supervised tabular classification and regression. It combines conditional
configuration-space construction, cross-validated evaluation, staged fidelity,
surrogate-guided candidate selection, meta-learning warm starts, process-level
timeout control, and final ensemble selection. The module keeps a familiar
Scikit-learn-style interface while exposing useful inspection outputs such as
the leaderboard and run history. This makes it usable as both a practical model
selection tool and an extensible research component inside Accelera.

\input{sections/methodology-deployment}


\section{Benchmark Platform Module}
\noindent The Benchmark Platform module is designed to provide a shared learning
environment where users can create machine learning benchmarks, submit
predicted results, compare submissions, and improve their techniques by studying
other solutions. The module includes a backend API, database schemas, Python
validation and scoring scripts, and a frontend interface for users and admins.

\noindent The module separates benchmark creation from submission evaluation.
This makes the workflow clearer: first, the benchmark creator defines the task
and evaluation rules; then, users submit predictions under the same conditions.
This separation helps ensure that all users are evaluated using the same
ground-truth file and the same metric configuration. The leaderboard and
repository links also allow users to see which approaches performed better,
learn from them, improve their own model, and submit again. Another important
design point is the use of Python scripts for file validation and metric
scoring. The backend is implemented using Node.js and Express, while Python
handles the machine learning evaluation part through Scikit-learn metrics and
dataset loading utilities.

\subsection{Functional Description}
\noindent The main function of this module is to support fair comparison and
learning between different machine learning solutions. A user can create a
benchmark by providing the benchmark title, description, problem type, target
column, dataset link, test dataset link, ground-truth target link, and
evaluation metric. The system validates the provided links and checks that the
test set does not contain the target column while the target file contains the
expected target column.

\noindent After a benchmark is created, other users can submit their prediction
file and repository link. The system validates the submitted prediction file,
calculates the score using the selected Scikit-learn metric, stores the
submission, and displays submissions on a leaderboard. The leaderboard is
ordered based on the metric configuration, where some metrics are better when
their value is higher and others are better when their value is lower. By
checking the ranking and the repository links, users can learn from other
solutions, modify their own approach, and submit improved predictions.

\subsection{Modular Decomposition}
\noindent The Benchmark Platform is decomposed into several smaller components. Each
component has a clear responsibility, so the benchmark workflow can be easier to
build, test, and maintain.

\begin{itemize}
  \item \textbf{Backend Server and API Layer:}
  The backend creates the Express application, enables CORS and JSON parsing,
  connects to MongoDB, and mounts the main API routes. The backend exposes
  separate routes for users, metrics, benchmarks, and submissions. This makes
  the server organized because each group of operations is placed in its own
  route.

  \item \textbf{Database Models:}
  The module uses Mongoose schemas to store the main data. The user schema
  stores the user name, email, hashed password, and role. The benchmark schema
  stores the title, description, target column, dataset links, problem type,
  selected metric, metric parameters, creation date, and creator. The metric
  schema stores the display name, Scikit-learn metric function name, problem
  type, needed parameters, and whether higher or lower values are better. The
  submission schema stores the benchmark id, submitting user, repository link,
  prediction file link, submission date, and calculated score.

  \item \textbf{Benchmark Management:}
  This component handles creating, listing, filtering, viewing, and deleting
  benchmarks. Users can list all benchmarks, filter benchmarks by problem type,
  or show only benchmarks created by a specific user. When a benchmark is
  created, the backend checks the problem type, checks that the title is unique,
  validates the dataset links, and calls the Python validation script before
  saving the benchmark in MongoDB. Deletion is protected, so only the benchmark
  creator or an admin can delete a benchmark, and a benchmark cannot be deleted
  if it already has submissions.

  \item \textbf{Metric Management:}
  This component allows metrics to be listed, filtered by problem type,
  displayed, created, and deleted. Creating and deleting metrics is limited to
  admin users because a wrong metric can affect many benchmarks. Each metric
  contains a display name, the matching Scikit-learn metric function name, the
  problem type, needed parameters, and the value direction using
  \texttt{higher} or \texttt{lower}. The delete route also checks if the metric
  is already used by a benchmark, so the system does not remove a metric that is
  still needed.

  \item \textbf{Submission Management:}
  This component allows users to submit prediction files for a specific
  benchmark and attach a repository link that shows their solution. Before
  saving a submission, the backend checks that the user did not already submit
  to the same benchmark, validates the links, loads the benchmark metric, and
  calls the Python scoring script. Users can also update their prediction file,
  which recalculates the score, or delete their submission if they are the owner
  or an admin.

  \item \textbf{Validation and Scoring Scripts:}
  The backend calls Python scripts for dataset validation and scoring. During
  benchmark creation, the validation script checks that the test file does not
  include the target column, that the target file includes the required target
  column, and that the test and target files have the same number of rows.
  During submission, the scoring script loads the hidden target file and the
  submitted prediction file, checks their length and target column, then
  computes the selected metric from \texttt{sklearn.metrics}. A helper function
  is also used to convert Google Drive file links into direct download links.

  \item \textbf{Authentication and Authorization:}
  The platform uses user accounts so benchmark and submission actions can be
  connected to their owners. The user routes handle signup and login, hash
  passwords, and return a JWT token. The authentication middleware reads the
  token and attaches the logged-in user to the request. The admin middleware is
  used for admin-only actions such as creating metrics and deleting metrics.
  These checks are also used to protect benchmark and submission deletion.

  \item \textbf{Leaderboard Interface:}
  The frontend leaderboard displays the submissions of one benchmark with the
  submitter name, repository link, score, and submission date. The backend sorts
  the submissions based on the selected metric direction, so the best score
  appears correctly whether the metric should be maximized or minimized. The
  same screen also lets a logged-in user add a new submission, update an
  existing prediction file, or delete a submission if the user has permission.

  \item \textbf{Frontend Benchmark and Metric Screens:}
  The React frontend provides screens for the main user actions. The benchmarks
  screen lists benchmarks and supports filtering by problem type or by the
  logged-in user. The create benchmark screen lets a user enter the title,
  description, target column, dataset links, problem type, metric, and metric
  parameters. The display benchmark screen shows benchmark details and links to
  the leaderboard. The metrics screens allow users to view available metrics,
  while admins can create new metrics with their Scikit-learn function name,
  needed parameters, problem type, and score direction.
\end{itemize}

\Needspace{12\baselineskip}
\begin{lstlisting}[style=algostyle,caption={Benchmark creation flow}]
Input: benchmark form data
Output: saved benchmark or validation error

1:  Receive benchmark creation request
2:  Validate problem type
3:  Check title uniqueness
4:  Validate dataset, test set, and target label links
5:  Run benchmark file validation
6:  If the test file contains the target column, reject benchmark
7:  If the target file does not contain the target column, reject benchmark
8:  Save benchmark document in MongoDB
\end{lstlisting}

\Needspace{12\baselineskip}
\begin{lstlisting}[style=algostyle,caption={Submission scoring flow}]
Input: benchmark target file, submitted prediction file, metric name
Output: score or validation error

1:  Retrieve true target file
2:  Retrieve submitted prediction file
3:  Check that both files have the same number of rows
4:  Check that submitted file contains the target column
5:  Load metric function from sklearn.metrics
6:  Compute metric(true_target, predicted_target, metric_parameters)
7:  Return score to the backend as JSON
8:  Save submission with score
\end{lstlisting}

\subsection{Design Constraints}
\noindent Several constraints affected the design of the Benchmark Platform:

\begin{itemize}
  \item \textbf{Task and data constraint:} The platform is limited to
  classification and regression benchmarks that use tabular files. This includes
  the training data, test data, prediction file, and target file.

  \item \textbf{Storage and access constraint:} Dataset and prediction files are
  handled through accessible links to reduce server storage usage. 

 \item \textbf{Scoring constraint:} The platform uses Scikit-learn metrics for
scoring. Therefore, the selected metric must be supported by Scikit-learn and
must match the benchmark problem type.
\end{itemize}


\subsection{Module Summary}
\noindent The Benchmark Platform module provides a controlled environment for
benchmark creation, prediction submission, validation, scoring, and leaderboard
ranking. It also supports a learning cycle where users compare their results
with others, review submitted repository links, improve their techniques, and
submit updated predictions. It keeps the web management logic in the backend and
frontend, while the metric calculation logic is handled by Python scoring
scripts.

\chapter{System Testing and Verification}
This chapter explains how the project was tested to make sure that the main
parts work correctly. The testing was not done in one way only, because each
part of the project has a different output. The main purpose of testing was to make sure that each part can receive the
input, apply the required steps, save the needed files, and return the expected
output without errors.

\section{Testing Setup}

The testing was done mainly in a Python development environment. The main tools
used during testing were Python, Pytest, Pandas, NumPy, Scikit-learn, PyTorch,
Matplotlib, and the other libraries used in the implementation. These tools were
used to test data preprocessing, model training, prediction, evaluation, and
report generation.

For the benchmark platform, the testing also included the web and backend tools.
Node.js, Express, React, MongoDB, and Python scoring scripts were checked to make
sure that submissions could be uploaded, processed, scored, and displayed
correctly. This helped verify that the platform works as a complete web-based
system, not only as separate frontend and backend files.

Some tests were written and executed automatically using Pytest. These tests
were useful for checking repeated cases quickly and making sure that changes in
the code did not break existing functionality. Other parts were tested manually,
especially visual outputs such as generated reports, graphs, benchmark pages,
and deployment responses. Manual testing was also used when the expected output
needed to be inspected by the user.

\section{Testing Plan and Strategy}

The testing plan was based on testing the project step by step. First, each
module was tested separately to make sure that its main function worked
correctly. For example, the preprocessing module was tested by checking the
generated cleaned data and reports, while the machine learning modules were
tested by checking predictions, accuracy values, and saved models.

After testing individual modules, integration testing was performed. In this
stage, the output of one module was passed to another module to check that the
modules could work together. For example, preprocessing output was passed to
machine learning models, generated reports were opened and inspected, and
benchmark submissions were checked after scoring.

The testing strategy also included comparing actual outputs with expected
outputs. For machine learning parts, this included checking that the model
produced valid predictions and reasonable metrics. For the benchmark platform,
this included checking that submitted files were scored correctly and that the
leaderboard was updated. For saved files and reports, the test checked that the
files were created successfully and could be opened again.

Overall, the testing strategy focused on three main goals: checking that each
module works alone, checking that connected modules work together, and checking
that the final outputs are correct and usable.

\subsection{Module Testing}
\subsubsection{Auto Preprocessing Module}
Most of the preprocessing functions were tested using unit tests. These tests
were used to check validation rules, artifact saving,and feature transformation. Real datasets were also used to test the system in a
more practical way. These datasets were collected from Kaggle for tabular,
text, and image data.
\paragraph{How the Auto Preprocessing testing works}

The Auto Preprocessing testing was done as a practical full-flow test. The idea
was to run preprocessing on real datasets, then pass the processed output to a
model. This means the test does not only check that preprocessing runs without
errors, but also checks that the returned data can actually be used for
training, validation, and prediction.

\noindent For tabular datasets, the test starts by loading each dataset with its
target column and problem type. The preprocessing flow splits the data, fits the
needed encoders and scalers, transforms the features and target, applies feature
selection, and saves the preprocessing artifacts. After that, a machine learning
model is trained on the processed training data and evaluated on the processed
validation data. Classification datasets are evaluated using Macro F1-score,
and regression datasets are evaluated using the R-squared score. The same
datasets are also processed by a baseline automated cleaning tool, so the final
scores can be compared and the Accelera output can be checked against an
existing preprocessing approach.

\noindent For text datasets, the test checks that raw text can be converted into
features that a classifier can understand. The preprocessing flow receives the
text column and target column, cleans the text, tokenizes it, applies TF-IDF,
encodes the labels, and returns processed training and validation data. A
classifier is then trained on the transformed text features and evaluated using
Macro F1-score. This confirms that the text preprocessing output is numeric,
consistent, and usable for a normal machine learning model.

\noindent For image classification, the test checks the full image preparation
flow. The preprocessing reads images from class folders, validates image files,
skips corrupted images, creates class-to-label mappings, resizes the images,
applies augmentation when enabled, and returns PyTorch DataLoaders. A neural
network model is then trained using these loaders. The inference path is also
checked by loading the saved model and applying the testing preprocessing to new
images. This verifies that the saved image size and class mapping can be reused
during testing, not only during training.

\noindent The main concept in all three tests is to check preprocessing through
the next step in the machine learning pipeline. If the tabular arrays, TF-IDF
matrices, labels, image tensors, or saved artifacts were wrong, the model
training or evaluation step would fail or give invalid results.

\end{table}
\begin{table}[H]
\centering
\caption{Models and Evaluation Metrics Used in Module Testing}
\label{tab:module_testing_models}

\noindent These results show that the preprocessing output can be used by different
models after transformation. The tabular results were strong in many datasets,
especially the larger classification datasets and the car price regression
dataset. The lower scores on datasets such as  TestReviews show
that preprocessing alone does not solve every modeling problem. Some datasets
still need better features, model tuning, or more suitable models.

\subsubsection{Accelera Pipe Module}

Accelera Pipe was tested at node, graph, and persistence levels. The automated
tests construct preprocessing, model, prediction, metric, merge, and branch
nodes, then execute the resulting graphs in both serial and parallel modes.
The tests compare transformed data, predictions, probabilities, and metric
values with the corresponding Scikit-learn operations. They also exercise
supervised and unsupervised workflows, mixed node types, and the
\texttt{all}, \texttt{max}, \texttt{min}, and custom path-selection strategies.

Table~\ref{tab:accelera-pipe-module-tests} organizes these tests according to
the graph behavior being verified. Each test builds a small graph with a known
expected result, executes it through the same public pipeline interface used by
applications, and compares the returned values or graph state with that
expectation.

\begin{table}[H]
\centering
\acceleraTableStyle
\caption{Accelera Pipe module-testing coverage.}
\label{tab:accelera-pipe-module-tests}
\begin{tabularx}{\textwidth}{>{\raggedright\arraybackslash}p{0.22\textwidth}
  >{\raggedright\arraybackslash}X >{\raggedright\arraybackslash}X}
\toprule
\textbf{Test area} & \textbf{Test procedure} & \textbf{Verified result} \\
\midrule
Node chains & Execute single and multiple preprocessors followed by model,
prediction, and metric nodes. & Transformed values, predictions, probabilities,
and metrics agree with the reference operations. \\
\midrule
Branches and merges & Build preprocessing and model branches, merge predictions,
and exercise hard voting and single-model merges. & Every branch receives the
correct input and the merge returns the expected combined output. \\
\midrule
Path selection & Execute the same candidate graph with \texttt{all},
\texttt{max}, \texttt{min}, and custom selectors. & Returned and retained paths
follow the requested metric-selection rule. \\
\midrule
Data lifetime & Share duplicate first branch nodes and execute multiple consumers
of one transformed array. & Shared work is not repeated, and intermediate data
is released only after its final consumer completes. \\
\midrule
Persistence and reuse & Save and reload unexecuted pipelines and fitted executed
graphs, including custom and lambda preprocessors. & Training graphs remain
trainable, while loaded executed graphs reproduce inference results. \\
\midrule
Failure handling & Supply invalid metrics, incompatible inputs, and unsupported
node behavior. & The pipeline raises an explicit error associated with the node
that failed instead of returning a partial result. \\
\bottomrule
\end{tabularx}
\end{table}

\noindent Branch-specific tests verify that equivalent first preprocessing
nodes are shared without incorrectly merging later nodes whose inputs differ.
Consumer-count tests ensure that an intermediate array remains available until
its final dependent node finishes and is then released. Save-and-load tests
train a graph, serialize the fitted executed graph, reload it, and confirm that
inference produces the same result. Invalid model, metric, and graph inputs are
also tested to confirm that failures are reported at the responsible node. The
tests passed for the supported graph structures, showing that graph execution,
branch sharing, intermediate lifetime management, and fitted-state persistence
behave consistently.

\noindent Testing both serial and parallel graph execution is important because
the mathematical result should not depend on scheduling. The same prediction
tests are therefore executed with parallel execution enabled and disabled.
Unsupervised tests additionally verify that clustering models can execute
without a supervised target, while mixed-type tests confirm that feature
selection and preprocessing objects can be chained before fitting. Together,
these tests cover the graph from construction to repeated inference rather than
checking only individual Python wrapper methods.

\subsubsection{Code Parallelizer Module}

The Code Parallelizer was tested through its complete source-to-execution
path. Test inputs include C/C++ files, C/C++ source strings, Python functions,
and custom transformer methods. The tests first verify input dispatch and loop
extraction, then check feature collection, classifier or rule-based decisions,
OpenMP pragma generation, native compilation, and callable loading. Temporary
output paths are supplied explicitly so file generation is isolated and can be
verified on Linux and Windows.

Table~\ref{tab:parallelizer-module-tests} summarizes the principal testing
layers. The test suite separates source transformation from native execution so
that a failure can be located in input handling, loop analysis, pragma
generation, conversion, compilation, or runtime behavior.

\acceleraTableStyle
\begin{longtable}{>{\raggedright\arraybackslash}p{0.20\textwidth}
  >{\raggedright\arraybackslash}p{0.35\textwidth}
  >{\raggedright\arraybackslash}p{0.35\textwidth}}
\caption{Code Parallelizer module-testing coverage.}
\label{tab:parallelizer-module-tests}\\
\toprule
\textbf{Test area} & \textbf{Test procedure} & \textbf{Verified result} \\
\midrule
\endfirsthead
\caption[]{Code Parallelizer module-testing coverage (continued).}\\
\toprule
\textbf{Test area} & \textbf{Test procedure} & \textbf{Verified result} \\
\midrule
\endhead
\midrule
\multicolumn{3}{r}{\small Continued on the next page}\\
\endfoot
\bottomrule
\endlastfoot
Input modes & Provide C/C++ files, source strings, Python functions, and custom
transformer methods. & Each input reaches the correct file-based or in-memory
processing path. \\
\midrule
Loop extraction & Analyze files containing one loop, multiple loops, nested
loops, or no loops and serialize extracted features. & Loop boundaries and
feature records match the source, including the empty-input case. \\
\midrule
Decision safety & Test classifier responses, obvious independent writes,
reductions, and unavailable classifier service. & Deterministic rules validate
or replace predictions and provide a rule-based fallback. \\
\midrule
Pragma generation & Generate directives for independent loops, reductions, and
nested structures. & Valid directives are inserted once; unsafe reductions and
redundant inner-loop directives are rejected. \\
\midrule
Python conversion & Convert assignments, augmented assignments, powers,
subscripts, ranges, function definitions, and supported helper calls. & The
generated C++ preserves the supported Python operation and parameter names. \\
\midrule
Native callable path & Compile supported NumPy loops, nested helpers, referenced
helpers, and custom-transformer \texttt{transform} methods. & The loaded PyBind11
callable matches the original Python output and remains pickleable. \\
\midrule
Fallback and cache & Pass unsupported syntax and repeat a supported compilation.
& Unsupported functions retain their Python implementation, while repeated
supported functions reuse cached artifacts. \\
\end{longtable}

\noindent The generated code is inspected for supported directives such as
\texttt{parallel for} and reductions. Nested-loop tests confirm that selecting
an outer loop does not also insert an unsafe directive into its inner loop.
Callable tests compare the compiled result with the original Python result,
while unsupported syntax is expected to return the original callable through
the conservative fallback path. Repeated compilation tests also verify cache
reuse. These checks showed that supported loops are transformed and executed
correctly, whereas unsupported or unsafe cases remain sequential instead of
silently changing program behavior.

\noindent Correctness is checked before performance. For compiled Python
callables, the test invokes both the original function and the generated native
callable with equivalent arrays and compares their outputs. File-based tests
also confirm that the requested output file is created at the supplied path,
which prevents tests and concurrent runs from writing to an implicit shared
location. The fallback tests are equally important: a construct outside the
converter subset must remain executable in Python rather than producing invalid
C++ or an incorrect OpenMP transformation.

\subsubsection{AutoML Module}

The AutoML experiments compare Accelera AutoML with Auto-sklearn, FLAML, and
TPOT on supervised tabular classification and regression tasks. The evaluation
asks two practical questions: whether the systems attain comparable predictive
quality, and how much wall-clock time they require to do so. Classification is
evaluated primarily by accuracy, while regression is evaluated by the
coefficient of determination ($R^2$), root mean squared error (RMSE), and mean
absolute error (MAE). Runtime is reported in seconds for both task types.

The classification experiments were executed through the open-source
AutoMLBenchmark (AMLB) runner, available at
\url{https://github.com/openml/automlbenchmark.git}. They use the
\modulepath{flaml\_paper\_39} classification suite and the
\modulepath{flaml\_paper\_1h} constraint. The reported classification
evaluation covers 12 datasets. The regression benchmark was built from the
Penn Machine Learning Benchmarks (PMLB) repository, available at
\url{https://github.com/EpistasisLab/pmlb.git}, and was also executed through
AMLB. The reported regression evaluation covers 10 datasets and uses the
\modulepath{pmlb\_regression} suite with the \modulepath{pmlb\_30m}
constraint.

\noindent All systems were run through the same benchmark interface. The recorded setup
used one fold and one allocated core per task. The regression time budget was
1,800 seconds per dataset; the classification constraint allowed up to one
hour.

\newcommand{\automlcomparativeresults}{%
\subsection{AutoML Module}
\paragraph{Comparative Results: Classification}
Table~\ref{tab:automl_classification_results} summarizes the classification
results stored with the project. Auto-sklearn obtained the highest mean
accuracy at 0.8842. Accelera reached a mean accuracy of 0.8791, a difference of
about 0.0051, while recording the lowest mean duration at 1,201.7 seconds. Its
mean runtime was approximately one third of the runtimes recorded for the other
three systems. Accelera also had the largest number of recorded speed wins.

recorded the lowest mean runtime at 892.64 seconds, compared with
1,820--1,864 seconds for the competing systems. Accelera also achieved six
speed wins, the most of any evaluated system.

\paragraph{Result Reproduction}
The benchmark experiments can be reproduced using the instructions provided in
the project repository. These instructions describe the required environment
setup and the commands used to rerun the classification and regression
benchmarks. The generated figures, CSV summaries, and raw benchmark outputs are
stored with the project results, allowing the reported values to be checked
against future reruns. The reproduction guide is available at
\href{https://github.com/Mohamed-Ashraf273/accelera/blob/main/accelera/src/accelera_automl/benchmark_results/setup/reproduce_results.md}
{\texttt{setup/reproduce\_results.md}}.

\paragraph{AutoML Result Summary}
Across the stored experiments, Accelera achieved predictive results close to
the strongest competing framework while requiring markedly less mean runtime.
The classification results show a small accuracy difference relative to
Auto-sklearn, and the regression results show a modest $R^2$ difference
relative to Auto-sklearn, accompanied in both cases by substantial runtime
reductions. The complete stored artifacts and reproduction instructions are
retained with the implementation so that the reported measurements can be
inspected and rerun.
}

\subsubsection{Deployment Module}
\input{sections/results-deployment}

\subsubsection{Accelera Pipeline Reporting Module}

The pipeline reporting part was tested by generating reports from executed
pipeline results. The test checks that the report can read the serialized graph
file, create the graph image, detect branches, show the selected path, and
display metric results in the correct format.

Different metric result types were checked, such as single numbers, arrays,
dictionaries, strings, tuples, and plotted figures. The expected output was a
\texttt{report.html} file with readable tables and figures. The graph image was
also checked manually to make sure that selected nodes and normal nodes were
shown using different colors.

\subsubsection{Benchmark Platform Module}

The Benchmark Platform module was evaluated using manual functional testing to check its main features from the user side. The testing focused on creating benchmark tasks, validating dataset links, submitting prediction files, calculating scores, and displaying the results in the leaderboard.

\noindent The leaderboard was tested by submitting different results and checking whether they were ordered correctly based on the selected evaluation metric. This included metrics where higher values are better and metrics where lower values are better.

\noindent The scoring process was also tested manually by using different target and prediction files. The platform was checked to make sure it rejects invalid submissions, such as files with different row counts or missing target columns. These tests showed that the benchmark platform can handle result submission, calculate scores, validate files, and display leaderboard results correctly.

\subsection{Integration Testing}

Integration testing was done by connecting the output of one part with another
part. For Auto Preprocessing, the transformed data was passed to machine
learning models to check that the output was usable. For pipeline reporting,
the report was generated after graph serialization and metric calculation. For
benchmarking, the backend called the Python scoring script and then saved the
returned score in MongoDB.

These checks were important because a function can pass a unit test but still
fail when used with the rest of the system.

\section{Testing Schedule}

Testing was performed continuously during implementation and then repeated at
module, integration, and project-release milestones. Table~\ref{tab:project-testing-schedule}
summarizes the schedule followed across the complete project. A stage was not
treated as complete until its automated checks passed and its generated outputs
had been inspected where manual verification was required.

\acceleraTableStyle
\begin{longtable}{>{\raggedright\arraybackslash}p{0.18\textwidth}
  >{\raggedright\arraybackslash}p{0.25\textwidth}
  >{\raggedright\arraybackslash}p{0.47\textwidth}}
\caption{Project-wide testing schedule.}
\label{tab:project-testing-schedule}\\
\toprule
\textbf{Stage} & \textbf{Modules emphasized} & \textbf{Testing activity} \\
\midrule
\endfirsthead
\caption[]{Project-wide testing schedule (continued).}\\
\toprule
\textbf{Stage} & \textbf{Modules emphasized} & \textbf{Testing activity} \\
\midrule
\endhead
\midrule
\multicolumn{3}{r}{\small Continued on the next page}\\
\endfoot
\bottomrule
\endlastfoot
During feature implementation & All modules & Run focused unit tests after each
function, class, graph node, API route, or compiler rule is introduced or
modified. \\
\midrule
Module stabilization & Auto Preprocessing and AutoML & Validate cleaning,
transformation, artifact persistence, model search, metrics, time limits, and
classification and regression behavior on representative datasets. \\
\midrule
Native-system stabilization & Accelera Pipe and Code Parallelizer & Test serial
and parallel graphs, branch behavior, memory lifetime, persistence, source
conversion, loop analysis, OpenMP generation, compilation, and fallback. \\
\midrule
Service stabilization & Benchmark Platform and Deployment & Verify frontend and
backend workflows, database operations, submission scoring, API validation,
telemetry, model versioning, and local or remote deployment command generation. \\
\midrule
Integration milestone & Connected project workflow & Pass preprocessing outputs
to training and AutoML, execute and save graphs, compile custom preprocessing,
generate reports, score submissions, and load trained artifacts for serving. \\
\midrule
Regression and portability & Complete repository & Run the full Pytest and
pre-commit suites after merges, rebuild native bindings, and check relevant
Linux and Windows paths. \\
\midrule
Final experimental evaluation & All evaluated modules & Freeze datasets and
configurations, repeat measurements, verify output equivalence, produce tables
and figures, and compare with the selected previous systems. \\
\end{longtable}

\noindent Manual verification followed the automated schedule for outputs that
cannot be judged fully by assertions. This included preprocessing and pipeline
reports, graph diagrams, benchmark pages, deployment responses, and generated
figures. Comparative experiments were performed only after the corresponding
module and integration tests were stable, which reduced the risk of reporting
performance measurements for an incorrect workflow.

\section{Comparative Results to Previous Work}
\input{sections/results-modhamed}
\subsection{Auto Preprocessing}
Auto Preprocessing was compared with AutoCleanML to check the performance of the proposed preprocessing approach. The comparison was done using several tabular datasets collected from Kaggle. The same datasets and the same machine learning models were used for both tools.
The comparison was applied only on tabular datasets because AutoCleanML mainly works with tabular data. For classification datasets, the Macro F1-score was used as the evaluation metric. For regression datasets, the R-squared score was used to measure how well the model predicts the target values.
After preprocessing the datasets, the output data was tested using four machine learning models. For classification problems, Logistic Regression, Random Forest, Decision Tree, and KNN were used. For regression problems, Linear Regression, Random Forest, Decision Tree, and KNN were used. This helped test the preprocessing output with different model types, not only one model.
The results were saved and plotted as comparison graphs between Accelera and AutoCleanML. These graphs show the score of each tool on the selected datasets. The purpose of this comparison was to check whether Accelera can produce preprocessing results that are close to, or better than, an existing preprocessing tool.

\noindent From the classification and regression results, Accelera preprocessing achieved
competitive performance compared to AutoCleanML. The comparison was tested using
different types of models, including linear models, tree based models, Random
Forest, and KNN. In many cases, the results of Accelera were very close to
AutoCleanML, and in some cases Accelera achieved better scores.

\noindent These results show that the implemented preprocessing steps in Accelera are able
to prepare data in a valid way for different machine learning techniques. The
performance was not limited to one specific model type, but appeared across
several models and datasets. This means that Accelera successfully reached
results close to an existing automated preprocessing tool, while still using its
own preprocessing approach.

\noindent However, the results also show that no preprocessing tool gives the best score
in all cases. The final performance can still depend on the dataset, the problem
type, and the model used after preprocessing.

\automlcomparativeresults

\chapter{Conclusions and Future Work}
\section{Faced Challenges}
The main implementation challenges were preserving correctness while sharing
branch computations, releasing intermediate arrays only after their final
consumer, and limiting generated OpenMP code to loops that pass dependency and
conversion checks. The experiments also identify memory usage during
branch-level parallel execution as the main remaining graph-backend challenge.

\section{Gained Experience}
Developing Accelera provided practical experience in designing a modular
machine-learning system whose components exchange data, models, reports, and
deployment artifacts through explicit interfaces. The team gained experience
with tabular, text, and image preprocessing; constrained AutoML search;
reproducible model evaluation; and the distinction between training state and
the fitted state required for inference.

\noindent The project also connected Python development with systems and
compiler engineering. Implementing the graph backend, Python--C++ bindings,
and Code Parallelizer required work with C++, PyBind11, CMake, Clang ASTs,
OpenMP, native-module compilation, and cross-platform build behavior. This work
demonstrated how data ownership, dependency analysis, caching, conservative
fallback, and serialization directly affect correctness, memory use, and
latency.

\noindent Finally, integrating the React and Express benchmark platform,
MongoDB storage, FastAPI serving, model versioning, telemetry, and container
deployment strengthened experience in end-to-end software delivery. Automated
tests, continuous integration, controlled comparisons, and output-equivalence
checks showed the importance of validating complete workflows rather than only
individual functions. The project therefore developed both machine-learning
knowledge and the engineering discipline needed to make experimental software
repeatable, testable, and usable across environments.

\section{Conclusions}
Accelera provides a connected framework for preparing data, selecting models,
building graph-based machine learning workflows, improving supported custom
code, deploying trained models, and comparing results through a benchmark
platform. The work shows that the project is not only a collection of separate
tools, but a workflow where each module can support a different stage of the
machine learning process.

\noindent The Auto Preprocessing module reduces repeated manual preparation work
for tabular, text, and image datasets. It saves fitted artifacts so that testing
data can be transformed in the same way as training data. The AutoML module
adds automated model search under time and trial limits, which helps compare
candidate models more systematically. Accelera Pipe represents workflows as
graphs, so shared preprocessing, branches, predictions, and metrics can be
organized more clearly. The Code Parallelizer adds a source-level optimization
path for supported loop-heavy code while keeping conservative safety checks.

\noindent The Deployment module and Benchmark Platform complete the workflow by
supporting model serving, version tracking, submission validation, scoring, and
leaderboard comparison. The testing and experimental results show that the
implemented modules can produce usable outputs, generate reports, evaluate
models, and compare results. Overall, Accelera gives a practical base for a
more organized and reusable machine learning workflow.

\section{Future Work}
Future work should continue reducing graph memory consumption while preserving
parallel branch execution, and should extend safe converter , loop-analysis
coverage without weakening correctness checks, The preprocessing pipeline can be improved by adapting the preprocessing steps based on the selected model type and by adding techniques to handle imbalanced datasets.
In the future, the benchmark platform can be improved by saving uploaded datasets on the server instead of using Google Drive links. It can also allow users to upload code files directly, instead of submitting only links.

\appendix
\renewcommand{\chaptername}{Appendix}
\titleformat{\chapter}[hang]
  {\normalfont\bfseries\fontsize{21}{25}\selectfont\raggedright}
  {Appendix\ \thechapter:}{0.5em}{}
\addtocontents{toc}{\protect\renewcommand{\protect\cftchappresnum}{Appendix~}}
\addtocontents{toc}{\protect\setlength{\protect\cftchapnumwidth}{6.5em}}
\chapter{Development Platforms and Tools}
\section{Software Platform}
The implemented modules use Python, C++, PyBind11, CMake, Clang-based AST
analysis, OpenMP, NumPy, Pandas, Matplotlib, PyTorch, Scikit-learn, Node.js,
React, and MongoDB. The Auto Preprocessing module depends mainly on Python
data-science libraries. The Pipeline Reporting module uses Python, HTML report
generation, Matplotlib, Pandas tables, and Graphviz for graph visualization.
The Benchmark Platform uses a Node.js and Express backend, MongoDB for storing
benchmarks and submissions, React for the frontend, and Python scripts for
dataset validation and metric scoring. The available experiments include Linux
and Windows execution paths.

\chapter{Use Cases}
\section{Auto Preprocessing Use Cases}
The user can use Auto Preprocessing when they have a dataset and want to
prepare it before training a model. For tabular data, the module can clean
missing values, encode categorical columns, scale numerical columns, select
features, save artifacts, and generate a report. For text data, it can clean
sentences, tokenize them, apply TF-IDF, encode labels, and generate a report.
For image data, it can validate images, map class folders to labels, resize
images, apply augmentation, and create DataLoaders.

\section{AutoML Use Cases}
The user can use Accelera AutoML when they need a competitive supervised
tabular model without manually testing many algorithms and hyperparameter
settings. The module supports classification and regression workflows through
\texttt{AutoMLClassifier} and \texttt{AutoMLRegressor}.

\noindent The main use cases are fitting a classifier and obtaining predicted
labels or class probabilities, fitting a regressor and obtaining numeric
predictions, restricting the search to an allowed subset of model families,
selecting the evaluation metric and cross-validation folds, limiting the total
search budget or per-trial timeout, enabling or disabling meta-learning warm
starts and ensemble construction, and inspecting the best score, leaderboard,
and complete run history.

\section{Pipeline Reporting Use Cases}
The user can use the pipeline report after running an Accelera pipeline. The
report helps show the graph structure, branches, selected path, and metric
results. This is useful when the pipeline contains more than one branch and the
user wants to understand which path was selected.

\section{Benchmark Platform Use Cases}
The benchmark platform can be used when different users or models need to be
compared on the same task. A benchmark creator defines the dataset, test file,
target column, and metric. Users then submit prediction files, and the platform
calculates the score and displays the leaderboard.

\renewcommand{\lstlistingname}{Code Example}
\chapter{User Guide}
\section{Using Tabular Auto Preprocessing}
To use tabular preprocessing, the user first loads the dataset as a Pandas
DataFrame. Then the user gives the target column, problem type, and output
folder. The output folder is important because the module saves the fitted
preprocessing objects and the HTML report inside it.

\begin{lstlisting}[style=code,caption={Calling tabular training preprocessing}]
import pandas as pd
from accelera.src.auto_preprocessing.core.classical_training_preprocessing import (
    ClassicalTrainingPreprocessing,
)

df = pd.read_csv("dataset.csv")

preprocessor = ClassicalTrainingPreprocessing(
    df=df,
    target_col="target",
    problem_type="classification",
    folder_path="outputs/tabular_run",
    val_size=0.2,
    random_state=42,
    is_report=True,
)

X_train, y_train, X_val, y_val = preprocessor.common_preprocessing()
\end{lstlisting}

For regression, the user only changes the problem type:

\begin{lstlisting}[style=code,caption={Changing tabular preprocessing to regression}]
preprocessor = ClassicalTrainingPreprocessing(
    df=df,
    target_col="price",
    problem_type="regression",
    folder_path="outputs/regression_run",
)
\end{lstlisting}

After training preprocessing, the same folder should be used for test data.
This makes the test data use the saved training transformers instead of fitting
new ones.

\begin{lstlisting}[style=code,caption={Calling tabular testing preprocessing}]
import pandas as pd
from accelera.src.auto_preprocessing.core.classical_testing_preprocessing import (
    ClassicalTestingPreprocessing,
)

test_df = pd.read_csv("test.csv")

test_preprocessor = ClassicalTestingPreprocessing(
    df=test_df,
    folder_path="outputs/tabular_run",
)

X_test, y_test = test_preprocessor.common_preprocessing()
\end{lstlisting}

\section{Using Text Auto Preprocessing}
For text preprocessing, the user provides the text column and the target
column. The text column must contain text values, and the target is used for
classification.

\begin{lstlisting}[style=code,caption={Calling text training preprocessing}]
import pandas as pd
from accelera.src.auto_preprocessing.core.text_training_preprocessing import (
    TextTrainingPreprocessing,
)

df = pd.read_csv("reviews.csv")

text_preprocessor = TextTrainingPreprocessing(
    df=df,
    target_col="label",
    text_col="review",
    folder_path="outputs/text_run",
    tfidf_max_features=500,
    is_report=True,
)

X_train, y_train, X_val, y_val = text_preprocessor.common_preprocessing()
\end{lstlisting}

For testing text data, the user again passes the same folder path. If the test
data does not contain the target column, the class returns the transformed text
features and \texttt{None} for the labels.

\begin{lstlisting}[style=code,caption={Calling text testing preprocessing}]
from accelera.src.auto_preprocessing.core.text_testing_preprocesing import (
    TextTestingPreprocessing,
)

test_text = pd.read_csv("reviews_test.csv")

text_test_preprocessor = TextTestingPreprocessing(
    df=test_text,
    folder_path="outputs/text_run",
)

X_test, y_test = text_test_preprocessor.common_preprocessing()
\end{lstlisting}

\section{Using Image Auto Preprocessing}
For image preprocessing, the dataset should be arranged in class folders. For
example:

\begin{verbatim}
Training/
  Cats/
  Dogs/
\end{verbatim}

The user passes the training folder and the output folder. If a validation
folder already exists, it can be passed also. If not, the module can split the
training folder.

\begin{lstlisting}[style=code,caption={Calling image training preprocessing}]
from accelera.src.auto_preprocessing.core.classification_image_training_preprocessing import (
    ClassificationImageTrainingPreprocessing,
)

image_preprocessor = ClassificationImageTrainingPreprocessing(
    training_folder_images="data/PetImages/Training",
    folder_path="outputs/image_run",
    split_training=True,
    val_size=0.2,
    images_size=(224, 224),
    batch_size=16,
    augment=True,
)

train_loader, val_loader = image_preprocessor.common_preprocessing()
\end{lstlisting}

For image testing, the user provides a list of image paths. If class names are
available, they can be passed too. If not, the loader can still be used for
prediction.

\begin{lstlisting}[style=code,caption={Calling image testing preprocessing}]
from accelera.src.auto_preprocessing.core.classification_image_testing_preprocessing import (
    ClassificationImageTestingPreprocessing,
)

image_paths = ["test/cat1.jpg", "test/dog1.jpg"]
class_names = ["Cats", "Dogs"]

image_test_preprocessor = ClassificationImageTestingPreprocessing(
    image_paths=image_paths,
    image_class_names=class_names,
    folder_path="outputs/image_run",
    batch_size=4,
)

test_loader, invalid_images = image_test_preprocessor.common_preprocessing()
\end{lstlisting}

\section{Reading Auto Preprocessing Reports}
After running preprocessing with \texttt{is\_report=True}, the user opens the
generated \texttt{report.html} file from the output folder. The report shows
the dataset overview, missing values, duplicate rows, dropped columns, graphs,
preprocessing decisions, selected features, and processed data samples.

\section{Using Accelera AutoML}
Accelera AutoML follows a Scikit-learn-style workflow. The user imports either
\texttt{AutoMLClassifier} or \texttt{AutoMLRegressor}, creates an estimator
with the required search settings, fits it on the training data, and then uses
the fitted model for prediction, scoring, or leaderboard inspection.

\begin{lstlisting}[style=code,caption={Basic AutoML classification workflow}]
from accelera.src.accelera_automl import AutoMLClassifier

model = AutoMLClassifier(
    time_budget=1800,
    cv=5,
    scoring="accuracy",
    use_ensemble=True,
    random_state=42,
)

model.fit(X_train, y_train)

predictions = model.predict(X_test)
score = model.score(X_test, y_test)
leaderboard = model.return_leaderboard()
\end{lstlisting}

\noindent In this example, the search is limited to 1,800 seconds, five-fold
cross-validation is used, and accuracy is selected as the optimization metric.
After fitting, the model can generate predictions, evaluate performance on test
data, and return the stored leaderboard for inspection.

\begin{lstlisting}[style=code,caption={Basic AutoML regression workflow}]
from accelera.src.accelera_automl import AutoMLRegressor

model = AutoMLRegressor(
    time_budget=1800,
    cv=5,
    scoring="r2",
    n_trials=50,
    use_meta_learning=True,
    random_state=42,
)

model.fit(X_train, y_train)

predictions = model.predict(X_test)
r2_score = model.score(X_test, y_test)
leaderboard = model.return_leaderboard(top_n=5)
\end{lstlisting}

\noindent The same public workflow is used for regression. The main difference
is that the selected score is a regression metric, such as $R^2$, and the
predictions are numeric values instead of class labels.

\section{Using Pipeline Reports}
To generate a pipeline report, the pipeline graph should be serialized first.
Then the user passes the XML graph path, output folder, and metric results to
\texttt{GraphReport}.

\begin{lstlisting}[style=code,caption={Calling the pipeline graph report}]
from accelera.src.accelera_pipe.wrappers.graph_report import GraphReport

report = GraphReport(
    folderpath="outputs/pipeline_report",
    xmlpath="outputs/pipeline_graph.xml",
    results=metric_results,
)

report.execute()
\end{lstlisting}

The output is a \texttt{report.html} file. It contains the graph image, branch
tables, selected branch, and metric summary.

\section{Using the Benchmark Platform}
The benchmark platform is used from the web interface. The benchmark creator
enters the title, problem type, target column, dataset link, test-set link,
target-label link, and evaluation metric. A user submits a prediction file link
and repository link. The backend calls the Python scoring script, saves the
score, and displays the result in the leaderboard.

The prediction file must contain the target column name expected by the
benchmark. Also, the number of rows in the prediction file must match the
number of rows in the target-label file.

\iffalse
\chapter{Feasibility Study}

\section{AutoML Feasibility}
The AutoML module is technically feasible because it is built on established
Python machine-learning libraries such as Scikit-learn and is integrated within
the existing Accelera package structure. The implementation does not require
specialized hardware and can run on standard CPU-based environments, although
larger search budgets and larger datasets require more computation time.

\noindent From an operational perspective, the module is feasible because it
provides a simple Scikit-learn-style interface. Users can create an AutoML
classifier or regressor, call \texttt{fit}, and then use standard methods such
as \texttt{predict}, \texttt{score}, and \texttt{return\_leaderboard}. This
makes the system suitable for users who want automated model selection without
manually testing many algorithms and hyperparameter settings.

\noindent The project is also economically feasible because it mainly depends on
open-source software tools and does not require paid cloud services or
commercial AutoML platforms. Reusing the same module across multiple
classification and regression tasks can reduce the manual effort needed for
model selection, tuning, and comparison.

\noindent The main limitation is computational cost. AutoML search can be
time-consuming, especially when many models, folds, and trials are evaluated.
However, this cost is controlled through time budgets, per-trial timeouts,
multi-fidelity evaluation, and configurable search settings. Therefore, the
module is feasible as a practical AutoML extension for the Accelera framework.

ng module for tabular, text, and image
  datasets.

  \item Support data cleaning, missing-value handling, categorical encoding,
  robust scaling, TF-IDF text representation, image preparation, feature
  selection, and report generation.

  \item Implement AutoML classifiers and regressors that search across supported
  model configurations using scoring metrics, cross-validation, time budgets,
  trial budgets, and optional ensemble construction.

  \item Provide a graph-based pipeline module that represents workflow steps as
  executable graph nodes rather than only as a sequential list.

  \item Support pipeline operations such as preprocessing, model fitting,
  prediction, metric evaluation, branching, merging, and executed-graph reuse.

  \item Integrate custom preprocessing functions with the parallelizer so that
  supported loop-heavy functions can be optimized automatically.

  \item Implement a conservative code parallelization path that uses C++,
  OpenMP, loop-feature extraction, classification, rule-based safety checks, and
  native compilation.

  \item Provide reporting tools that make preprocessing outputs, model metrics,
  and pipeline structure more visible to the user.

  \item Build a benchmark platform prototype for datasets, submissions, users,
  metrics, and leaderboard-based comparison.
\end{enumerate}

\medskip
\noindent The problem addressed by the project can be stated as follows:

\begin{quote}
Machine-learning workflows in Python are easy to write but become difficult to
organize, repeat, compare, and optimize when they include many preprocessing
steps, model alternatives, custom functions, and evaluation paths. Accelera aims
to preserve Python usability while adding automated preprocessing, budgeted
model search, graph-based execution, safe loop-level acceleration, and
benchmark-oriented evaluation.
\end{quote}

\medskip
\noindent This problem has two sides. The first side is \textbf{workflow complexity}. As
the number of preprocessing steps, branches, and candidate models increases, the
workflow becomes harder to manage manually. The second side is
\textbf{execution performance}. Even if the workflow is correct, it may repeat
shared work or spend too much time in slow Python loops. Accelera addresses both
sides by combining high-level automation with lower-level execution support.

\section{Project Outcomes}

The project outcomes are the implemented modules and the integration between
them. Each module provides a different part of the final system.

\subsection*{Outcome 1: Automated preprocessing}

The \texttt{auto\_preprocessing} module prepares datasets for machine-learning
models. For tabular data, it handles duplicates, missing values, column-type
detection, categorical encoding, robust scaling, target processing, feature
selection, and report generation. For text data, it applies cleaning,
tokenization, stemming, TF-IDF vectorization, and label encoding. For image
data, it supports classification and segmentation preparation. The outcome is a
set of processed arrays, saved preprocessors, selected features, and reports.

\subsection*{Outcome 2: AutoML model search}

The \texttt{accelera\_automl} module provides estimator-style AutoML classes for
classification and regression. It searches candidate configurations, evaluates
them with cross-validation, supports warm-starting from dataset metafeatures,
and can build voting or stacked ensembles. The outcome is a fitted final model,
best score, leaderboard, and run history.

\subsection*{Outcome 3: Graph-based pipeline execution}

The \texttt{accelera\_pipe} module provides a graph-based pipeline interface.
It represents preprocessing, model, prediction, metric, branch, and merge
operations as graph nodes. The output of a pipeline run includes predictions and
an \texttt{ExecutedGraph}, which stores the selected fitted path for later use.
This outcome gives the project its workflow-execution layer.

\subsection*{Outcome 4: Source-level parallelization}

The \texttt{parallelizer} module accelerates supported loop-heavy code. It can
process C/C++ code, supported Python code, custom Python functions, and
transformer methods. It extracts loops, computes features, classifies
parallelization opportunities, applies safety rules, inserts OpenMP pragmas, and
compiles native Python-callable extensions when possible. If any step fails, it
falls back to the original Python implementation.

\subsection*{Outcome 5: Pipeline and parallelizer integration}

One of the most important outcomes is the connection between
\texttt{accelera\_pipe} and the \texttt{parallelizer}. When a preprocessing node
contains a supported custom Python function, the pipeline attempts to optimize
it before adding it to the graph. This allows the system to combine graph-level
workflow execution with code-level acceleration.

\subsection*{Outcome 6: Reporting and benchmark support}

The project includes reports for preprocessing, model metrics, and graph
visualization. It also includes a benchmark platform prototype with backend and
frontend components for datasets, metrics, users, submissions, and leaderboards.
These outcomes improve visibility and comparison.

\begin{table}[!htbp]
\centering
\acceleraTableStyle
\caption{Summary of project outcomes.}
\label{tab:intro-outcomes}
\begin{tabular}{p{0.30\textwidth}p{0.60\textwidth}}
\toprule
\textbf{Outcome} & \textbf{Description} \\
\midrule

Automated preprocessing &
The project provides reusable preprocessing support for tabular, text, and image
data, with generated reports and saved preprocessing artifacts. \\
\midrule

Automated model selection &
The AutoML module helps search and compare candidate classification and
regression models under time and trial constraints. \\
\midrule

Graph-based pipeline execution &
Accelera Pipe represents machine-learning workflows as executable graphs,
supporting preprocessing, modelling, prediction, metrics, branching, and merging. \\
\midrule

Code-level acceleration &
The Parallelizer attempts to optimize supported loop-heavy Python/C++ code using
static analysis, safety checks, OpenMP pragmas, compilation, and caching. \\
\midrule

Benchmarking support &
The Benchmark Platform provides a foundation for dataset-based evaluation,
prediction submission, metric calculation, and leaderboard-style comparison. \\

\bottomrule
\end{tabular}
\end{table}

\section{Document Organization}

The rest of this report is organized into five main parts: Chapter 2 presents the market visibility study, 
Chapter 3 discusses the literature survey and related work, Chapter 4 explains the methodology and system 
design of the main Accelera modules, Chapter 5 presents testing, verification, and experimental results, 
and Chapter 6 concludes the report by summarizing the contributions, limitations, and possible future improvements. 
The appendices include supporting material such as documentation, usage instructions, feasibility details, 
and additional implementation notes.


\chapter{Market Visibility Study}
\label{chap:market-visibility}

This chapter studies the market need for Accelera and compares it with existing
tools that solve related problems. The goal is to show who can benefit from the
project, what alternatives already exist, and why Accelera provides a different
value. The chapter also presents a simplified business case and financial
analysis to clarify how the project could be developed into a product or service.

\medskip
\noindent Accelera targets the data-science workflow as a complete process, not as one
isolated task. Some existing tools focus on preprocessing, others focus on model
selection, and others focus on competition platforms or code acceleration.
Accelera combines these directions through five connected parts:
\texttt{auto\_preprocessing}, \texttt{accelera\_automl},
\texttt{accelera\_pipe}, \texttt{parallelizer}, and the benchmark platform.

\section{Targeted Customers}

\medskip
\noindent The targeted customers for Accelera are users and organizations that build,
test, compare, and optimize machine-learning workflows. These customers can be
divided into several groups, as shown in Table~\ref{tab:target-customers}.

\begin{table}[!htbp]
\centering
\acceleraTableStyle
\caption{Targeted customers for Accelera.}
\label{tab:target-customers}
\begin{tabular}{p{0.25\textwidth}p{0.33\textwidth}p{0.32\textwidth}}
\toprule
\textbf{Customer group} & \textbf{Main need} & \textbf{How Accelera helps} \\
\midrule

Students and beginners &
Need an easier way to build complete machine-learning workflows without writing
every step manually. &
Provides automated preprocessing, model search, reports, and a pipeline
interface that organizes the workflow. \\
\midrule

Data scientists &
Need to prepare data, compare models, and repeat experiments quickly. &
Reduces repetitive preprocessing, supports AutoML search, and gives reports and
leaderboards for comparison. \\
\midrule

Machine-learning engineers &
Need structured, reusable, and optimized workflows that can be inspected and
executed reliably. &
Provides graph-based pipeline execution, executed-graph reuse, and integration
with custom preprocessing acceleration. \\
\midrule

Researchers &
Need to compare approaches, measure results, and experiment with workflow and
code optimization. &
Provides modular components for benchmarking, graph execution, OpenMP
parallelization, and performance evaluation. \\
\midrule

Small teams and startups &
Need a practical toolkit that reduces development time without building an
internal ML platform from scratch. &
Combines preprocessing, AutoML, pipeline execution, reports, and benchmark
support in one project. \\

\bottomrule
\end{tabular}
\end{table}

\medskip
\noindent The most important customer segment is the data-science and machine-learning
user who wants to move from a raw dataset to an evaluated model with less manual
work. For beginners, Accelera provides guidance and automation. For experienced
users, it provides a more structured execution model and opportunities for
acceleration.

\medskip
\noindent Accelera can also be useful in educational environments. In many machine
learning courses, students repeat the same steps: clean the data, encode
features, scale numerical values, split the dataset, train models, calculate
metrics, and compare results. Accelera can make these steps easier to understand
because it provides reports and separates the workflow into clear modules.

\medskip
\noindent For professional users, the value is different. They care more about
reproducibility, execution time, reusable pipeline structure, and fair
comparison. The \texttt{accelera\_pipe} and benchmark modules are especially
useful here because they organize workflow execution and comparison. The
\texttt{parallelizer} adds value when the workflow includes supported custom
preprocessing functions that are slow in Python.

\subsection*{Customer Value Proposition}

The main value proposition of Accelera is that it reduces the gap between
experimentation and structured execution. Instead of forcing the user to choose
between simple scripts and large industrial platforms, Accelera provides a
research-friendly framework with practical automation and optimization features.

\begin{itemize}
  \item \textbf{Less manual work:} preprocessing and model search are partially
  automated.
  \item \textbf{Better organization:} workflows can be represented as graph
  pipelines instead of scattered scripts.
  \item \textbf{Better visibility:} reports and benchmark views help users
  inspect results.
  \item \textbf{Possible speedup:} supported loop-heavy preprocessing code can be
  accelerated through C++ and OpenMP.
  \item \textbf{Fairer comparison:} benchmark components help compare pipelines
  using common datasets and metrics.
\end{itemize}

\section{Market Survey}

Many existing tools provide features related to Accelera, but most of them focus
on only one part of the workflow. Some tools focus on preprocessing, some on
AutoML, some on competitions, and some on code acceleration. Accelera is
different because it tries to connect these ideas into one toolkit.

\begin{table}[!htbp]
\centering
\acceleraTableStyle
\caption{Market comparison summary.}
\label{tab:market-comparison}
\begin{tabular}{p{0.22\textwidth}p{0.28\textwidth}p{0.19\textwidth}p{0.21\textwidth}}
\toprule
\textbf{Tool or platform} & \textbf{Main focus} & \textbf{Limitation} & \textbf{Accelera difference} \\
\midrule

\texttt{scikit-learn} &
Machine-learning models, preprocessing tools, and linear pipelines. &
Pipeline execution is mainly linear and does not focus on graph-based branch
execution or custom code parallelization. &
Adds graph-based pipeline execution and integration with the Parallelizer. \\
\midrule

AutoCleanML &
Automatic data cleaning and preprocessing. &
Focused mainly on data cleaning and does not cover the whole workflow. &
Adds AutoML, graph pipelines, parallelization, reports, and benchmarks. \\
\midrule

Auto-sklearn / TPOT / AutoGluon &
Automated model selection and machine-learning search. &
Focus mainly on model search, not graph execution or source-level code
parallelization. &
Choose Accelera when AutoML must be part of an end-to-end workflow: it combines
model search with preprocessing, graph pipelines, and the Parallelizer. \\
\midrule

Numba / Cython &
Python performance acceleration. &
Require user knowledge of acceleration syntax and do not manage ML workflow
structure. &
Provides an automatic attempt to optimize supported preprocessing functions
inside the pipeline. \\

\bottomrule
\end{tabular}
\end{table}

\subsection{AutoCleanML}

AutoCleanML is a Python tool focused on automating data cleaning and
preprocessing tasks for machine-learning datasets. It is useful because data
preparation is one of the most repetitive and time-consuming parts of the
machine-learning workflow. The tool helps automate common data-cleaning and
preprocessing decisions, which can make dataset preparation easier, especially
for beginners. It also provides reporting features that explain some of the
preprocessing decisions applied during the workflow.

\medskip
\noindent However, AutoCleanML mainly focuses on preprocessing and data cleaning rather
than the complete machine-learning workflow. It does not provide a graph-based
pipeline execution engine, does not target custom code acceleration or OpenMP
parallelization, and does not provide a benchmark platform for comparing full
pipelines.

\medskip
\noindent Compared with AutoCleanML, Accelera includes preprocessing but does not stop
there. It also includes AutoML search, graph pipeline execution, source-level
parallelization, reports, and benchmark support. Therefore, Accelera aims to be
closer to a complete workflow framework.


\subsection{scikit-learn}

\texttt{scikit-learn} is one of the strongest tools in the machine-learning
market. It provides a wide range of models, preprocessing tools, metrics, and a
simple \texttt{Pipeline} abstraction. It is mature, widely used, and integrates
well with the Python data-science ecosystem. Its pipeline interface is simple
and reliable, which makes it useful for building standard machine-learning
workflows.

\medskip
\noindent Despite these strengths, the standard \texttt{scikit-learn} pipeline is mainly
linear. Branch-heavy workflows may require repeated pipelines or external search
utilities, and the library does not focus on native graph scheduling for
pipeline branches. It also does not automatically convert supported custom
preprocessing functions to C++ and OpenMP.

\medskip
\noindent Accelera uses \texttt{scikit-learn} as a strong baseline rather than ignoring
it. The difference is that \texttt{accelera\_pipe} represents the workflow as a
graph and integrates with the Parallelizer for supported custom preprocessing
acceleration. For this reason, \texttt{scikit-learn} is the most important
competitor for \texttt{accelera\_pipe}.


\subsection{AutoML Tools}

AutoML tools such as Auto-sklearn, TPOT, and AutoGluon focus on automating model
selection and hyperparameter search. They are useful because they reduce the
manual effort needed to test many models and configurations. These tools can
often provide strong baseline performance, and some of them also support
ensemble construction.

\medskip
\noindent However, most AutoML tools focus mainly on model search rather than complete
workflow execution. They do not mainly target graph-based pipeline execution,
and they do not automatically accelerate custom preprocessing loops using
OpenMP. Some AutoML systems can also be heavy or complex for beginners.

\medskip
\noindent Accelera includes \texttt{accelera\_automl}, but also connects AutoML with
preprocessing, graph execution, reports, and parallelization. This makes the
AutoML module one part of a larger framework rather than the only feature.


\subsection{Code Acceleration Tools}

Tools such as Numba, Cython, and manual C++ extensions can speed up Python code.
They are relevant because Accelera also tries to accelerate supported custom
preprocessing functions. These tools can significantly improve performance for
numeric and loop-heavy workloads, and they provide more control for experienced
developers who understand optimization and compilation.

\medskip
\noindent However, these tools often require users to understand extra syntax,
compilation steps, or type constraints. They also do not automatically decide
where acceleration fits inside a machine-learning pipeline. In addition, they do
not provide pipeline graphs, AutoML, reports, or benchmark features.

\medskip
\noindent The Accelera Parallelizer is different because it is designed to work with the
pipeline module. It attempts to optimize supported custom preprocessing
functions and attach the optimized callable back to the pipeline node. This makes
code acceleration part of the workflow instead of a separate manual task.
```


\section{Business Case and Financial Analysis}

Accelera can be developed as an open-source framework with optional hosted
services around benchmarking, reporting, team collaboration, and managed
execution. The open-source part helps adoption because students, researchers,
and developers can use the framework freely. The business value can come from
hosted benchmark features, team dashboards, managed experiment tracking, and
support services.

\subsection{Business Case}

The main business case is based on reducing time spent in repetitive
data-science work. Users and teams spend time preparing data, comparing models,
rewriting scripts, debugging pipeline steps, and measuring results. Accelera can
reduce this time by providing one framework that handles multiple stages of the
workflow.

\begin{table}[!htbp]

Faster experimentation &
Automated preprocessing and AutoML reduce repeated manual trials. &
Students, data scientists, small teams. \\
\midrule

Graph pipelines and executed graphs make workflows easier to inspect and reuse. &
ML engineers and research teams. \\
\midrule

Parallelizer can speed up supported loop-heavy preprocessing code. &
Teams with custom preprocessing bottlenecks. \\
\midrule

Better comparison &

Universities, communities, and organizations. \\
\midrule

Lower entry barrier &
Reports and automation make ML workflows easier for beginners. &

\bottomrule
\end{tabular}
\end{table}

The first possible product direction is a hosted benchmark platform. Users can
submit pipelines, compare results on public datasets, and view leaderboards. A
free tier can support public datasets and basic submissions, while paid tiers can
support private benchmarks, team spaces, larger storage, and advanced reports.

The second possible product direction is a managed workflow dashboard. This
dashboard can visualize preprocessing results, model leaderboards, pipeline
graphs, and runtime comparisons. Teams can use it to organize experiments and
share results without relying only on notebooks.

The third possible product direction is professional support and integration.
Organizations that want to use Accelera internally may need help adapting the
framework to their datasets, infrastructure, or benchmark requirements. This
creates a possible support and consulting model.

\subsection{Financial Analysis}

The financial analysis can be divided into expected costs and possible revenue
streams. Since Accelera is mainly a software project, the capital expenditure is
limited compared with hardware-based products. Most costs come from development,
hosting, cloud resources, storage, and maintenance.

  monitoring for teams.

  support for organizations.

A simple financial plan can start with low-cost hosting and manual support. In
the first stage, the focus should be adoption and validation. The open-source
framework can attract users, while the benchmark platform can collect early
feedback. In the second stage, private benchmark spaces and team dashboards can

students, researchers, and small teams through the free framework, the benchmark
and reporting services can become the commercial layer that supports future
development.


\chapter{Literature Survey}
In this chapter, we give an overview of the topics, algorithms, and techniques needed to understand the project as a whole. We discuss the technical subjects and background knowledge, and then summarize the research papers related to our project.

\section{Background and Previous Work}
\noindent This section introduces the main background needed to understand the Accelera project as a whole. It explains the important concepts, techniques, and tools that were used in different parts of the system. These background topics help clarify how the project supports data preparation, pipeline execution, report generation, model evaluation, and benchmarking. The goal of this section is to give the reader enough context before moving to the methodology and implementation chapters.

\subsection{Data Science and Machine Learning}
Data science is the field that uses data, statistics, programming, and domain
knowledge to understand a problem and extract useful insights from it. It is
usually used when we have raw data and we want to turn it into useful
information or predictions.

\noindent Machine learning is one of the main parts of data science. Instead of writing
fixed rules for every case, we train a model on examples, and the model learns
patterns from these examples. After training, the model can use what it learned
to make predictions on new data.

\noindent In supervised machine learning, the training data contains input features and a
target label. The model learns the relationship between the features and the
target. If the target is a class, such as spam or not spam, the problem is called
classification. If the target is a continuous number, such as price or
temperature, the problem is called regression.

\subsubsection{Data Exploration and Preprocessing ~\cite{komorowski2016exploratory}}
Exploratory Data Analysis, or EDA, is the step where we inspect the dataset
before building the model. We summarize columns, draw visualizations, check
missing values, and try to understand relationships between variables. This step
helps us notice problems early instead of discovering them after training the
model.

\noindent Data preprocessing is the stage of transforming raw data into a clean and usable
format for machine learning models. In real datasets, the data may contain
missing values, duplicated rows, different feature scales, and categorical
columns that cannot be used directly by many models. Because of this,
preprocessing is usually considered an essential part of a machine learning
workflow.

\noindent Missing value imputation is used when some values in a dataset are empty.
Instead of removing all rows that contain missing values, the missing values can
be replaced using a suitable value, such as the mean, median, mode, or another
estimated value.

\noindent Robust scaling is a scaling technique that uses the median and the interquartile
range instead of the mean and standard deviation. It is useful when the data
contains outliers, because the median and interquartile range are less affected
by extreme values:
\[
x' = \frac{x - \mathop{\mathrm{median}}(X)}{\mathop{\mathrm{IQR}}(X)}
\]
where $x'$ is the scaled value, $x$ is the original value,
$\mathop{\mathrm{median}}(X)$ is the median of the feature values, and
$\mathop{\mathrm{IQR}}(X)$ is the interquartile range.

\noindent Categorical encoding is the process of converting text categories into numeric
values. Many traditional machine learning models and preprocessing pipelines
expect numerical input. Two examples are binary encoding and frequency encoding.
Binary encoding first gives each category a number, then converts this number
into binary form and stores the binary digits in separate columns. Frequency
encoding replaces each category with how often it appears in the dataset:
\[
f(c) = \frac{\mathop{\mathrm{count}}(c)}{N}
\]
where $f(c)$ is the frequency of category $c$,
$\mathop{\mathrm{count}}(c)$ is the number of rows containing this category,
and $N$ is the total number of rows.

\subsubsection{Model Evaluation Metrics ~\cite{hossin2015review}}
After training a model, we need metrics to check whether the model is performing
well or not. The selected metric depends on the type of problem. Classification
models are evaluated using metrics that compare predicted classes with actual
classes, while regression models are evaluated using metrics that measure the
difference between predicted numerical values and real values.\\

\noindent\textbf{Classification metrics:}
Classification metrics are used when the output is a class or category, such as
