tree \(t\) predicts the cost \(\hat{c}_t(x)\) for candidate vector \(x\), then
the surrogate mean prediction is estimated as

\[
\mu(x) =
\frac{1}{T}
\sum_{t=1}^{T}
\hat{c}_t(x),
\]

\noindent where \(T\) is the number of trees in the forest. The uncertainty estimate is
computed from the disagreement between the trees:

\[
\sigma(x) =
\sqrt{
\frac{1}{T}
\sum_{t=1}^{T}
\left(
\hat{c}_t(x) - \mu(x)
\right)^2
}.
\]

\noindent A small value of \(\sigma(x)\) means that the trees give similar predictions,
while a larger value means that the trees disagree more strongly. Therefore,
\(\mu(x)\) estimates the expected cost of a candidate, and \(\sigma(x)\)
estimates the uncertainty of that prediction.

\noindent The optimizer then uses expected improvement to rank candidate configurations.
Because the objective is to minimize cost, improvement is measured relative to
the best observed cost \(c^+\). For a candidate \(x\), the predicted improvement
is

\[
I(x) = c^+ - \mu(x) - \xi,
\]

\noindent where \(\xi\) is a small exploration parameter. A larger value of \(\xi\)
encourages the optimizer to prefer candidates that are more likely to produce a
clear improvement rather than only a small predicted gain.

\noindent The standardized improvement is

\[
z(x) =
\frac{I(x)}{\sigma(x)}.
\]

\noindent The expected improvement is then computed as

\[
\operatorname{EI}(x) =
I(x)\Phi(z)
+
\sigma(x)\phi(z),
\]

\noindent where \(\Phi\) is the standard normal cumulative distribution function and
\(\phi\) is the standard normal probability density function. The first term
rewards candidates with a low predicted cost, while the second term rewards
candidates with higher predictive uncertainty. Therefore, expected improvement
balances exploitation and exploration.

\begin{table}[H]
\centering
\caption{Main quantities used in surrogate-guided candidate selection}
\label{tab:gp-automl-surrogate-quantities}
\renewcommand{\arraystretch}{1.2}
\begin{tabular}{p{0.22\textwidth}p{0.68\textwidth}}
\toprule
\textbf{Symbol} & \textbf{Meaning} \\
\midrule
\(x_{\lambda}\) &
Numerical vector representation of configuration \(\lambda\). \\

\(x'\) &
Augmented vector containing configuration and fidelity information. \\

\(\mu(x)\) &
Mean cost predicted by the random-forest surrogate. \\

\(\sigma(x)\) &
Prediction uncertainty estimated from disagreement between trees. \\

\(c^+\) &
Best observed cost so far. \\

\(\xi\) &
Exploration parameter used in expected improvement. \\

\(\operatorname{EI}(x)\) &
Expected improvement score used to rank candidate configurations. \\
\bottomrule
\end{tabular}
\end{table}

\noindent In each surrogate-guided step, the optimizer samples a pool of candidate
configurations from the conditional search space. The candidates are converted
to numerical vectors, duplicate vectors are removed, and the remaining unseen
candidates are scored using expected improvement. The candidate with the highest
expected improvement is scheduled for evaluation.

\begin{figure}[H]
\centering
\renewcommand{\arraystretch}{1.25}
\begin{tabular}{c}
Sample candidate pool \\
\(\Downarrow\) \\
Convert configurations to numerical vectors \\
\(\Downarrow\) \\
Append fidelity information \\
\(\Downarrow\) \\
Remove duplicate and already-evaluated candidates \\
\(\Downarrow\) \\
Predict \(\mu(x)\) and \(\sigma(x)\) using the surrogate \\
\(\Downarrow\) \\
Compute expected improvement \\
\(\Downarrow\) \\
Select the highest-ranked unseen candidate
\end{tabular}
\caption{Surrogate-guided candidate-selection workflow}
\label{fig:gp-automl-surrogate-candidate-selection}
\end{figure}

\noindent This procedure avoids exhaustive enumeration of the mixed conditional search
space. Instead of evaluating every possible configuration, the optimizer uses the
surrogate model to focus the search on configurations that are either predicted
to perform well or have enough uncertainty to be worth exploring.

\subsubsection{Warm-Start Selection}

Warm-start selection gives the optimizer an informed starting point by reusing
configurations that performed well on related historical datasets. For a new
dataset \(D\), the engine first computes a task-specific metafeature vector
\(m_D\). Historical datasets are represented by stored metafeature vectors
\(m_i\), together with their previously successful configurations.

\noindent The similarity between the new dataset and each historical dataset is measured
using a normalized distance. Let \(r\) be a scaling vector for the metafeature
dimensions. The distance between the current dataset \(D\) and a historical
dataset \(i\) is

\[
d(D,i) =
\left\|
\frac{m_D - m_i}{r}
\right\|_2 .
\]

\noindent The normalization prevents large-scale metafeatures, such as the number of
samples or features, from dominating smaller-scale metafeatures. Historical
datasets are then ranked by increasing distance, so datasets with smaller
distances are considered more similar to the current task.

\noindent After ranking the historical datasets, the engine gathers candidate
configurations from the nearest datasets. These configurations are not inserted
blindly into the search. First, configurations whose model names are not present
in the active model set are removed. This ensures that warm starts respect the
current user constraints and dataset compatibility checks. Next, configurations
are deduplicated using their model name and parameter signature, so repeated
historical suggestions do not occupy multiple positions in the initial queue.

\noindent Each remaining historical configuration is then converted into a valid
ConfigSpace configuration for the current search space. During this conversion,
invalid or unsupported parameters are skipped safely. This can happen, for
example, when the current version of a component does not contain a parameter
that existed in a stored historical configuration, or when the current active
space has stricter bounds. A failed conversion does not stop the search; it only
removes that warm-start candidate.

\begin{figure}[H]
\centering
\renewcommand{\arraystretch}{1.25}
\begin{tabular}{rcl}
Current dataset \(D\)
& \(\xrightarrow{\text{extract}}\) &
Metafeatures \(m_D\) \\

Historical metafeatures \(m_i\)
& \(\xrightarrow{\text{compare with } m_D}\) &
Distances \(d(D,i)\) \\

Distances \(d(D,i)\)
& \(\xrightarrow{\text{sort}}\) &
Nearest historical datasets \\

Nearest datasets
& \(\xrightarrow{\text{collect}}\) &
Historical configurations \\

Historical configurations
& \(\xrightarrow{\text{filter + deduplicate}}\) &
Valid warm-start candidates \\

Valid warm-start candidates
& \(\xrightarrow{\text{convert}}\) &
Initial configuration queue \\
\end{tabular}
\caption{Warm-start transfer from similar historical datasets}
\label{fig:gp-automl-warm-start-transfer}
\end{figure}

\noindent The number of historical datasets used, the number of configurations taken from
each dataset, and the total number of warm-start configurations are bounded by
user parameters. These bounds keep the warm-start stage controlled and prevent
historical suggestions from dominating the search.

\noindent Warm starts are inserted into the initial configuration queue before uninformed
random samples. However, they are still evaluated on the current dataset using
the same validation and cost-conversion procedure as all other configurations.
Therefore, warm starts only guide the early search; they do not bypass
evaluation and cannot directly force the final selected model.

\subsubsection{Parallel Execution and Timeout Control}

The optimizer may suggest a batch of trials in one scheduling step. A batch can
contain promoted configurations from earlier fidelity stages, warm-start
configurations from historical datasets, and newly proposed configurations
selected either by the surrogate model or by random sampling. This batching
allows the search to use multiple CPU cores while keeping the optimizer logic
centralized in the parent process.

\noindent For parallel execution, each trial is executed in a separate worker process. The
worker receives the configuration, builds the corresponding estimator, evaluates
it, and returns an \texttt{EvaluationResult} through a process-safe queue. The
parent process does not wait passively for all workers to finish. Instead, it
periodically checks the worker queues, collects completed results, and monitors
the elapsed time of each running process.

\noindent If a worker process exceeds the configured
\texttt{per\_run\_time\_limit}, the parent process terminates it and records the
trial with a timeout status. The timeout result is stored in the run history in
the same way as other failed evaluations, together with a failure cost. This
ensures that one slow or non-terminating estimator does not block the complete
AutoML search.

\noindent The timeout mechanism can be summarized as follows:

\begin{figure}[H]
\centering
\renewcommand{\arraystretch}{1.25}
\begin{tabular}{rcl}
Optimizer batch
& \(\longrightarrow\) &
Worker processes \\

Worker process
& \(\longrightarrow\) &
Evaluate one trial \\

Successful worker
& \(\longrightarrow\) &
Return \texttt{EvaluationResult} through queue \\

Slow worker
& \(\longrightarrow\) &
Terminate after \texttt{per\_run\_time\_limit} \\

Parent process
& \(\longrightarrow\) &
Collect results and update run history \\
\end{tabular}
\caption{Process-level parallel execution and timeout handling}
\label{fig:gp-automl-parallel-timeout-control}
\end{figure}

\noindent Process-level execution is used instead of thread-level execution because some
estimators cannot always be interrupted reliably once they enter a long-running
fit procedure. Terminating a separate process is more robust than attempting to
stop a thread inside the same Python process. This is especially useful when an
estimator hangs, becomes extremely slow, or calls compiled code that does not
regularly return control to Python.

\noindent Overall, this design improves robustness and resource control. The optimizer can
continue receiving valid results even when some trials fail or time out, and the
caller can control the amount of parallel work performed by the search.


\subsubsection{Final Model and Ensemble Selection}

After the search phase finishes, the engine selects the strongest configuration
evaluated at full fidelity. This configuration is rebuilt from its stored model
name, preprocessing choice, and hyperparameters, and then fitted on all
available training data. This final refitting step is necessary because the
search phase estimates performance using validation folds, while the final model
should use the complete training set before being returned to the user.

\noindent If ensemble construction is disabled, the refitted best individual model is
returned directly. If ensembles are enabled, the engine considers successful
candidates from the run history as possible ensemble members. Failed trials,
timed-out trials, and configurations without valid fitted estimators are ignored.
This ensures that the ensemble is built only from models that were evaluated
successfully during the search.

\noindent The ensemble construction stage supports two strategies: voting and stacking.
For voting, the engine selects a bounded set of competitive estimators from the
run history. These estimators are combined using a task-specific voting model.
For classification, the voting ensemble combines class predictions or class
probabilities. For regression, it combines numeric predictions. Voting is simple
and robust because it does not train an additional meta-model.

\noindent Stacking uses a more expressive structure. Instead of directly averaging the
base-model outputs, it trains a meta-model that learns how to combine them. The
engine first considers candidate diversity before selecting base estimators, so
the stack is not formed only from many near-identical models. Each selected base
estimator is wrapped in bagging to improve stability. The base estimators then
produce out-of-fold predictions, which are used as training features for the
meta-model.

\noindent Forward selection is used to choose the final set of base estimators for the
stacking ensemble. The procedure starts with the strongest single candidate.
Additional candidates are tested one at a time. A candidate is kept if adding it
improves the validation score of the meta-model. The process stops when no
remaining candidate improves the score, or when the configured ensemble size is
reached. However, the minimum base-model requirement is also respected, so the
engine can include enough base estimators to form a valid stack even if early
improvements are small.

\noindent For classification, each base estimator may output a probability vector over the
class labels. Before these outputs are passed to the meta-model, the probability
columns are aligned according to the global class order. This avoids a common
problem where different classifiers store class labels in different orders. If
the columns were not aligned, the meta-model could interpret the probability of
one class as if it belonged to another class. After alignment, the probability
blocks from all base estimators are concatenated and passed to logistic
regression as the classification meta-model.

\noindent For regression, the stacking representation is simpler. Each base estimator
contributes one numeric prediction column. These columns are concatenated to
form the meta-feature matrix, and ridge regression is used as the regression
meta-model. Ridge regression is suitable here because it can combine correlated
base predictions while controlling the size of the learned coefficients.

\noindent The best individual model is always available as a safe fallback. The ensemble
is only returned when it performs at least as well as this individual model under
the same validation metric. The final decision can be written as

\[
\text{Final model} =
\begin{cases}
\text{ensemble}, & \text{if } S_{\text{ens}} \geq S_{\text{best}}, \\
\text{best individual model}, & \text{otherwise},
\end{cases}
\]

\noindent where \(S_{\text{ens}}\) is the validation score of the ensemble and
\(S_{\text{best}}\) is the validation score of the strongest individual model.
This rule prevents the system from returning a more complex ensemble unless it
provides at least the same predictive quality as the best single model. As a
result, ensemble construction can improve performance when the base models
complement each other, while still preserving the simpler individual model when
the ensemble does not provide a measurable benefit.

\subsection{Modular Decomposition}
\noindent The AutoML module is divided into cooperating components so that search,
evaluation, model definitions, warm starts, and final model construction can be
extended separately.

\begin{table}[H]
\centering
\acceleraTableStyle
\caption{AutoML implementation units}
\label{tab:automl_implementation_units}
\small
\begin{tabularx}{\textwidth}{>{\raggedright\arraybackslash}p{0.43\textwidth}X}
\toprule
\textbf{File or directory} & \textbf{Responsibility} \\
\midrule
\modulepath{base.py} & Shared estimator lifecycle, validation, fitted state,
leaderboard, and warning handling. \\
\midrule
\modulepath{classification.py} & Classification defaults and task-specific engine
construction. \\
\midrule
\modulepath{regression.py} & Regression defaults and task-specific engine

\midrule
\modulepath{core/automl.py} & Search orchestration, compatibility filtering,

\midrule

\midrule
\modulepath{configspace\_search\_space.py} & Task-specific conditional

\midrule

\midrule

\bottomrule
\end{tabularx}
\end{table}

\subsection{Constraints and Assumptions}

\begin{itemize}
  \item The current module targets supervised tabular classification and
  regression.
  \item Inputs must be compatible with Scikit-learn validation utilities.
  \item The evaluator assumes independent and identically distributed samples.
  Classification uses stratified splitting where possible, while regression uses
  ordinary K-fold or shuffle splitting.
  \item Specialized grouped, temporal, or spatial validation is outside the
  current design.
  \item Optional libraries such as XGBoost, LightGBM, and CatBoost are used only
  when available in the environment.
  \item Trial failures, timeouts, and estimator exceptions must be recorded in
  the run history rather than crashing the full search.
  \item Search should remain reproducible when a random state is provided.
  \item Warm-start metadata must be stored relative to the package so it works
  after installation.
\end{itemize}

\subsection{Module Summary}
\noindent The AutoML module provides an Accelera-integrated model-search layer for
supervised tabular classification and regression. It combines conditional
configuration-space construction, cross-validated evaluation, staged fidelity,
surrogate-guided candidate selection, meta-learning warm starts, process-level
timeout control, and final ensemble selection. The module keeps a familiar
Scikit-learn-style interface while exposing useful inspection outputs such as
the leaderboard and run history. This makes it usable as both a practical model
selection tool and an extensible research component inside Accelera.

\input{sections/methodology-deployment}


\section{Benchmark Platform Module}
\noindent The Benchmark Platform module is designed to provide a shared
environment where users can create machine learning benchmarks, submit
predicted results, and compare submissions using selected evaluation metrics.
The module includes a backend API, database schemas, Python validation and
scoring scripts, and a frontend interface for users and admins.

\noindent The module separates benchmark creation from submission evaluation.
This makes the workflow clearer: first, the benchmark creator defines the task
and evaluation rules; then, users submit predictions under the same conditions.
This separation helps ensure that all users are evaluated using the same
ground-truth file and the same metric configuration. Another important design
point is the use of Python scripts for file validation and metric scoring. The
backend is implemented using Node.js and Express, while Python handles the
machine learning evaluation part through Scikit-learn metrics and dataset
loading utilities.

\subsection{Functional Description}
\noindent The main function of this module is to support fair comparison
between different machine learning solutions. A user can create a benchmark by
providing the benchmark title, description, problem type, target column,
dataset link, test dataset link, ground-truth target link, and evaluation
metric. The system validates the provided links and checks that the test set
does not contain the target column while the target file contains the expected
target column.

\noindent After a benchmark is created, other users can submit their prediction
file and repository link. The system validates the submitted prediction file,
calculates the score using the selected Scikit-learn metric, stores the
submission, and displays submissions on a leaderboard. The leaderboard is
ordered based on the metric configuration, where some metrics are better when
their value is higher and others are better when their value is lower.

\subsection{Modular Decomposition}
\noindent The Benchmark Platform is decomposed into several smaller components:

\begin{itemize}
  \item \textbf{Benchmark Management:}
  This component handles benchmark creation, listing, filtering, viewing,
and deletion. It stores benchmark information such as title, description,
problem type, dataset links, target column, selected metric, metric parameters,
creation date, and creator.

  \item \textbf{Metric Management:}
  This component allows admin users to define evaluation metrics. Each
metric contains a display name, the corresponding Scikit-learn metric function
name, needed parameters, problem type, and whether a higher or lower score is
better. Metrics are linked to benchmarks and used during submission scoring.

  \item \textbf{Submission Management:}
  This component allows users to submit prediction files for a specific
benchmark. Each submission stores the benchmark id, submitting user, repository
link, prediction file link, score, and submission date. Users can also update
their existing prediction file or delete their submission if they have
permission.

  \item \textbf{Validation and Scoring Scripts:}
  The backend calls Python scripts to validate benchmark files and score
submissions. During benchmark creation, the validation script checks that the
test file does not include the target column and that the target file contains
the required target column. During submission, the scoring script loads the
ground-truth file and the submitted prediction file, checks their length and
target column, then computes the selected Scikit-learn metric.

  \item \textbf{Leaderboard Interface:}
  The frontend displays benchmark submissions in a leaderboard. It shows
the submitter information, repository link, score, and submission date. The
sorting direction depends on the selected metric, so the platform can support
both metrics where higher is better and metrics where lower is better.
\end{itemize}

\Needspace{12\baselineskip}
\begin{lstlisting}[style=algostyle,caption={Benchmark creation flow}]
Input: benchmark form data
Output: saved benchmark or validation error

1:  Receive benchmark creation request
2:  Validate problem type
3:  Check title uniqueness
4:  Validate dataset, test set, and target label links
5:  Run validate_user_links.py
6:  If the test file contains the target column, reject benchmark
7:  If the target file does not contain the target column, reject benchmark
8:  Save benchmark document in MongoDB
\end{lstlisting}

\Needspace{12\baselineskip}
\begin{lstlisting}[style=algostyle,caption={Submission scoring flow}]
Input: benchmark target file, submitted prediction file, metric name
Output: score or validation error

1:  Retrieve true target file
2:  Retrieve submitted prediction file
3:  Check that both files have the same number of rows
4:  Check that submitted file contains the target column
5:  Load metric function from sklearn.metrics
6:  Compute metric(true_target, predicted_target, metric_parameters)
7:  Return score to the backend as JSON
8:  Save submission with score
\end{lstlisting}

\subsection{Design Constraints}
\noindent Several constraints affected the design of the Benchmark Platform:

\begin{itemize}
  \item \textbf{Task and data constraint:} The platform is limited to
  classification and regression benchmarks that use tabular files. This includes
  the training data, test data, prediction file, and target file.

  \item \textbf{Storage and access constraint:} Dataset and prediction files are
  handled through accessible links to reduce server storage usage. 

 \item \textbf{Scoring constraint:} The platform uses Scikit-learn metrics for
scoring. Therefore, the selected metric must be supported by Scikit-learn and
must match the benchmark problem type.
\end{itemize}


\subsection{Module Summary}
\noindent The Benchmark Platform module provides a controlled environment for
benchmark creation, prediction submission, validation, scoring, and leaderboard
ranking. It keeps the web management logic in the backend and frontend, while
the metric calculation logic is handled by Python scoring scripts.

\chapter{System Testing and Verification}
This chapter explains how the project was tested to make sure that the main
parts work correctly. The testing was not done in one way only, because each
part of the project has a different output. The main purpose of testing was to make sure that each part can receive the
input, apply the required steps, save the needed files, and return the expected
output without errors.

\section{Testing Setup}
Testing was performed in isolated Python environments using the same dependency
versions as the implementation. Pytest was used for automated unit and
integration tests, while Pandas, NumPy, Scikit-learn, PyTorch, and Matplotlib
supported data processing, model validation, and result visualization.
CMake, C++, PyBind11, LLVM/Clang, and OpenMP were used to build and test the
native graph and Parallelizer components.

The Benchmark Platform was tested using its React frontend, Express backend,
MongoDB database, and Python scoring scripts. Deployment tests covered FastAPI
endpoints, input validation, model loading, telemetry, and Docker packaging.
Tests were executed on Linux and Windows where platform-specific behavior was
relevant.

Automated tests verified expected outputs, exceptions, serialization, graph
execution, and module integration. Manual inspection was used for generated
reports, diagrams, web interfaces, deployment workflows, and other visual or
environment-dependent outputs.

\section{Testing Plan and Strategy}

Testing followed a layered strategy. Unit tests first validated individual
functions, classes, graph nodes, preprocessing operations, metrics, and code
conversion rules. Integration tests then verified communication between
components, including Python--C++ bindings, graph branches, preprocessing and
model nodes, AutoML workflows, benchmark scoring, and deployment services.

End-to-end tests exercised complete workflows from dataset preparation through
model training, evaluation, persistence, and inference. Results were compared
with reference implementations where possible. Performance experiments measured
latency and memory consumption under controlled datasets, while Parallelizer
tests additionally checked output equivalence before reporting speedup.

The testing strategy followed these principles:

\begin{itemize}
    \item use identical datasets, splits, models, metrics, and random seeds when
    comparing implementations;
    \item test successful behavior together with invalid inputs and expected
    failures;
    \item verify numerical and prediction equivalence before evaluating
    performance;
    \item distinguish cold-cache, warm-cache, compilation, and execution time;
    \item test saved pipelines and models after loading them in a new workflow;
    \item inspect platform-specific behavior on Linux and Windows; and
    \item combine automated regression tests with manual validation of visual
    and deployment outputs.
\end{itemize}

This layered approach allowed defects to be located within individual modules
before validating the complete Accelera workflow.


\subsection{Module Testing}
\input{sections/results-modhamed}
\subsubsection{Auto Preprocessing Module}
Most of the preprocessing functions were tested using unit tests. These tests
were used to check validation rules, artifact saving,and feature transformation. Real datasets were also used to test the system in a
more practical way. These datasets were collected from Kaggle for tabular,
text, and image data.
\paragraph{How the Auto Preprocessing testing works}

The Auto Preprocessing testing was done as a practical full-flow test. The idea
was to run preprocessing on real datasets, then pass the processed output to a
model. This means the test does not only check that preprocessing runs without
errors, but also checks that the returned data can actually be used for
training, validation, and prediction.

\noindent For tabular datasets, the test starts by loading each dataset with its
target column and problem type. The preprocessing flow splits the data, fits the
needed encoders and scalers, transforms the features and target, applies feature
selection, and saves the preprocessing artifacts. After that, a machine learning
model is trained on the processed training data and evaluated on the processed
validation data. Classification datasets are evaluated using Macro F1-score,
and regression datasets are evaluated using the R-squared score. The same
datasets are also processed by a baseline automated cleaning tool, so the final
scores can be compared and the Accelera output can be checked against an
existing preprocessing approach.

\noindent For text datasets, the test checks that raw text can be converted into
features that a classifier can understand. The preprocessing flow receives the
text column and target column, cleans the text, tokenizes it, applies TF-IDF,
encodes the labels, and returns processed training and validation data. A
classifier is then trained on the transformed text features and evaluated using
Macro F1-score. This confirms that the text preprocessing output is numeric,
consistent, and usable for a normal machine learning model.

\noindent For image classification, the test checks the full image preparation
flow. The preprocessing reads images from class folders, validates image files,
skips corrupted images, creates class-to-label mappings, resizes the images,
applies augmentation when enabled, and returns PyTorch DataLoaders. A neural
network model is then trained using these loaders. The inference path is also
checked by loading the saved model and applying the testing preprocessing to new
images. This verifies that the saved image size and class mapping can be reused
during testing, not only during training.

\noindent The main concept in all three tests is to check preprocessing through
the next step in the machine learning pipeline. If the tabular arrays, TF-IDF
matrices, labels, image tensors, or saved artifacts were wrong, the model
training or evaluation step would fail or give invalid results.

\centering
\acceleraTableStyle
\caption{Datasets Used in Module Testing}
\label{tab:module_testing_datasets}

@{}}

\noindent These results show that the preprocessing output can be used by different
models after transformation. The tabular results were strong in many datasets,
especially the larger classification datasets and the car price regression
dataset. The lower scores on datasets such as  TestReviews show
that preprocessing alone does not solve every modeling problem. Some datasets
still need better features, model tuning, or more suitable models.

\subsubsection{AutoML Module}
\paragraph{Testing Setup}
The AutoML experiments compare Accelera AutoML with Auto-sklearn, FLAML, and
TPOT on supervised tabular classification and regression tasks. The evaluation
asks two practical questions: whether the systems attain comparable predictive
quality, and how much wall-clock time they require to do so. Classification is
evaluated primarily by accuracy, while regression is evaluated by the
coefficient of determination ($R^2$), root mean squared error (RMSE), and mean
absolute error (MAE). Runtime is reported in seconds for both task types.

\paragraph{Testing Plan and Strategy}
The classification experiments were executed through the open-source
AutoMLBenchmark (AMLB) runner, available at
\url{https://github.com/openml/automlbenchmark.git}. They use the
\modulepath{flaml\_paper\_39} classification suite and the
\modulepath{flaml\_paper\_1h} constraint. The reported classification
evaluation covers 12 datasets. The regression benchmark was built from the
Penn Machine Learning Benchmarks (PMLB) repository, available at
\url{https://github.com/EpistasisLab/pmlb.git}, and was also executed through
AMLB. The reported regression evaluation covers 10 datasets and uses the
\modulepath{pmlb\_regression} suite with the \modulepath{pmlb\_30m}
constraint.

\noindent All systems were run through the same benchmark interface. The recorded setup
used one fold and one allocated core per task. The regression time budget was
1,800 seconds per dataset; the classification constraint allowed up to one
hour.

\paragraph{Comparative Results: Classification}
Table~\ref{tab:automl_classification_results} summarizes the classification
results stored with the project. Auto-sklearn obtained the highest mean
accuracy at 0.8842. Accelera reached a mean accuracy of 0.8791, a difference of
about 0.0051, while recording the lowest mean duration at 1,201.7 seconds. Its
mean runtime was approximately one third of the runtimes recorded for the other
three systems. Accelera also had the largest number of recorded speed wins.

\begin{table}[H]
\centering

\caption{Classification benchmark summary.}
\label{tab:automl_classification_results}

\midrule
Auto-sklearn & 0.884178 & 3615.71 & 3 & 3 \\
FLAML        & 0.881427 & 3655.63 & 6 & 0 \\
Accelera     & 0.879076 & 1201.72 & 6 & 15 \\
TPOT         & 0.877654 & 3953.78 & 8 & 1 \\

\paragraph{Comparative Results: Regression}
Table~\ref{tab:automl_regression_results} reports the updated regression
summary over ten datasets. Auto-sklearn obtained the highest mean $R^2$ at
0.5609, followed closely by TPOT at 0.5592. Accelera reached 0.5294 and
recorded the lowest mean runtime at 892.64 seconds, compared with
1,820--1,864 seconds for the competing systems. Accelera also achieved six
speed wins, the most of any evaluated system.

\paragraph{Result Reproduction}
The benchmark experiments can be reproduced using the instructions provided in
the project repository. These instructions describe the required environment
setup and the commands used to rerun the classification and regression
benchmarks. The generated figures, CSV summaries, and raw benchmark outputs are
stored with the project results, allowing the reported values to be checked
against future reruns. The reproduction guide is available at
\href{https://github.com/Mohamed-Ashraf273/accelera/blob/main/accelera/src/accelera_automl/benchmark_results/setup/reproduce_results.md}
{\texttt{setup/reproduce\_results.md}}.

\paragraph{AutoML Result Summary}
Across the stored experiments, Accelera achieved predictive results close to
the strongest competing framework while requiring markedly less mean runtime.
The classification results show a small accuracy difference relative to
Auto-sklearn, and the regression results show a modest $R^2$ difference
relative to Auto-sklearn, accompanied in both cases by substantial runtime
reductions. The complete stored artifacts and reproduction instructions are
retained with the implementation so that the reported measurements can be
inspected and rerun.

\subsubsection{Deployment Module}
\input{sections/results-deployment}

\subsubsection{Accelera Pipeline Reporting Module}

The pipeline reporting part was tested by generating reports from executed
pipeline results. The test checks that the report can read the serialized graph
file, create the graph image, detect branches, show the selected path, and
display metric results in the correct format.

Different metric result types were checked, such as single numbers, arrays,
dictionaries, strings, tuples, and plotted figures. The expected output was a
\texttt{report.html} file with readable tables and figures. The graph image was
also checked manually to make sure that selected nodes and normal nodes were
shown using different colors.

\subsubsection{Benchmark Platform Module}

The Benchmark Platform module was evaluated using manual functional testing to check its main features from the user side. The testing focused on creating benchmark tasks, validating dataset links, submitting prediction files, calculating scores, and displaying the results in the leaderboard.

\noindent The leaderboard was tested by submitting different results and checking whether they were ordered correctly based on the selected evaluation metric. This included metrics where higher values are better and metrics where lower values are better.

\noindent The scoring process was also tested manually by using different target and prediction files. The platform was checked to make sure it rejects invalid submissions, such as files with different row counts or missing target columns. These tests showed that the benchmark platform can handle result submission, calculate scores, validate files, and display leaderboard results correctly.

\subsection{Integration Testing}

Integration testing was done by connecting the output of one part with another
part. For Auto Preprocessing, the transformed data was passed to machine
learning models to check that the output was usable. For pipeline reporting,
the report was generated after graph serialization and metric calculation. For
benchmarking, the backend called the Python scoring script and then saved the
returned score in MongoDB.

These checks were important because a function can pass a unit test but still
fail when used with the rest of the system.

\subsection{Testing Schedule}

The testing was done during implementation and after completing each module.
First, small functions were tested. Then complete module flows were tested.
After that, final outputs such as reports, saved files, and leaderboard results
were checked manually. The final comparison with AutoCleanML was done after the
tabular preprocessing flow became stable.

\subsection{Comparative Results to Previous Work}
\subsubsection{Auto Preprocessing}
Auto Preprocessing was compared with AutoCleanML to check the performance of the proposed preprocessing approach. The comparison was done using several tabular datasets collected from Kaggle. The same datasets and the same machine learning models were used for both tools.
The comparison was applied only on tabular datasets because AutoCleanML mainly works with tabular data. For classification datasets, the Macro F1-score was used as the evaluation metric. For regression datasets, the R-squared score was used to measure how well the model predicts the target values.
After preprocessing the datasets, the output data was tested using four machine learning models. For classification problems, Logistic Regression, Random Forest, Decision Tree, and KNN were used. For regression problems, Linear Regression, Random Forest, Decision Tree, and KNN were used. This helped test the preprocessing output with different model types, not only one model.
The results were recorded and compared between Accelera and AutoCleanML for each selected dataset and model. The purpose of this comparison was to check whether Accelera can produce preprocessing results that are close to, or better than, an existing preprocessing tool.

\noindent From the classification and regression results, Accelera preprocessing achieved
competitive performance compared to AutoCleanML. The comparison was tested using
different types of models, including linear models, tree based models, Random
Forest, and KNN. In many cases, the results of Accelera were very close to
AutoCleanML, and in some cases Accelera achieved better scores.

\noindent These results show that the implemented preprocessing steps in Accelera are able
to prepare data in a valid way for different machine learning techniques. The
performance was not limited to one specific model type, but appeared across
several models and datasets. This means that Accelera successfully reached
results close to an existing automated preprocessing tool, while still using its
own preprocessing approach.

\noindent However, the results also show that no preprocessing tool gives the best score
in all cases. The final performance can still depend on the dataset, the problem
type, and the model used after preprocessing.

\chapter{Conclusions and Future Work}
\section{Faced Challenges}
The main implementation challenges were preserving correctness while sharing
branch computations, releasing intermediate arrays only after their final
consumer, and limiting generated OpenMP code to loops that pass dependency and
conversion checks. For the Deployment module, the main challenge was combining
model artifacts, serving code, validation, telemetry, Docker packaging, and
remote execution into one repeatable workflow. The experiments also identify
memory usage during branch-level parallel execution as the main remaining
graph-backend challenge.

\section{Gained Experience}
Through the project, the team gained practical experience in combining machine
learning, systems programming, web development, testing, and deployment
engineering inside one framework. The Pipeline and Parallelizer work improved
our understanding of graph execution, branch dependencies, memory release, and
safe parallel execution. The Auto Preprocessing and AutoML work strengthened
our experience with reusable preprocessing artifacts, model comparison, and
search-space design. The Benchmark Platform added experience with backend APIs,
database schemas, authentication, dashboards, and fair submission evaluation.

\noindent The Deployment module added a different kind of experience because it
required moving beyond model training into service delivery. It involved
FastAPI serving, schema validation, telemetry, Docker packaging, command-line
automation, model artifact versioning, and remote execution on cloud/server
targets. This helped connect the academic model-building workflow with the
engineering work needed to make trained models usable by external clients.

\section{Conclusions}
Accelera provides a connected framework for preparing data, selecting models,
building graph-based machine learning workflows, improving supported custom
code, deploying trained models, and comparing results through a benchmark
platform. The work shows that the project is not only a collection of separate
tools, but a workflow where each module can support a different stage of the
machine learning process.

\noindent The Auto Preprocessing module reduces repeated manual preparation work
for tabular, text, and image datasets. It saves fitted artifacts so that testing
data can be transformed in the same way as training data. The AutoML module
adds automated model search under time and trial limits, which helps compare
candidate models more systematically. Accelera Pipe represents workflows as
graphs, so shared preprocessing, branches, predictions, and metrics can be
organized more clearly. The Code Parallelizer adds a source-level optimization
path for supported loop-heavy code while keeping conservative safety checks.

\noindent The Deployment module and Benchmark Platform complete the workflow by
supporting model serving, version tracking, submission validation, scoring, and
leaderboard comparison. The testing and experimental results show that the
implemented modules can produce usable outputs, generate reports, evaluate
models, and compare results. Overall, Accelera gives a practical base for a
more organized and reusable machine learning workflow.

\noindent Accelera Pipe provides a graph-based execution model for branch-heavy
machine-learning pipelines and supports fitted executed graphs. The Code
Parallelizer provides a source-to-source path from supported Python or C++
loops to OpenMP-annotated C++ using converter rules, loop feature extraction,
classifier guidance, and safety checks. The Deployment module completes the
workflow by turning saved models and generated artifacts into validated API
services that can run locally or on supported cloud/server targets. The
available experiments show runtime improvements on large branch-selection
workloads while preserving selected models, accuracy, and output equivalence,
and the deployment tests verify repeatable serving, validation, telemetry, and
orchestration behavior.

\section{Future Work}
Future work should continue reducing graph memory consumption while preserving
parallel branch execution, and should extend safe converter and loop-analysis
coverage without weakening correctness checks. The preprocessing pipeline can
be improved by adapting preprocessing decisions based on the selected model
type and by adding stronger techniques for imbalanced datasets.

\noindent In the future, the benchmark platform can be improved by saving
uploaded datasets on the server instead of using Google Drive links. It can
also allow users to upload code files directly, instead of submitting only
links.

\noindent
The Deployment module can also be extended with additional targets such as
managed container services, richer monitoring, authentication for prediction
endpoints, and more advanced model registry metadata.
