\appendix
\chapter{Development Platforms and Tools}
\section{Hardware Platform}


\section{Software Platform}
The implemented modules use Python, C++, PyBind11, CMake, Clang-based AST
analysis, OpenMP, NumPy, Pandas, Matplotlib, PyTorch, Scikit-learn, Node.js,
React, MongoDB, FastAPI, Uvicorn, Great Expectations, Docker, Heroku CLI, SSH,
and AWS EC2 workflows. The Auto Preprocessing module depends mainly on Python
data-science libraries. The Pipeline Reporting module uses Python, HTML report
generation, Matplotlib, Pandas tables, and Graphviz for graph visualization.
The Benchmark Platform uses a Node.js and Express backend, MongoDB for storing
benchmarks and submissions, React for the frontend, and Python scripts for
dataset validation and metric scoring. The Deployment module uses FastAPI and
Uvicorn for serving, Great Expectations for runtime validation, Docker for
container packaging, and SSH/\texttt{rsync} for EC2 synchronization. The
available experiments include Linux and Windows execution paths.

\chapter{Use Cases}
\section{Auto Preprocessing Use Cases}
The user can use Auto Preprocessing when they have a dataset and want to
prepare it before training a model. For tabular data, the module can clean
missing values, encode categorical columns, scale numerical columns, select
features, save artifacts, and generate a report. For text data, it can clean
sentences, tokenize them, apply TF-IDF, encode labels, and generate a report.
For image data, it can validate images, map class folders to labels, resize
images, apply augmentation, and create DataLoaders.

\section{AutoML Use Cases}
The user can use Accelera AutoML when they need a competitive supervised
tabular model without manually testing many algorithms and hyperparameter
settings. The module supports classification and regression workflows through
\texttt{AutoMLClassifier} and \texttt{AutoMLRegressor}.

\noindent The main use cases are fitting a classifier and obtaining predicted
labels or class probabilities, fitting a regressor and obtaining numeric
predictions, restricting the search to an allowed subset of model families,
selecting the evaluation metric and cross-validation folds, limiting the total
search budget or per-trial timeout, enabling or disabling meta-learning warm
starts and ensemble construction, and inspecting the best score, leaderboard,
and complete run history.
\section{Accelera Pipe Use Cases}
Accelera Pipe is suitable when a machine-learning experiment is better
represented as a graph than as one fixed sequence of operations. The user can
place alternative preprocessing objects, estimators, or metrics in branches,
execute independent paths concurrently, and select a fitted path using
validation results. The executed graph can then be reused for inference without
retraining the selected model.

{\acceleraTableStyle
\renewcommand{\arraystretch}{1.02}
\setlength{\tabcolsep}{3pt}
\begin{longtable}{>{\bfseries}p{0.20\textwidth} p{0.36\textwidth} p{0.36\textwidth}}
\caption{Representative Accelera Pipe use cases.}
\label{tab:accelera-pipe-use-cases} \\
\toprule
Use case & Graph construction & Expected benefit \\
\midrule
\endfirsthead
\toprule
Use case & Graph construction & Expected benefit \\
\midrule
\endhead
\midrule
\multicolumn{3}{r}{\small Continued on the next page} \\
\endfoot
\bottomrule
\endlastfoot
Candidate comparison & Branch over preprocessing methods, models, or both,
then evaluate every path with the same metric. & A consistent search procedure
without manually constructing and executing each candidate pipeline. \\
\addlinespace
Validation-based selection & Attach prediction and metric nodes containing
validation data, then use a maximum, minimum, or custom selection strategy. & A
fitted executable graph containing the path chosen by the required objective. \\
\addlinespace
Parallel experimentation & Enable the default parallel graph execution when
independent branches are sufficiently large. & Concurrent evaluation of graph
paths while retaining a serial mode for debugging or small workloads. \\
\addlinespace
Ensemble construction & Connect model branches to a merge node using a
supported voting strategy. & Combined predictions from several estimators
inside the same graph. \\
\addlinespace
Reusable inference & Save the selected executed graph and load it in a later
process. & Persistence of fitted preprocessing and model objects in one file. \\
\addlinespace
Custom transformation & Add a Python function or custom transformer as a
preprocessing node. & Automatic Parallelizer optimization when the callable is
supported, with fallback to its original Python implementation. \\
\end{longtable}
}

\section{Code Parallelizer Use Cases}
The Code Parallelizer is intended for loop-heavy transformations whose
iterations are independent or form a supported scalar reduction. It can be
used as a standalone source-to-source tool or as an optimization service inside
Accelera Pipe. The source form determines whether the result is generated code
or an executable callable.

{\acceleraTableStyle
\renewcommand{\arraystretch}{1.02}
\setlength{\tabcolsep}{3pt}
\begin{longtable}{>{\bfseries}p{0.20\textwidth} p{0.36\textwidth} p{0.36\textwidth}}
\caption{Representative Code Parallelizer use cases.}
\label{tab:parallelizer-use-cases} \\
\toprule
Use case & Accepted input & Produced result \\
\midrule
\endfirsthead
\toprule
Use case & Accepted input & Produced result \\
\midrule
\endhead
\midrule
\multicolumn{3}{r}{\small Continued on the next page} \\
\endfoot
\bottomrule
\endlastfoot
Existing source file & Path to a supported Python, C, or C++ source file. & A
\texttt{parallelized\_<name>.c} file written to the selected output directory. \\
\addlinespace
Source inspection & In-memory Python or C/C++ source text. & Parallelized C++
source returned as a string for inspection or later compilation. \\
\addlinespace
Python acceleration & A supported top-level Python function containing
loop-oriented numerical code. & A compiled Python-callable wrapper backed by
C++ and OpenMP, or the original function if optimization fails. \\
\addlinespace
Transformer acceleration & A custom transformer with a supported
\texttt{transform} method. & The transformer instance with its transform method
replaced by the optimized callable when compilation succeeds. \\
\addlinespace
Pipeline integration & A custom function or transformer passed to
\texttt{Pipeline.preprocess}. & Transparent optimization before the callable is
stored in the preprocessing node. \\
\end{longtable}
}

The module is most useful for element-wise array updates, row-wise numerical
transformations, nested independent loops, and sum or product reductions. A
loop with cross-iteration dependencies, indirect writes, unsupported Python
syntax, or state that cannot be translated safely should remain sequential.

\section{Pipeline Reporting Use Cases}
The user can use the pipeline report after running an Accelera pipeline. The
report helps show the graph structure, branches, selected path, and metric
results. This is useful when the pipeline contains more than one branch and the
user wants to understand which path was selected.

\section{Benchmark Platform Use Cases}
The benchmark platform can be used when different users or models need to be
compared on the same task. A benchmark creator defines the dataset, test file,
target column, and metric. Users then submit prediction files, and the platform
calculates the score and displays the leaderboard.

\section{Deployment Module Use Cases}
The Deployment module is used after a model, fitted pipeline, or selected graph
path has produced serializable artifacts. It is most useful when the project
needs to move from experimentation to an API service that can be tested locally,
tracked, and optionally moved to a remote environment.

{\acceleraTableStyle
\renewcommand{\arraystretch}{1.02}
\setlength{\tabcolsep}{3pt}
\begin{longtable}{>{\bfseries}p{0.22\textwidth} p{0.34\textwidth} p{0.36\textwidth}}
\caption{Representative Deployment module use cases.}
\label{tab:deployment-use-cases} \\
\toprule
Use case & Deployment input & Expected benefit \\
\midrule
\endfirsthead
\toprule
Use case & Deployment input & Expected benefit \\
\midrule
\endhead
\midrule
\multicolumn{3}{r}{\small Continued on the next page} \\
\endfoot
\bottomrule
\endlastfoot
Local serving test & \modulepath{config.json} and saved model files under
\modulepath{models/}. & A FastAPI service running in Docker before any remote
release is attempted. \\
\addlinespace
Schema-protected prediction & Validation rules in the deployment
configuration. & Invalid payloads are rejected before they reach the model,
reducing runtime prediction errors. \\
\addlinespace
Batch CSV inference & Uploaded CSV file matching the configured schema. &
Multiple rows can be predicted through the same deployed API. \\
\addlinespace
Model snapshot and rollback & Active configuration and model artifacts. &
The user can commit a deployable model state and restore an older state when
needed. \\
\addlinespace
Remote server deployment & Prepared Docker service, SSH credentials, and EC2
host information. & The same serving package can run on a reachable virtual
machine. \\
\addlinespace
Operational inspection & Tracking log and service health endpoint. & The user
can inspect request counts, row counts, status counts, and service readiness. \\
\end{longtable}
}

\renewcommand{\lstlistingname}{Code Example}
\chapter{User Guide}

\section{Using Accelera Pipe}
\subsection{Constructing and selecting a graph}
An Accelera Pipe program is assembled by chaining node-building methods. A
node passed with \texttt{branch=True} is returned as a branch description;
\texttt{branch()} inserts those descriptions into the graph. The following
example compares two scalers under the same classifier and selects the path
with the highest validation accuracy.

\begin{lstlisting}[style=code,caption={Building and selecting an Accelera Pipe graph}]
from sklearn.linear_model import LogisticRegression
from sklearn.preprocessing import MinMaxScaler, StandardScaler

from accelera.src.accelera_pipe.core.pipeline import Pipeline

pipe = Pipeline()
pipe.branch(
    "preprocessing",
    pipe.preprocess("standard", StandardScaler(), branch=True),
    pipe.preprocess("minmax", MinMaxScaler(), branch=True),
).model(
    "classifier", LogisticRegression(max_iter=1000)
).predict(
    "validation_prediction", test_data=X_val
).metric(
    "validation_accuracy", "accuracy_score", y_true=y_val
)

validation_results, executed_graph = pipe(
    X_train, y_train, select_strategy="max"
)
test_results = executed_graph(X_test, y_true=y_test)
\end{lstlisting}

The training call returns a list of graph outputs and an
\texttt{ExecutedGraph}. The latter contains the fitted objects from the
selected path and accepts new feature data for inference. The available path
selection modes are \texttt{all}, \texttt{max}, and \texttt{min}; a user-defined
function can be supplied through \texttt{custom\_strategy}. Parallel graph
execution is enabled by default. It can be disabled for deterministic debugging
with \texttt{pipe.disable\_parallel\_execution()}, while
\texttt{pipe.set\_multicore\_threshold(n)} controls the workload threshold used
by the native scheduler.

Column-specific preprocessing can be declared without a custom adapter by
passing column names to \texttt{preprocess}. Internally, the node wraps the
transformer in a Scikit-learn \texttt{ColumnTransformer} and passes all remaining
columns through unchanged.

\begin{lstlisting}[style=code,caption={Applying a transformer to selected columns}]
from sklearn.preprocessing import OneHotEncoder

pipe.preprocess(
    "encode_categories",
    OneHotEncoder(handle_unknown="ignore", sparse_output=False),
    columns=["city", "category"],
)
\end{lstlisting}

The \texttt{cache} argument of preprocessing and model nodes is disabled by
default. It should be enabled only when repeated executions reuse the same
expensive input and the retained data is worth the additional memory.

\subsection{Saving training graphs and executed graphs}
Both graph forms are saved as one pickle file. Saving a \texttt{Pipeline}
preserves its graph recipe for later training. Saving an
\texttt{ExecutedGraph} preserves the fitted selected path for inference.

\begin{lstlisting}[style=code,caption={Saving and loading Accelera Pipe graphs}]
from accelera.src.accelera_pipe.core.executed_graph import ExecutedGraph
from accelera.src.accelera_pipe.core.pipeline import Pipeline

pipe.save("training_graph.pkl")
loaded_pipe = Pipeline.load("training_graph.pkl")
_, fitted_graph = loaded_pipe(X_train, y_train, select_strategy="max")

fitted_graph.save("inference_graph.pkl")
loaded_graph = ExecutedGraph.load("inference_graph.pkl")
predictions = loaded_graph(X_test, y_true=y_test)
\end{lstlisting}

Top-level custom preprocessing functions and simple lambdas are represented by
source-backed wrappers when possible. Functions that capture closure variables
cannot be reconstructed safely and should be rewritten as top-level functions
before the pipeline is saved.

\section{Using the Code Parallelizer}
\subsection{Parallelizing files and source strings}
The native bindings and a compiler with OpenMP support must be available before
using the module. Passing a file path writes generated source to the repository
root by default; \texttt{output\_dir} selects another directory. Passing source
text instead returns the transformed C++ code directly.

\Needspace{0.40\textheight}
\begin{lstlisting}[style=code,caption={Parallelizing a source file and a code string}]
from accelera.src.parallelizer.parallelizer import parallelizer

parallelizer.parallelize(
    "data/hard_test_files/hard_eval_002.py",
    output_dir="generated",
)

source = """
double sum(const double *x, int n) {
    double total = 0.0;
    for (int i = 0; i < n; ++i) total += x[i];
    return total;
}
"""
parallelized_source = parallelizer.parallelize(source)
print(parallelized_source)
\end{lstlisting}

For Python files and Python source strings, the supported subset is converted
to C++ before loop extraction. C and C++ inputs enter loop extraction directly.
The output should always be compiled and tested against the original program,
because parallel execution is valid only when the loop iterations preserve the
same observable result.

\subsection{Parallelizing a Python callable}
Passing a supported custom function returns a callable that can be invoked like
the original Python function. The Parallelizer extracts its source and helper
functions, converts them to C++, injects OpenMP directives, compiles a PyBind11
module, and caches the compiled artifact.

\begin{lstlisting}[style=code,caption={Compiling a custom numerical function}]
import numpy as np

from accelera.src.parallelizer.parallelizer import parallelizer

def square_values(X):
    for i in range(len(X)):
        for j in range(len(X[i])):
            X[i][j] = X[i][j] * X[i][j]
    return X

optimized_square = parallelizer.parallelize(square_values)
result = optimized_square(np.ones((10000, 25), dtype=np.float64))
\end{lstlisting}

If extraction, conversion, analysis, compilation, or import fails, callable
optimization prints a warning and returns the original Python implementation.
This fallback protects functional execution, but users should compare outputs
and timings to confirm both correctness and a useful speedup.

\subsection{Using automatic pipeline integration}
No direct Parallelizer call is required for custom preprocessing. Accelera Pipe
passes custom functions and custom transformer instances through
\texttt{parallelizer.parallelize()} when the preprocessing node is created.

\begin{lstlisting}[style=code,caption={Adding an automatically optimized custom function}]
from accelera.src.accelera_pipe.core.pipeline import Pipeline

pipe = Pipeline()
pipe.preprocess("square_values", square_values)
outputs, executed_graph = pipe(X_train, y_train)
\end{lstlisting}

For a custom transformer, only a class-defined \texttt{transform} method is
considered for replacement. Standard Scikit-learn objects and unsupported
callables pass through unchanged, preserving their normal behavior.

\section{Using Tabular Auto Preprocessing}

For tabular data, the user loads the dataset as a Pandas DataFrame, chooses the
target column, chooses the problem type, and gives an output folder. The output
folder stores the fitted preprocessing objects and the report.

\begin{lstlisting}[style=code,caption={Calling tabular training preprocessing}]
import pandas as pd
from accelera.src.auto_preprocessing.core.classical_training_preprocessing import (
    ClassicalTrainingPreprocessing,
)

df = pd.read_csv("dataset.csv")

preprocessor = ClassicalTrainingPreprocessing(
    df=df,
    target_col="target",
    problem_type="classification",
    folder_path="outputs/tabular_run",
    val_size=0.2,
    random_state=42,
    is_report=True,
)

X_train, y_train, X_val, y_val = preprocessor.common_preprocessing()
\end{lstlisting}

\noindent For regression, the main change is the problem type and target column.

\begin{lstlisting}[style=code,caption={Calling tabular regression preprocessing}]

preprocessor = ClassicalTrainingPreprocessing(
    df=df,
    target_col="price",
    problem_type="regression",
    folder_path="outputs/regression_run",
)
\end{lstlisting}

\noindent For test data, the user should pass the same folder path. This makes the test
data use the saved training transformers instead of fitting new transformers.

\begin{lstlisting}[style=code,caption={Calling tabular testing preprocessing}]
import pandas as pd
from accelera.src.auto_preprocessing.core.classical_testing_preprocessing import (
    ClassicalTestingPreprocessing,
)

test_df = pd.read_csv("test.csv")

test_preprocessor = ClassicalTestingPreprocessing(
    df=test_df,
    folder_path="outputs/tabular_run",
)

X_test, y_test = test_preprocessor.common_preprocessing()
\end{lstlisting}

\section{Using Text Auto Preprocessing}

For text data, the user provides the text column and target column. The module
cleans the text, applies TF-IDF, encodes the labels, and saves the needed
objects.

\begin{lstlisting}[style=code,caption={Calling text training preprocessing}]
import pandas as pd
from accelera.src.auto_preprocessing.core.text_training_preprocessing import (
    TextTrainingPreprocessing,
)

df = pd.read_csv("reviews.csv")

text_preprocessor = TextTrainingPreprocessing(
    df=df,
    target_col="label",
    text_col="review",
    folder_path="outputs/text_run",
    tfidf_max_features=500,
    is_report=True,
)

X_train, y_train, X_val, y_val = text_preprocessor.common_preprocessing()
\end{lstlisting}

\noindent For testing text data, the user again passes the same folder path. If the test
data does not contain the target column, the class returns the transformed text
features and \modulepath{None} for labels.

\begin{lstlisting}[style=code,caption={Calling text testing preprocessing}]
from accelera.src.auto_preprocessing.core.text_testing_preprocesing import (
    TextTestingPreprocessing,
)

test_text = pd.read_csv("reviews_test.csv")

text_test_preprocessor = TextTestingPreprocessing(
    df=test_text,
    folder_path="outputs/text_run",
)

X_test, y_test = text_test_preprocessor.common_preprocessing()
\end{lstlisting}

\section{Using Image Auto Preprocessing}

For image preprocessing, the dataset should be arranged as class folders. For
example:

\begin{verbatim}
Training/
  Cats/
  Dogs/
\end{verbatim}

\noindent The user passes the training folder and output folder. If there is no separate
validation folder, the module can split the training folder.

\begin{lstlisting}[style=code,caption={Calling image training preprocessing}]
from accelera.src.auto_preprocessing.core.classification_image_training_preprocessing import (
    ClassificationImageTrainingPreprocessing,
)

image_preprocessor = ClassificationImageTrainingPreprocessing(
    training_folder_images="data/PetImages/Training",
    folder_path="outputs/image_run",
    split_training=True,
    val_size=0.2,
    images_size=(224, 224),
    batch_size=16,
    augment=True,
)

train_loader, val_loader = image_preprocessor.common_preprocessing()
\end{lstlisting}

\noindent For image testing, the user provides a list of image paths. Class names are
optional, but if they are provided, they must match the saved training classes.

\begin{lstlisting}[style=code,caption={Calling image testing preprocessing}]
from accelera.src.auto_preprocessing.core.classification_image_testing_preprocessing import (
    ClassificationImageTestingPreprocessing,
)

image_paths = ["test/cat1.jpg", "test/dog1.jpg"]
class_names = ["Cats", "Dogs"]

image_test_preprocessor = ClassificationImageTestingPreprocessing(
    image_paths=image_paths,
    image_class_names=class_names,
    folder_path="outputs/image_run",
    batch_size=4,
)

test_loader, invalid_images = image_test_preprocessor.common_preprocessing()
\end{lstlisting}

\section{Reading Auto Preprocessing Reports}

After running preprocessing with \modulepath{is\_report=True}, the user opens
the generated \modulepath{report.html} file from the output folder. The report
shows the dataset overview, missing values, duplicate rows, dropped columns,
graphs, preprocessing decisions, selected features, and processed data samples.

\section{Using Accelera AutoML}
Accelera AutoML follows a Scikit-learn-style workflow. The user imports either
\texttt{AutoMLClassifier} or \texttt{AutoMLRegressor}, creates an estimator
with the required search settings, fits it on the training data, and then uses
the fitted model for prediction, scoring, or leaderboard inspection.

\begin{lstlisting}[style=code,caption={Basic AutoML classification workflow}]
from accelera.src.accelera_automl import AutoMLClassifier

model = AutoMLClassifier(
    time_budget=1800,
    cv=5,
    scoring="accuracy",
    use_ensemble=True,
    random_state=42,
)

model.fit(X_train, y_train)

predictions = model.predict(X_test)
score = model.score(X_test, y_test)
leaderboard = model.return_leaderboard()
\end{lstlisting}

\noindent In this example, the search is limited to 1,800 seconds, five-fold
cross-validation is used, and accuracy is selected as the optimization metric.
After fitting, the model can generate predictions, evaluate performance on test
data, and return the stored leaderboard for inspection.

\begin{lstlisting}[style=code,caption={Basic AutoML regression workflow}]
from accelera.src.accelera_automl import AutoMLRegressor

model = AutoMLRegressor(
    time_budget=1800,
    cv=5,
    scoring="r2",
    n_trials=50,
    use_meta_learning=True,
    random_state=42,
)

model.fit(X_train, y_train)

predictions = model.predict(X_test)
r2_score = model.score(X_test, y_test)
leaderboard = model.return_leaderboard(top_n=5)
\end{lstlisting}

\noindent The same public workflow is used for regression. The main difference
is that the selected score is a regression metric, such as $R^2$, and the
predictions are numeric values instead of class labels.

\section{Using Pipeline Reports}

To generate a pipeline report, the pipeline graph should be serialized first.
Then the user passes the XML graph path, output folder, and metric results to
\modulepath{GraphReport}.

\begin{lstlisting}[style=code,caption={Calling the pipeline graph report}]
from accelera.src.accelera_pipe.wrappers.graph_report import GraphReport

report = GraphReport(
    folderpath="outputs/pipeline_report",
    xmlpath="outputs/pipeline_graph.xml",
    results=metric_results,
)

report.execute()
\end{lstlisting}

\noindent The output is a \modulepath{report.html} file. It contains the graph image,
branch tables, selected branch, and metric summary.

\section{Using the Deployment Module}
\subsection{Preparing deployment artifacts}
Before deploying a model, the workspace must contain a deployment
\modulepath{config.json} file and the model artifacts referenced by that file.
The configuration identifies the model files, optional preprocessing artifacts,
schema-validation rules, and request-tracking options. The preparation command
checks these paths, copies the runtime service files, writes dependency
requirements, and generates the Dockerfile used by local or remote targets.

\noindent A complete example of the Deployment module configuration is shown
below. The \modulepath{models} object points to the serialized preprocessing,
feature-selection, and final-estimator artifacts. The \modulepath{schema}
object defines the expected prediction fields and validation constraints, while
the \modulepath{tracking} object enables JSONL request logging.

\begin{lstlisting}[style=code,caption={Complete example of deployment config.json}]
{
  "models": {
    "preprocessing_pipeline": "models/preprocessing_pipeline.pkl",
    "feature_selector": "models/feature_selector.pkl",
    "final_model": "models/final_model.pkl"
  },
  "schema": {
    "features": [
      {
        "name": "alcohol",
        "type": "number",
        "required": false,
        "min": 1,
        "max": 15
      },
      {
        "name": "malic_acid",
        "type": "number",
        "required": false,
        "min": 2,
        "max": 5
      },
      {
        "name": "ash",
        "type": "number",
        "required": false,
        "min": 1,
        "max": 3
      },
      {
        "name": "alcalinity_of_ash",
        "type": "number",
        "required": false,
        "min": 10,
        "max": 30
      },
      {
        "name": "magnesium",
        "type": "number",
        "required": false,
        "min": 20,
        "max": 160
      },
      {
        "name": "total_phenols",
        "type": "number",
        "required": false,
        "min": 1,
        "max": 4
      },
      {
        "name": "flavanoids",
        "type": "number",
        "required": false,
        "min": 0,
        "max": 6
      },
      {
        "name": "color_intensity",
        "type": "number",
        "required": false,
        "min": 1,
        "max": 13
      },
      {
        "name": "hue",
        "type": "number",
        "required": false,
        "min": 1,
        "max": 2
      },
      {
        "name": "proline",
        "type": "number",
        "required": false,
        "min": 278,
        "max": 1680
      },
      {
        "name": "color_group",
        "type": "string",
        "required": false,
        "allowed_values": ["dark", "light"]
      },
      {
        "name": "proline_class",
        "type": "string",
        "required": false,
        "allowed_values": ["high", "low", "medium"]
      }
    ]
  },
  "tracking": {
    "enabled": true,
    "path": "prediction_logs/predictions.jsonl"
  }
}
\end{lstlisting}

\begin{lstlisting}[style=code,caption={Preparing a deployment workspace}]
cd path/to/deployment/workspace
python accelera_deployment/deployment.py prepare
\end{lstlisting}

\noindent The preparation stage should be run before local Docker testing,
Heroku deployment, or EC2 deployment. If a configured model file is missing,
the command stops before creating an invalid deployment package.

\subsection{Running the service locally}
The local workflow builds the Docker image and starts a container that exposes
the FastAPI prediction service. This path is recommended before remote
deployment because it verifies the same service package in a controlled
environment.

\begin{lstlisting}[style=code,caption={Building and running the local deployment service}]
python accelera_deployment/deployment.py local --port 8000
\end{lstlisting}

\noindent After the container starts, the main endpoints are
\modulepath{/health}, \modulepath{/predict}, \modulepath{/predict/csv},
\modulepath{/tracking/summary}, and \modulepath{/gui}. The GUI route is useful
for manually testing the schema and prediction payload without writing a
separate client.

\begin{lstlisting}[style=code,caption={Calling the deployed prediction endpoint}]
curl -X POST http://localhost:8000/predict \
  -H "Content-Type: application/json" \
  -d '{"input": [{"feature_1": 5.1, "feature_2": "A"}]}'
\end{lstlisting}

\subsection{Using deployment VCS}
The deployment VCS snapshots the active \modulepath{config.json} file and the
model artifacts referenced by it. This is useful when several model versions
are tested and the user needs to return to a previous deployable state.

\begin{lstlisting}[style=code,caption={Creating and restoring deployment snapshots}]
python accelera_deployment/vcs.py init
python accelera_deployment/vcs.py commit -m "baseline model"
python accelera_deployment/vcs.py log
python accelera_deployment/vcs.py deploy <commit-hash>
\end{lstlisting}

\noindent A deployment commit is different from a Git commit. It is focused on
runtime artifacts: the configuration and model files needed by the serving
container. Restoring a commit copies those artifacts back into the active
deployment workspace.

\subsection{Deploying to EC2}
For EC2 deployment, the user provides the host, SSH user, optional key file,
remote directory, service port, image name, and container name. The module
synchronizes the prepared service to the server, builds the Docker image
remotely, starts the container, and checks the health endpoint.

\begin{lstlisting}[style=code,caption={Deploying the service to an EC2 instance}]
python accelera_deployment/deployment.py ec2-deploy \
  --host ec2-xx-xx-xx-xx.compute.amazonaws.com \
  --user ubuntu \
  --key-path ~/.ssh/accelera.pem \
  --remote-dir ~/accelera-deployment \
  --port 8000
\end{lstlisting}

\noindent If the internal health check passes but the public URL is unreachable,
the most likely cause is a closed inbound port or a missing security-group
rule. The module reports this condition so that the user can inspect the EC2
network settings.

\section{Using the Benchmark Platform}

The benchmark platform is used from the web interface. The benchmark creator
enters the title, problem type, target column, dataset link, test-set link,
target-label link, and evaluation metric. A user submits a prediction file link
and repository link. The backend calls the Python scoring script, saves the
score, and displays the result in the leaderboard.

\noindent The prediction file must contain the target column expected by the benchmark.
Also, the number of rows in the prediction file must match the number of rows
in the target label file.

\mbox{}
\fi
\chapter{Feasibility Study}

\section{Purpose and Evaluation Criteria}
This chapter checks whether Accelera is practical to build, run, and improve.
The study covers the full project: Auto Preprocessing, AutoML, Accelera Pipe,
the Code Parallelizer, the Benchmark Platform, and Deployment. Since this is a
student project and some parts are still prototypes, the chapter separates what
already works from what still needs more production-level work.

The evaluation is based on seven simple points: technical feasibility,
operational feasibility, economic feasibility, schedule feasibility, legal and
ethical feasibility, security feasibility, and scalability. In simple words, the
study asks whether the tools exist, whether users can run the system, whether
the cost is reasonable, whether the work can be delivered in stages, whether the
project respects data and license rules, whether it is safe to use, and whether
it can grow later.

In this chapter, a module is called \textbf{feasible} when its main workflow can
be implemented and tested with available tools. It is called \textbf{feasible
with constraints} when it can work, but still depends on extra requirements such
as compilers, cloud accounts, hardware limits, or stronger production controls.
A \textbf{prototype-feasible} result means that the idea and core workflow are
shown, but the module still needs more security, testing, or scaling before
public production use.

\section{Technical Feasibility}
\subsection{Architecture and Technology Availability}
Accelera is technically feasible because it is built from common and available
technologies. Python is used for the public APIs and machine-learning libraries
such as NumPy, Pandas, and Scikit-learn. C++20 and PyBind11 are used for the
native graph backend and Python bindings. CMake handles native builds, while
LLVM/Clang and OpenMP support loop analysis and parallel code generation. The
web platform uses React, Express, and MongoDB. The Deployment module uses
FastAPI and Docker, with optional commands for Heroku and EC2. These tools are
well known and do not require special hardware.

{\acceleraTableStyle
\begin{longtable}{p{0.17\textwidth} p{0.39\textwidth} p{0.36\textwidth}}
\caption{Module-level technical feasibility assessment.}
\label{tab:module-technical-feasibility} \\
\hline
\textbf{Module} & \textbf{Implementation evidence and resources} &
\textbf{Constraints and assessment} \\
\hline
\endfirsthead

\hline
\textbf{Module} & \textbf{Implementation evidence and resources} &
\textbf{Constraints and assessment} \\
\hline
\endhead

\hline
\multicolumn{3}{r}{\small Continued on the next page} \\
\hline
\endfoot

\hline
\endlastfoot

Auto Preprocessing & Uses Pandas, NumPy, Scikit-learn, text tools, image tools,
saved transformers, and HTML reports. Training and testing can reuse saved
preprocessing artifacts. & \textbf{Feasible.} It needs valid input columns and
enough storage for reports and artifacts. Very unusual data types may need more
rules. \\
\hline

AutoML & Supports classification and regression search, trial and time budgets,
meta-learning support, and ensembles using existing estimator libraries. &
\textbf{Feasible with constraints.} Search time grows with dataset size,
number of trials, and number of model families. Fixed seeds and versions are
needed for repeatable results. \\
\hline

Accelera Pipe & Uses a Python graph API, a C++ execution backend, PyBind11
bindings, branch scheduling, shared nodes, memory release, metrics, path
selection, and pickle saving/loading. & \textbf{Feasible with constraints.}
Native compilation is required. Parallel branches can improve speed, but branch
width and dataset size must be controlled to avoid high memory use. \\
\hline

Code Parallelizer & Uses Python-to-C++ conversion, Clang AST loop extraction,
safety rules, classifier support, OpenMP insertion, PyBind11 compilation, and a
compiled-module cache. & \textbf{Feasible with constraints.} It supports only a
documented subset of Python and analyzable loops. LLVM, Clang, a C++ compiler,
and OpenMP must be available. Unsupported functions fall back to Python. \\
\hline

Benchmark Platform & Uses a React frontend, an Express backend, MongoDB,
authentication helpers, submission records, metrics, and scoring scripts. &
\textbf{Prototype-feasible.} The main web technologies are mature, but public use
needs stronger upload isolation, authorization tests, rate limiting, monitoring,
backups, and more backend tests. \\
\hline

Deployment & Uses FastAPI serving, input validation, telemetry, model artifact
loading, Docker packaging, and commands for local Docker, Heroku, and EC2. &
\textbf{Feasible with constraints.} Local Docker deployment is practical. Remote
deployment needs accounts, credentials, network access, supported CLIs, health
checks, and proper secret management. \\
\hline

\end{longtable}
}


\subsection{Integration and Portability}
Integration is feasible because the modules exchange normal artifacts such as
arrays, data frames, fitted models, preprocessing objects, graph objects, metric
results, configuration files, and saved models. Auto Preprocessing can prepare
data for AutoML or Accelera Pipe. Accelera Pipe can call the Parallelizer for
supported custom preprocessing functions. Selected models or executed graphs can
then be used by the Deployment module. The Benchmark Platform is a separate path
for comparing prediction files and showing results.

The package supports Python 3.9 or newer and uses CMake with C++20. Linux and
Windows are realistic targets because the build logic includes platform-specific
LLVM discovery and installation paths. However, this does not mean everything is
zero-configuration. Compiler versions, OpenMP runtimes, Node.js, MongoDB,
Docker, and cloud CLIs still need clear documentation and testing. Users can
still run pure-Python parts even if the native graph or AST tools are not
installed.

\section{Operational Feasibility}
Accelera is operationally feasible for students, researchers, and data-science
developers who already know basic Python and machine-learning workflows. The
APIs use familiar words such as \texttt{fit}, \texttt{transform}, and
\texttt{predict}. Reports, graph visualizations, metrics, examples, and saved
artifacts make the output easier to understand. The project is modular, so users
can run one module without running the full web platform.

{\acceleraTableStyle
\begin{longtable}{p{0.22\textwidth} p{0.32\textwidth} p{0.38\textwidth}}
\caption{Operational workflows and requirements.}
\label{tab:operational-feasibility} \\
\toprule
\textbf{Workflow} & \textbf{Main user and output} &
\textbf{What is needed} \\
\midrule
\endfirsthead
\toprule
\textbf{Workflow} & \textbf{Main user and output} &
\textbf{What is needed} \\
\midrule
\endhead
\bottomrule
\endlastfoot
Prepare a dataset & Data scientist; processed data, saved artifacts, and a
report. & Correct target and feature settings, writable output folders, and
consistent use of training transformations during testing. \\
\hline
Search for a model & Data scientist; ranked trials, selected model, and
optional ensemble. & Clear time/trial budget, suitable metric, fixed seed, and
enough memory for candidate evaluation. \\
\hline
Build a graph pipeline & Python developer; validation results and an executed
graph. & Native graph binding, compatible nodes, validation data for selecting a
path, and memory-aware branch design. \\
\hline
Parallelize code & Developer who needs speed; annotated C++ or compiled Python
callable. & Supported source syntax, compiler toolchain, correctness comparison,
and timing comparison with the original code. \\
\hline
Run a benchmark & Platform admin and participants; scored submissions and a
leaderboard. & Running frontend/backend, database connection, available dataset,
metric definition, user controls, and submission validation. \\
\hline
Deploy a model & ML or DevOps user; local or remote prediction service. & Valid
model/config files, Docker or provider tools, credentials, network access,
monitoring, and rollback plan. \\
\end{longtable}
}

The main operational challenge is installation. A full setup may include Python,
C++ tools, Node.js, MongoDB, Docker, and optional cloud tools. This is acceptable
for a modular research project, but the user experience can be improved with
version checks, clearer error messages, prebuilt wheels, containerized services,
and smoke tests. The Parallelizer fallback also helps because unsupported code
continues to run in Python instead of breaking the full workflow.


\section{Performance, Scalability, and Maintenance Feasibility}
Accelera can scale on one machine and can also grow at service boundaries, but
each module has a different limit. Auto Preprocessing is limited mainly by data
size and transformation cost. AutoML is limited by the number and cost of trials.
Accelera Pipe is limited by branch width, model training time, and memory. The
Parallelizer is limited by the supported source patterns and whether the target
loop actually benefits from parallelism. The Benchmark Platform is limited by
database traffic, storage, and submissions. Deployment is limited by request
rate, model latency, container resources, and telemetry growth.

Some controls already support moderate workloads. AutoML has time and trial
budgets. Accelera Pipe releases intermediate arrays after their last consumer and
keeps node caching optional. The Parallelizer caches generated and compiled code.
Docker helps make deployment repeatable, and MongoDB stores benchmark records.
Further scaling needs load tests, queues, artifact cleanup rules, indexes,
isolation, and monitoring. Parallel execution should be enabled based on timing
measurements, not just assumed to help every workload.

Maintenance is feasible because the modules are separated, but the dependency
surface is large. The project should clearly define supported Python, Node.js,
compiler, LLVM, database, and cloud-tool versions. CI should build native modules
on Linux and Windows, run Python tests, lint frontend/backend code, build Docker
images, and run a small end-to-end test. Generated caches and models should store
version metadata so old artifacts can be rejected or rebuilt safely.

\section{Overall Feasibility Decision}
\begin{table}[H]
\centering
\caption{Overall project feasibility decision.}
\label{tab:overall-feasibility-decision}
\acceleraTableStyle
\begin{tabularx}{\textwidth}{p{0.22\textwidth} p{0.22\textwidth} X}
\toprule
\textbf{Dimension} & \textbf{Decision} & \textbf{Condition} \\
\midrule
Technical & Feasible with constraints & Keep native toolchain support and
document module limits. \\
\hline
Operational & Feasible & Keep modular workflows, examples, diagnostics, and
clear artifacts. \\
\hline
Security & Prototype-feasible & Add isolation, authentication, secret management,
artifact trust, and public-service hardening. \\
\hline
Scalability & Feasible for moderate workloads & Measure memory and latency,
limit concurrency, and scale services based on demand. \\
\bottomrule
\end{tabularx}
\end{table}

The final decision is that Accelera is feasible as an academic, research, and
controlled prototype platform. The main module workflows use available
technologies and can be tested in the repository. The project is also practical
for local and small-team use when dependencies and resource limits are respected.
Public production use is not fully complete yet. The Benchmark Platform and
remote Deployment paths still need stronger security, load testing, monitoring,
backups, and clear ownership. These missing parts do not make the design wrong;
they show the next engineering step needed to move from a working prototype to a
reliable production service.
